**Level Three — Reversibility Level**: Many physical changes can be simply reversed (freezing/melting, evaporation/condensation); chemical changes require entirely different reaction conditions to reverse.

---

## 6.2 Rate of Reaction

### 6.2.1 The Deep Principles of Collision Theory

Collision theory is the **microscopic kinetic foundation** for explaining reaction rates. Its core premises:

**Premise One: Reactions require collisions.** Molecules/ions/atoms must encounter each other in space to interact. However, not all collisions are effective.

**Premise Two: Effective collisions must satisfy two conditions —**

$$P_{\text{success}} = P_{\text{orientation}} \times P_{\text{energy}}$$

Where:
- $P_{\text{orientation}}$ = the probability that colliding particles have the **correct spatial orientation**
- $P_{\text{energy}}$ = the probability that the total kinetic energy of colliding particles $\geq$ the **activation energy $E_a$**

**Premise Three: The essence of activation energy $E_a$.** Activation energy is the **minimum energy barrier** that reactants must overcome to transition from a stable state to the transition state. Understood from an energy diagram:

$$\text{Reactants} \xrightarrow{E_a} [\text{Transition State}]^\ddagger \rightarrow \text{Products}$$

The transition state (Activated Complex) is a **high-energy, extremely unstable** configuration where old bonds are partially broken and new bonds are partially formed. The transition state **cannot be isolated** — it collapses toward products the moment it forms.

**Energy Distribution and the Boltzmann Distribution**:

At a given temperature, the kinetic energy of particles in a system is not uniformly distributed but follows the **Maxwell-Boltzmann distribution curve**. This curve:
- Horizontal axis: kinetic energy
- Vertical axis: proportion of particles with that kinetic energy
- **Total area under the curve** = total number of particles (constant)
- **Rightward shift** of the curve indicates increased average kinetic energy
- Only particles in the region where $E \geq E_a$ (the right tail of the curve) can undergo effective collisions

When temperature increases, the Boltzmann distribution curve's **peak shifts right and lowers**, and more importantly, the **area of the right tail (proportion of particles with energy $\geq E_a$) grows exponentially**. This is the fundamental reason why temperature has a far greater impact on reaction rate than concentration.

### 6.2.2 In-Depth Mechanistic Analysis of Factors Affecting Rate

#### (1) Concentration — The Frequency Dimension

Increasing concentration = increasing the **number density** of particles per unit volume.

Let the number density of reactant particles be $N/V$. The collision frequency $Z$ approximately satisfies:

$$Z \propto \left(\frac{N}{V}\right)^2$$

(For bimolecular reactions, collision frequency is proportional to the product of both reactant concentrations)

Concentration doubled $\rightarrow$ collision frequency approximately **quadruples** (for bimolecular reactions) $\rightarrow$ reaction rate increases.

**Key point**: Concentration only affects **collision frequency**, not the **proportion of effective collisions** (since $E_a$ remains unchanged). Therefore, concentration affects rate through a **linear or quadratic** relationship, not an exponential one.

#### (2) Pressure (Gases) — The Spatial Compression Dimension

The essence of pressure is the impact of gas particles on container walls. Increasing pressure (decreasing volume) $\rightarrow$ particle spacing decreases $\rightarrow$ number of particles per unit volume increases $\rightarrow$ equivalent to increasing concentration.

$$P \propto \frac{n}{V}$$

For gases, pressure is directly proportional to particle number density.

#### (3) Surface Area (Solids) — The Exposure Dimension

When solids react, only **surface particles** can come into contact with other reactants. Increasing surface area means:
- More atoms/ions are exposed at the reaction interface
- Increased collision probability
- However, particles inside the solid are bound by the lattice and cannot participate in the reaction until the surface layer has reacted

Crushing a solid $\rightarrow$ total surface area increases **quadratically** $\rightarrow$ more active sites exposed $\rightarrow$ rate increases.

#### (4) Temperature — The Energy Dimension (Most Critical)

Temperature's effect on rate is a **deep energy effect**, encompassing two levels of change:

**Level One**: Average particle kinetic energy increases $\rightarrow$ particles move faster $\rightarrow$ collision frequency increases (linear effect)

**Level Two** (**dominant factor**): More particles acquire energy $\geq E_a$ $\rightarrow$ proportion of effective collisions increases **exponentially**

The Arrhenius quantitative relationship:

$$k = A e^{-\frac{E_a}{RT}}$$

Where:
- $k$ = rate constant
- $A$ = pre-exponential factor (frequency factor)
- $E_a$ = activation energy
- $R$ = gas constant
- $T$ = absolute temperature

This equation reveals the **exponential relationship** between the rate constant and temperature: the larger $E_a$ is, the more sensitive the rate is to temperature. As a rough rule of thumb, for every $10^\circ\text{C}$ rise in temperature, the reaction rate approximately **doubles** (van't Hoff's empirical rule) — the essence of which lies in the exponential expansion of the Boltzmann distribution tail area with increasing temperature.

#### (5) Catalysts — The Pathway-Changing Dimension

Catalysts accelerate reactions by **changing the reaction mechanism** and **providing a new pathway with a lower activation energy**. Catalysts **do not change the total enthalpy change $\Delta H$** of the reaction; they **do not change the intrinsic energy difference** between reactants and products. They only lower the energy barrier that must be crossed from reactants to products.

$$\text{Without catalyst: } E_a \text{ (high)} \quad \text{vs.} \quad \text{With catalyst: } E_a' \text{ (lower)}$$

From the Arrhenius equation:

$$\frac{k_{\text{catalysed}}}{k_{\text{uncatalysed}}} = \frac{A e^{-E_a'/RT}}{A e^{-E_a/RT}} = e^{(E_a - E_a')/RT}$$

Even if $E_a - E_a'$ is only a few tens of kJ/mol, the exponential effect causes the rate constant to increase by several orders of magnitude.

**Key characteristics of catalysts**:
- **Chemical composition unchanged** before and after the reaction
- **Do not change the equilibrium position** (they accelerate forward and reverse reactions equally)
- **Exhibit selectivity** — different catalysts catalyze different reactions
- **Can be poisoned** — certain impurities adsorb onto the catalyst surface, rendering it inactive

Regarding **enzymes** as biological catalysts: enzymes exhibit high specificity (lock-and-key model or induced-fit model for substrate binding to the active site). The optimum temperature and optimum pH of an enzyme are determined by the thermal stability of the enzyme protein's three-dimensional structure. Exceeding the optimal conditions leads to enzyme **denaturation** — irreversible destruction of the enzyme's three-dimensional structure and loss of activity.

### 6.2.3 Deep Principles of Measuring Reaction Rates

The fundamental idea of rate measurement is to track **the change in concentration of a substance over time**. The key is to select a physical quantity that can be monitored **in real-time or intermittently**.

**Method One: Mass Change Method**
- Principle: If a gaseous product escapes from the reaction, the total mass of the system continuously decreases
- Data form: mass $m$ vs. time $t$ curve
- Physical meaning of the curve's slope: $\frac{dm}{dt} \propto \text{reaction rate}$
- The initial slope is greatest (highest reactant concentration); the slope gradually decreases to zero as the reaction ends

**Method Two: Gas Volume Method**
- Principle: Use a **gas syringe** or **downward displacement of water** to collect the gas produced
- Data form: gas volume $V$ vs. time $t$ curve
- More accurate than the mass method (not affected by air buoyancy)

**Method Three: Turbidity/Color Method**
- Suitable for reactions accompanied by color changes or precipitate formation
- Uses a spectrophotometer or visual color comparison

**Principles for Interpreting Rate Curves**:
- **Curve slope** = instantaneous rate
- Average rate = $\frac{\Delta \text{(concentration)}}{\Delta t}$
- Curve becoming **horizontal** indicates the reaction has finished
- Comparing curves under different conditions: steeper slope = faster rate; shorter time to reach horizontal = faster completion of the reaction

---

## 6.3 Reversible Reactions and Chemical Equilibrium

### 6.3.1 Deep Understanding of Reversibility

Not all reactions proceed to completion. **Reversible reactions** are fundamentally characterized by the fact that the forward and reverse directions **can occur simultaneously under the same conditions**; the reaction does not go to "completion" but instead tends toward a **dynamic equilibrium** state.

The symbol $\rightleftharpoons$ represents reversibility, which is fundamentally different from the usual reaction arrow $\rightarrow$:
- $\rightarrow$: The reaction proceeds **almost completely** to the right; reactants are nearly exhausted
- $\rightleftharpoons$: The reaction proceeds **in both directions**; reactants and products coexist

The microscopic essence of reversibility: when the energy state of the product molecules is sufficiently close to that of the reactants, collisions between product molecules can also trigger the reverse reaction — i.e., the activation energy for the reverse reaction is not infinitely large.

### 6.3.2 The Rigorous Definition of Dynamic Equilibrium

In a **closed system**, matter cannot enter or escape, but energy can be exchanged.

When the system reaches **equilibrium**, both of the following are simultaneously satisfied:

$$\text{Rate}_{\text{forward}} = \text{Rate}_{\text{reverse}}$$

and

$$[\text{Reactants}] = \text{constant}, \quad [\text{Products}] = \text{constant}$$

**Dynamic equilibrium** ≠ **static equilibrium**. At the microscopic level:
- The forward and reverse reactions **are still ongoing**
- Only the **net effect is zero**
- In any unit of time, the number of reactant molecules consumed by the forward reaction equals the number of reactant molecules regenerated by the reverse reaction

### 6.3.3 The Deep Logic of Le Chatelier's Principle

Le Chatelier's Principle can be stated as:

$$ \text{System perturbed} \rightarrow \text{Shifts to counteract perturbation} \rightarrow \text{New equilibrium achieved} $$

This principle is essentially a manifestation of the **Second Law of Thermodynamics** in chemical equilibrium — the system always tends to **minimize the impact of external intervention**.

#### (1) Temperature Change — Perturbation at the Energy Level

The essence of temperature is the average kinetic energy of particles. Raising the temperature is equivalent to **injecting energy** into the system.

For a reversible reaction:
- **Exothermic direction** ($\Delta H < 0$): releases energy
- **Endothermic direction** ($\Delta H > 0$): absorbs energy

When the system is heated (energy is injected), the system **consumes excess energy by shifting toward the direction that absorbs energy**, i.e., toward the **endothermic direction**. Conversely, when cooled, the system shifts toward the **exothermic direction**, releasing more energy to compensate for the loss.

$$\text{Heating} \rightarrow \text{Equilibrium shifts toward endothermic direction} (\Delta H > 0)$$
$$\text{Cooling} \rightarrow \text{Equilibrium shifts toward exothermic direction} (\Delta H < 0)$$

#### (2) Pressure Change — The Volume/Molecule Number Dimension

For gas reactions, changing pressure is fundamentally about changing the **volume constraint** on the system.

Consider the reaction: $$aA(g) + bB(g) \rightleftharpoons cC(g) + dD(g)$$

Define the change in the number of gas molecules: $$\Delta n_g = (c+d) - (a+b)$$

- If $\Delta n_g < 0$: the forward reaction **decreases the number of gas molecules**
- If $\Delta n_g > 0$: the forward reaction **increases the number of gas molecules**
- If $\Delta n_g = 0$: the number of gas molecules does not change

**When pressure is increased**: the system shifts toward the direction with **fewer gas molecules** to reduce the pressure — because according to the ideal gas law $PV = nRT$, under conditions of volume compression, reducing the number of molecules lowers the pressure.

**When pressure is decreased**: the system shifts toward the direction with **more gas molecules** — increasing volume gives more molecules space to move.

**Important note**: if the number of gas molecules is equal before and after the reaction ($\Delta n_g = 0$), a change in pressure **does not affect** the equilibrium position.

#### (3) Concentration Change — Perturbation of Material Proportions

Increasing the concentration of a substance = disrupting the equilibrium concentration of that substance, causing the rate of the reaction that **consumes that substance** to increase, until the concentration ratio at the new equilibrium once again satisfies the equilibrium constant.

$$K_c = \frac{[\text{C}]^c[\text{D}]^d}{[\text{A}]^a[\text{B}]^b}$$

The value of $K_c$ is a **constant at a given temperature**. When the concentration of a single substance is changed, the system must adjust the concentrations of all substances to restore the fraction to $K_c$.

#### (4) The Boundary of a Catalyst's Role

Catalysts **simultaneously and equally** lower the activation energies of both forward and reverse reactions, thus proportionally accelerating both forward and reverse rates. They help the system **reach equilibrium faster**, but **do not change the equilibrium position** (i.e., they do not change the equilibrium constant $K_c$ or the equilibrium concentration ratios of substances).

### 6.3.4 The Deep Principles of the Haber Process and Contact Process

These two industrial processes are classic examples of the **trade-off between rate and equilibrium**.

#### The Haber Process

$$\text{N}_2(g) + 3\text{H}_2(g) \rightleftharpoons 2\text{NH}_3(g) \quad \Delta H = -92 \text{ kJ/mol}$$

**Equilibrium Analysis (Thermodynamics)**:
- Exothermic reaction ($\Delta H < 0$): low temperature favors $\text{NH}_3$ yield (shifts toward exothermic direction)
- $\Delta n_g = 2 - (1+3) = -2$: high pressure favors $\text{NH}_3$ yield (shifts toward direction with fewer gas molecules)

**Rate Analysis (Kinetics)**:
- Low temperature $\rightarrow$ reaction rate is extremely slow; reaching equilibrium takes too long (uneconomical)
- High temperature $\rightarrow$ rate increases, but equilibrium shifts toward the reverse reaction (yield decreases)
- High pressure $\rightarrow$ increases rate and favors yield, but equipment costs and safety risks increase

**The Logic of Condition Selection**:
- **450°C**: a compromise temperature — ensures a sufficiently fast reaction rate while maintaining an acceptable yield (~15% conversion)
- **200 atm**: high pressure pushes equilibrium toward ammonia and also increases rate (but not excessively high to avoid extreme safety risks)
- **Iron catalyst**: lowers $E_a$, accelerates reaching equilibrium, making the reaction feasible at lower temperatures
- **Recycling process**: unreacted $\text{N}_2$ and $\text{H}_2$ are recovered and reused; $\text{NH}_3$ is continuously removed to keep the equilibrium shifting to the right (the Le Chatelier strategy of "removing products")

#### The Contact Process

$$2\text{SO}_2(g) + \text{O}_2(g) \rightleftharpoons 2\text{SO}_3(g) \quad \Delta H = -196 \text{ kJ/mol}$$

**Equilibrium Analysis**:
- Strongly exothermic ($\Delta H = -196 \text{ kJ/mol}$): low temperature favors $\text{SO}_3$ yield
- $\Delta n_g = 2 - (2+1) = -1$: high pressure favors $\text{SO}_3$ yield

**Condition Selection**:
- **450°C**: similar compromise strategy to the Haber process
- **2 atm (low pressure)**: unlike the Haber process, low pressure is used here. Reasons:
  1. Even at atmospheric pressure, the conversion of $\text{SO}_2$ to $\text{SO}_3$ already exceeds 95% (the equilibrium constant at 450°C is already large)
  2. High pressure increases equipment costs with negligible benefit
  3. In the subsequent step, $\text{SO}_3$ is dissolved in $\text{H}_2\text{SO}_4$ to form oleum; continuously removing the product further shifts equilibrium to the right
- **$\text{V}_2\text{O}_5$ catalyst**: lowers activation energy, allowing the reaction to proceed at an acceptable temperature

**Safety considerations**: $\text{SO}_2$ is a toxic gas; $\text{SO}_3$ is highly corrosive. Equipment sealing and safety protection are critical. $\text{SO}_3$ cannot be dissolved directly in water (it forms an acid mist that corrodes equipment); instead, it is dissolved in concentrated sulfuric acid to form oleum.

---

## 6.4 Redox Reactions — The Universal Language of Electron Transfer

### 6.4.1 The Triple Definition System of Redox

Redox can be understood along the **historical development of concepts** in three levels, each progressively penetrating deeper into the essence:

| Level | Oxidation | Reduction | Scope |
|-------|-----------|-----------|-------|
| **Level 1** (oxygen gain/loss) | Gain of oxygen | Loss of oxygen | Limited to reactions involving oxygen |
| **Level 2** (electron transfer) | Loss of electrons | Gain of electrons | All reactions involving electron transfer |
| **Level 3** (oxidation number change) | Increase in oxidation number | Decrease in oxidation number | **Universal** (covers all cases) |

These three levels are not contradictory; they are **progressively more encompassing** relationships. Level 3 (oxidation numbers) is the most powerful tool because:
1. It does not depend on whether "electron transfer is explicit" — in covalent bond reactions, electrons are not completely transferred, but oxidation numbers can still be assigned
2. It can identify the redox nature of various types of reactions

### 6.4.2 The Complete Rules for Determining Oxidation Numbers

Oxidation Number (Oxidation State) is a **formal charge** assignment tool, not a real charge.

**Rule System**:

**Rule 1**: The oxidation number of an element in its elemental form = $0$. Regardless of the number of atoms in the elemental molecule ($\text{H}_2, \text{O}_2, \text{Fe}, \text{S}_8$), the oxidation number of each atom is $0$.

**Rule 2**: The oxidation number of a monatomic ion = the charge on the ion. e.g., $\text{Na}^+ = +1$, $\text{Cl}^- = -1$, $\text{Mg}^{2+} = +2$.

**Rule 3**: The **sum** of the oxidation numbers of all atoms in a compound = $0$ (electroneutrality principle).

**Rule 4**: The **sum** of the oxidation numbers of all atoms in a polyatomic ion = the charge on the ion.

**Rule 5** (Priority Rules): Certain elements have relatively fixed oxidation numbers in compounds:
- **Fluorine** is always $-1$ (the most electronegative element)
- **Oxygen** is usually $-2$ ($+2$ when combined with fluorine, $-1$ in peroxides)
- **Hydrogen** is usually $+1$ ($-1$ in metal hydrides, e.g., $\text{NaH}$)

**Rule 6**: The highest positive oxidation number of a main group element usually equals its **group number**.

### 6.4.3 Core Mnemonic Framework for Electron Transfer in Redox

$$\text{Loss of Electrons} = \text{Oxidation} \quad (\text{LEO})$$
$$\text{Gain of Electrons} = \text{Reduction} \quad (\text{GER})$$

i.e., **LEO the Lion says GER**.

Expressing electron transfer in **half-reactions**:

**Oxidation half-reaction** (anode):

$$\text{Reduced form} \rightarrow \text{Oxidised form} + ne^-$$

**Reduction half-reaction** (cathode):

$$\text{Oxidised form} + ne^- \rightarrow \text{Reduced form}$$

### 6.4.4 The Essence of Oxidising and Reducing Agents

$$ \text{Oxidising Agent (Oxidant)} + ne^- \rightarrow \text{Reduced Form} $$
$$ \text{Reducing Agent (Reductant)} \rightarrow \text{Oxidised Form} + ne^- $$

An **oxidising agent** is an **electron acceptor** — it itself **gets reduced**. The oxidizing ability of a substance depends on its **tendency to gain electrons**.

A **reducing agent** is an **electron donor** — it itself **gets oxidised**. The reducing ability of a substance depends on its **tendency to lose electrons**.

Key conceptual chain:
- Oxidising agent + electrons $\rightarrow$ reduced product
- Reducing agent $\rightarrow$ oxidised product + electrons
- In a reaction: **the oxidising agent oxidises another substance** and **is itself reduced**; **the reducing agent reduces another substance** and **is itself oxidised**.

$$ \text{Oxidising agent (OA): } \text{OA} \xrightarrow{\text{receives } e^-} \text{reduced} $$
$$ \text{Reducing agent (RA): } \text{RA} \xrightarrow{\text{loses } e^-} \text{oxidised} $$

### 6.4.5 Color Changes of Common Oxidising and Reducing Agents

**Acidified potassium manganate(VII) ($\text{KMnO}_4$ / $\text{H}^+$)**:
- Purple $\text{MnO}_4^- \rightarrow$ colourless $\text{Mn}^{2+}$
- Mn oxidation number decreases from $+7 \rightarrow +2$ (decrease of 5), it is reduced
- Potassium manganate(VII) is a **strong oxidising agent**
- Color change: **purple $\rightarrow$ colourless** is a clear indication that a redox reaction has occurred

**Potassium iodide ($\text{KI}$)**:
- Colourless $\text{I}^- \rightarrow$ yellow-brown $\text{I}_2$
- I oxidation number increases from $-1 \rightarrow 0$ (increase), it is oxidised
- Potassium iodide is a **reducing agent**
- Color change: **colourless $\rightarrow$ yellow-brown** indicates the reaction has occurred

---

# TOPIC 7 — Acids, Bases and Salts (Deep Expansion)

---

## 7.1 Properties of Acids and Bases and Their Theoretical Framework

### 7.1.1 Characteristic Properties of Acids and Their Molecular-Level Explanation

The reason acids exhibit chemical activity is fundamentally that they can provide **$\text{H}^+$ ions (protons)**.

**Reaction of Acids with Metals**:

$\text{H}^+$ ions from the acid gain electrons from metal atoms and are reduced to $\text{H}_2$ gas. The metal acts as a **reducing agent** (loses electrons), and $\text{H}^+$ acts as an **oxidising agent** (gains electrons). This is essentially a **redox reaction**.

Whether a metal can react with an acid depends on its position in the **reactivity series** — only metals **above hydrogen** can undergo this reaction, as their tendency to lose electrons (reducing ability) is greater than that of hydrogen.

**Reaction of Acids with Bases** (Neutralisation):

$$\text{H}^+(aq) + \text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{O}(l)$$

The two ions combine to form water. This is an **exothermic reaction**, essentially a proton combining with a hydroxide ion to form a stable covalent bond.

**Reaction of Acids with Carbonates**:

Carbonates ($\text{CO}_3^{2-}$) are basic. When reacting with an acid, $\text{H}^+$ combines with $\text{CO}_3^{2-}$ to form unstable $\text{H}_2\text{CO}_3$ (carbonic acid), which immediately decomposes:

$$\text{CO}_3^{2-} + 2\text{H}^+ \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{CO}_3 \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{O} + \text{CO}_2\uparrow$$

The evolution of bubbles ($\text{CO}_2$) is the characteristic sign of this reaction.

### 7.1.2 The Principle of Indicator Color Change

Acid-base indicators are **weak organic acids or bases** whose **molecular form ($\text{HIn}$)** and **ionic form ($\text{In}^-$)** have different colors.

In acidic solutions, the $\text{H}^+$ concentration is high, the equilibrium shifts to the left, and the molecular form ($\text{HIn}$) dominates, displaying Color A:

$$\text{HIn} \rightleftharpoons \text{H}^+ + \text{In}^-$$
$$\text{Color A (acid form)} \quad \text{Color B (base form)}$$

In alkaline solutions, $\text{OH}^-$ consumes $\text{H}^+$, the equilibrium shifts to the right, and the ionic form ($\text{In}^-$) dominates, displaying Color B.

**Color change ranges of various indicators**:

- **Litmus**: A natural indicator derived from lichens. **Red** in acid, **blue** in alkali, **purple** when neutral. Color change range approximately pH 5–8.
- **Methyl orange**: **Red** in acid (pH < 3.1), **yellow** in alkali (pH > 4.4). The endpoint is at pH ~3.7, suitable for strong acid-weak base titrations.
- **Thymolphthalein**: **Colourless** in acid, **blue** in alkali (pH > 10). Suitable for weak acid-strong base titrations.
- **Universal Indicator**: A **mixture** of several indicators. It shows a gradual range of colors at different pH values and can be compared with a pH color chart for semi-quantitative measurement.

### 7.1.3 Conceptual Differentiation: Base, Basic Oxide, and Alkali

**Base**: In the broad sense, any substance that reacts with an acid to form a salt and water. Most are **oxides** or **hydroxides** of metals.

**Alkali**: A base that is **soluble in water**. All alkalis are bases, but not all bases are soluble in water (e.g., $\text{CuO}$ is a base but is insoluble, so it is not an alkali).

**Characteristic reactions of bases**:
- Reaction with acids (neutralisation):
  $$\text{Base} + \text{Acid} \rightarrow \text{Salt} + \text{Water}$$
- Reaction with ammonium salts (produces ammonia gas):
  $$\text{Base} + \text{NH}_4^+ \rightarrow \text{Salt} + \text{H}_2\text{O} + \text{NH}_3\uparrow$$

The reaction with ammonium salts is a **characteristic identification test** for bases — the base provides $\text{OH}^-$, which reacts with $\text{NH}_4^+$ to produce $\text{NH}_3$ gas (pungent odor, turns damp red litmus paper blue).

### 7.1.4 Brønsted-Lowry Proton Theory of Acids and Bases (Deep Dive)

Compared to the Arrhenius theory (limited to aqueous solutions), the **Brønsted-Lowry theory** is more universal:

- **Acid** = a substance that can **donate a proton** ($\text{H}^+$)
- **Base** = a substance that can **accept a proton** ($\text{H}^+$)

**Conjugate Acid-Base Pairs** are the core concept of this theory:

$$ \text{HA} \rightleftharpoons \text{H}^+ + \text{A}^- $$
$$ \text{Acid} \quad \quad \text{Conjugate Base} $$

Every acid has a corresponding **conjugate base** (the form after losing a proton), and every base has a corresponding **conjugate acid** (the form after gaining a proton).

**The essence of acid-base reactions**: a **proton transfer** process. In a reaction, the acid transfers a proton to the base.

$$\text{Acid}_1 + \text{Base}_2 \rightleftharpoons \text{Base}_1 + \text{Acid}_2$$

For example, the **autoionization** of water:

$$\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightleftharpoons \text{H}_3\text{O}^+(aq) + \text{OH}^-(aq)$$

Here, one water molecule acts as an acid (donates a proton) and another water molecule acts as a base (accepts a proton). This reveals the **amphoteric** nature of water.

### 7.1.5 Deep Comparison of Strong and Weak Acids

The core difference between strong and weak acids lies in the **degree of dissociation** — the proportion of solute molecules that actually dissociate into ions.

| Property | Strong Acid | Weak Acid |
|----------|-------------|-----------|
| Extent of dissociation | **Complete** (~100%) | **Partial** (< 100%) |
| Equilibrium symbol | $\rightarrow$ (unidirectional) | $\rightleftharpoons$ (equilibrium) |
| Ions in solution | Almost entirely in ionic form | Molecules and ions coexist |
| pH at same concentration | **Lower** ($[\text{H}^+]$ is larger) | **Higher** ($[\text{H}^+]$ is smaller) |
| Electrical conductivity | **Higher** (more free ions) | **Lower** (fewer free ions) |
| Reaction rate | **Faster** ($\text{H}^+$ immediately available) | **Slower** (must continuously dissociate to replenish $\text{H}^+$) |

**Dissociation of a strong acid** is a "one-shot" process — all acid molecules dissociate simultaneously; no reverse reaction occurs.

$$\text{HCl}(aq) \rightarrow \text{H}^+(aq) + \text{Cl}^-(aq)$$

**Dissociation of a weak acid** is an **equilibrium process** — while molecules are continuously dissociating, ions are also continuously recombining:

$$\text{CH}_3\text{COOH}(aq) \rightleftharpoons \text{H}^+(aq) + \text{CH}_3\text{COO}^-(aq)$$

The equilibrium constant $K_a$ (acid dissociation constant) of a weak acid quantifies its strength:

$$K_a = \frac{[\text{H}^+][\text{A}^-]}{[\text{HA}]}$$

The larger the $K_a$, the stronger the acid. It is often expressed as $\text{p}K_a = -\log K_a$; the smaller the $\text{p}K_a$, the stronger the acid.

**Key distinction between a concentrated weak acid and a dilute strong acid**: even at the same molar concentration, the pH of a strong acid is far lower than that of a weak acid. However, it is important to note that although a weak acid has a small degree of dissociation, its **stoichiometric neutralisation capacity** (total number of protons available for neutralisation) is the same as that of an equal concentration of a strong acid. This is because as the neutralisation reaction proceeds (consuming $\text{H}^+$), the dissociation equilibrium of the weak acid continuously shifts to the right, and **all protons are ultimately released**.

### 7.1.6 The Mathematical Essence of the pH Scale

pH is a **negative logarithmic** measure of $[\text{H}^+]$. The significance of this logarithmic scale is that $[\text{H}^+]$ spans a factor of $10^{14}$ (pH 0–14). The logarithmic transformation maps this vast range onto the intuitive 0–14 interval.

$$\text{pH} = -\log_{10}[\text{H}^+]$$

$$[\text{H}^+] = 10^{-\text{pH}} \text{ mol/dm}^3$$

**The Ionic Product of Water ($K_w$)**:

$$K_w = [\text{H}^+][\text{OH}^-] = 1.0 \times 10^{-14} \text{ mol}^2\text{dm}^{-6} \text{ (at 25°C)}$$

In pure water, $[\text{H}^+] = [\text{OH}^-] = 1.0 \times 10^{-7} \text{ mol/dm}^3$, pH = 7.

**Deep understanding of the pH scale**:
- For each unit change in pH, $[\text{H}^+]$ changes by a factor of **10**
- pH 0: $[\text{H}^+] = 1 \text{ mol/dm}^3$ (e.g., 1M strong acid)
- pH 7: $[\text{H}^+] = 10^{-7} \text{ mol/dm}^3$ (neutral water)
- pH 14: $[\text{H}^+] = 10^{-14} \text{ mol/dm}^3$ ($[\text{OH}^-] = 1 \text{ mol/dm}^3$, e.g., 1M strong alkali)

---

## 7.2 Classification of Oxides — A Manifestation of Elemental Properties

The acidity/basicity of oxides is a direct reflection of an element's **metallic/non-metallic character**.

### 7.2.1 Acidic Oxides

**Source**: **Non-metallic elements** combining with oxygen.

**Chemical essence**: React with water to form **oxyacids**, or react with bases to form salt and water.

$$\text{Non-metal oxide} + \text{H}_2\text{O} \rightarrow \text{Acid}$$

For example:

$$\text{CO}_2 + \text{H}_2\text{O} \rightleftharpoons \text{H}_2\text{CO}_3$$

$$\text{SO}_2 + \text{H}_2\text{O} \rightleftharpoons \text{H}_2\text{SO}_3$$

Most acidic oxides are **molecular compounds** (covalent bonds) with relatively low melting points.

### 7.2.2 Basic Oxides

**Source**: **Metallic elements** combining with oxygen.

**Chemical essence**: React with acids to form salt and water. Most **do not** react directly with water (except for reactive metal oxides like $\text{Na}_2\text{O}, \text{K}_2\text{O}, \text{CaO}$).

$$\text{Metal oxide} + \text{Acid} \rightarrow \text{Salt} + \text{Water}$$

For example:

$$\text{CuO} + 2\text{H}^+ \rightarrow \text{Cu}^{2+} + \text{H}_2\text{O}$$

Basic oxides are usually **ionic compounds** with high melting points and lattice structures.

### 7.2.3 Amphoteric Oxides — The Dual Personality of Acid-Base Behavior

Amphoteric oxides **can react with both acids and bases**, in each case forming a salt and water.

Amphoteric oxides appear at the **transition zone between metal and non-metal regions** of the Periodic Table — such as $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$ and $\text{ZnO}$.

**The bonding essence of amphoteric behavior**:

The $\text{Al-O}$ bond in $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$ has **partial covalent** character. It is neither purely ionic (like $\text{Na}_2\text{O}$) nor purely covalent (like $\text{CO}_2$). This intermediate nature gives it the ability to "please both sides":

- When reacting with an acid: $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$ acts as a **base**, accepting $\text{H}^+$, breaking $\text{Al-O}$ bonds, forming $\text{Al}^{3+}$ and water
- When reacting with an alkali: $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$ acts as an **acid**, reacting with $\text{OH}^-$ to form **aluminate ($\text{AlO}_2^-$)** and water

$$\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3 + 6\text{H}^+ \rightarrow 2\text{Al}^{3+} + 3\text{H}_2\text{O} \quad (\text{acts as base})$$

$$\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3 + 2\text{OH}^- \rightarrow 2\text{AlO}_2^- + \text{H}_2\text{O} \quad (\text{acts as acid})$$

---

## 7.3 Preparation of Salts — Solubility and Reaction Pathways

### 7.3.1 The Underlying Logic of Solubility Rules

Solubility rules derive from the competition between the **lattice energy** of ions and their **hydration energy**.

**The thermodynamics behind the rules**:

Dissolution process:

$$\text{Ionic solid} \xrightarrow{\text{(1) Lattice energy}} \text{Individual ions} \xrightarrow{\text{(2) Hydration energy}} \text{Hydrated ions in solution}$$

- **Lattice energy**: must be overcome (endothermic)
- **Hydration energy**: energy is released (exothermic)
- **Net energy** = Hydration energy - Lattice energy

If the net energy is negative (exothermic), dissolution is spontaneous (soluble); if positive and sufficiently large, the substance is insoluble.

**Reasons behind specific rules** (using chlorides as an example):
- Most chlorides are soluble: $\text{Cl}^-$ has a moderate radius and a relatively large hydration energy
- $\text{AgCl}$ is insoluble: the lattice energy between $\text{Ag}^+$ and $\text{Cl}^-$ is exceptionally high (due to d-electron polarization by $\text{Ag}^+$)
- $\text{PbCl}_2$ is sparingly soluble: for similar reasons, $\text{Pb}^{2+}$ has strong polarizing ability

### 7.3.2 Principles of Preparing Soluble Salts

**Method One: Acid + Alkali (Titration Method)**
- Applicable conditions: both acid and alkali are soluble
- Difficulty: the endpoint cannot be determined by an "excess method" (both are solutions; there is no visible signal of completion)
- Solution: use an **indicator** and **titration technique**
  - Add the alkali (or acid) dropwise from a **burette** into the acid (or alkali)
  - The indicator changes color at the **end-point**
  - Precisely record the volume required to reach the endpoint
  - Re-mix the same volumes of acid and alkali (**without indicator**) to obtain a pure salt solution
  - Evaporate to crystallize to obtain solid salt

**Method Two: Acid + Excess Metal**
- Applicable conditions: the metal is above hydrogen in the reactivity series and forms a soluble salt with the acid
- Principle: the metal reacts with the acid until the acid is exhausted; excess metal can be removed by **filtration**
- Advantage: simple operation, no titration or indicator required
- Filtrate = pure salt solution → evaporate to crystallize

**Method Three: Acid + Excess Insoluble Base**
- Applicable conditions: the base (metal oxide or hydroxide) is insoluble in water
- Principle: the solid base reacts with the acid; the base is gradually consumed. When the acid is completely reacted, the excess solid base does not dissolve and can be removed by **filtration**
- Filtrate = pure salt solution
- For slow-reacting bases (e.g., $\text{CuO}$), **heating** is required to increase the reaction rate

**Method Four: Acid + Excess Insoluble Carbonate**
- Principle: similar to the excess insoluble base method
- Observing the endpoint: when $\text{CO}_2$ bubbles are no longer produced, the reaction is complete
- Excess carbonate is removed by filtration

### 7.3.3 Preparation of Insoluble Salts — The Precipitation Method

The core principle of **precipitation**: mix two soluble salts in water so that the target ions combine to form an **insoluble salt** that precipitates out.

$$\text{Soluble salt A} + \text{Soluble salt B} \rightarrow \text{Insoluble salt} + \text{Soluble salt}$$

**Key to precipitation**: choose the correct soluble reactants so that when the target cation and anion meet in solution, their **ionic product exceeds the solubility product ($K_{sp}$)**, causing precipitation.

The complete operational procedure of precipitation:
1. Select two soluble salts — one providing the desired cation, the other providing the desired anion
2. Dissolve both salts separately in water
3. Mix the solutions → the target salt precipitates out
4. **Filtration** to collect the precipitate
5. **Washing** the precipitate (using deionized water to remove residual soluble impurities)
6. **Drying** the precipitate (in an oven at low temperature or air-dried naturally)

### 7.3.4 Deep Analysis of Water of Crystallisation and Hydrated Salts

**Hydration**: Certain ionic compounds, during crystallisation, incorporate water molecules **in a fixed ratio** into the crystal structure. These water molecules are not "wet" water; they are **chemically bonded** into the crystal lattice through hydrogen bonds or coordination bonds.

- **Anhydrous**: contains no water of crystallisation
- **Hydrated**: contains a specific number of water molecules of crystallisation

**Effects of water of crystallisation on material properties**:
- **Color change**: the most notable feature. For example, anhydrous $\text{CuSO}_4$ is **white**, hydrated is **blue**; anhydrous $\text{CoCl}_2$ is **blue**, hydrated is **pink**
- **Crystal morphology**: hydrated salts usually form regular large crystals
- **Thermal stability**: heating can drive off the water of crystallisation
  $$\text{CuSO}_4 \cdot 5\text{H}_2\text{O} \xrightarrow{\Delta} \text{CuSO}_4 + 5\text{H}_2\text{O}$$

**Notation for the number of water molecules of crystallisation** (the **•** symbol indicates hydration):
- $\text{CuSO}_4 \cdot 5\text{H}_2\text{O}$ — copper(II) sulfate pentahydrate (blue vitriol)
- $\text{CoCl}_2 \cdot 6\text{H}_2\text{O}$ — cobalt(II) chloride hexahydrate

The **•** here does not represent a multiplication sign for a chemical bond, but indicates that water molecules are bound to the ions in a special way.

### 7.3.5 The Deep Significance of the Ionic Equation for Neutralisation

$$\text{H}^+(aq) + \text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{O}(l)$$

The **universality** of this ionic equation lies in the fact that regardless of which strong acid and strong base react, the **net chemical reaction** of neutralisation is always this. All strong acids provide $\text{H}^+$, all strong bases provide $\text{OH}^-$, and the two combine to form water; **spectator ions** do not participate in the reaction.

**The exothermic nature of neutralisation**: the formation of a covalent bond between $\text{H}^+$ and $\text{OH}^-$ releases approximately $57.3 \text{ kJ/mol}$ of heat (standard enthalpy of neutralisation).

**Application dimensions of neutralisation**:
- Laboratory preparation of salts
- Agriculture: adjusting soil pH (using lime to neutralise acidic soil)
- Industrial wastewater treatment (acidic wastewater is neutralised with alkali before being released into the environment)
- Medicine: antacid medications (e.g., $\text{Mg(OH)}_2$ neutralises stomach acid)

---

> **Comprehensive Summary of Both Topics**:
> 
> **Topic 6 (Chemical Reactions)** is essentially understood through three dimensions: (1) **Physical vs chemical changes** — judging whether matter has changed at the molecular level; (2) **Reaction rate** — from collision theory, how temperature, concentration, pressure, surface area, and catalysts affect particle behavior at the microscopic level; (3) **Reversibility and equilibrium** — Le Chatelier's Principle as a thermodynamic intuition guiding us to predict how condition changes affect equilibrium, with the Haber and Contact processes providing a window into the strategic trade-off between rate and equilibrium in industrial chemistry; (4) **Redox** — from oxygen gain/loss to electron transfer to oxidation numbers, this triple progressive definition is a master key for understanding "who gets oxidised and who gets reduced" in the chemical world.
> 
> **Topic 7 (Acids, Bases and Salts)** proceeds from the characteristic properties of acids and bases, passes through the theoretical evolution from Arrhenius to Brønsted-Lowry, delves into the essence of strong vs weak dissociation and the logarithmic mysteries of the pH scale; then extends to the connection between oxide acidity/basicity and Periodic trends; and finally lands on salt preparation as the applied endpoint, integrating solubility rules, the chemistry of water of crystallisation, and the principles of precipitation into a unified framework.
> ---
> ---
> # TOPIC 8: The Periodic Table — A Deep Dive

---

## 8.1 The Arrangement of Elements — Foundational Principles

### 8.1.1 Historical Trajectory: From Mendeleev to the Modern Periodic Table

The prototype of the periodic table was proposed by the Russian chemist **Dmitri Mendeleev** in 1869. Mendeleev's core insight was that the elements known at the time were not a chaotic collection; rather, when arranged in order of **increasing relative atomic mass**, their chemical properties exhibited **periodic repetition**. He made a remarkably forward-thinking move—**boldly leaving gaps**—predicting the existence and properties of elements not yet discovered (such as gallium, scandium, and germanium). These predictions were later confirmed one by one. This stands as a classic case in the history of science where theoretical prediction preceded experimental discovery.

The **modern periodic table** differs fundamentally from Mendeleev's era in its core organizing principle—the ordering basis shifted from **relative atomic mass** to **atomic number (proton number, $Z$)** . This change stemmed from the X-ray spectroscopy experiments of **Henry Moseley** in 1913, which demonstrated that the nuclear charge (i.e., the number of protons) is the most fundamental characteristic of an element, not its atomic mass. This discovery resolved several contradictions in Mendeleev's table (such as the inverted positions of tellurium and iodine, and argon and potassium—where ordering by atomic mass gave the wrong sequence, but ordering by proton number was perfectly consistent).

The modern periodic table can be precisely summarized as:

> **The Periodic Table is an arrangement of elements in order of increasing atomic number (proton number), with elements sharing similar electron configurations placed in the same column (group), and elements in the same row (period) having the same number of occupied electron shells.**

### 8.1.2 The Two-Dimensional Structure: The Physical Essence of Periods and Groups

The periodic table is "two-dimensional" because it simultaneously reflects two independent yet related physical quantities:

**Horizontal Axis — Periods**:
Each period begins when a new electron shell starts to fill. The period number equals the **number of occupied electron shells** in the atoms of that period. For example:
- Period 1 (H, He): Only 1 electron shell (K shell)
- Period 2 (Li → Ne): 2 electron shells (K shell full, L shell partially filled)
- Period 3 (Na → Ar): 3 electron shells

Thus, **moving downward** in the table means adding a complete electron shell.

**Vertical Axis — Groups**:
Elements in the same group have the **same number of outer-shell electrons (valence electrons)** , which is the fundamental reason for their similar chemical properties.

- **Main Group Elements**: Groups I, II, III, IV, V, VI, VII, VIII
  - For Groups I → VII, **group number = number of outer-shell electrons**
  - For Groups I → III, **group number = the common positive charge of ions formed by elements in that group** (e.g., Na in Group I forms $\text{Na}^+$; Mg in Group II forms $\text{Mg}^{2+}$; Al in Group III forms $\text{Al}^{3+}$)
  - For Groups V → VII, **group number = 8 − negative charge of the anion** (e.g., Cl in Group VII forms $\text{Cl}^-$; O in Group VI forms $\text{O}^{2-}$)

This pattern originates from the tendency of atoms to achieve the **stable noble gas electron configuration** (the octet rule) by gaining or losing electrons.

- **Transition Elements**: Located in the d-block in the middle of Periods 4, 5, and 6. Their valence electrons include not only the outermost s electrons but also the d electrons of the penultimate shell, giving them unique properties (see Section 8.4).

### 8.1.3 Periodic Trends: The Gradual Shift from Left to Right

The gradient from **metallic to non-metallic character** is the most important horizontal trend in the periodic table. The microscopic origin of this trend lies in the increase of **Effective Nuclear Charge ($Z_{\text{eff}}$)** .

Effective nuclear charge is the net positive charge experienced by outer electrons from the nucleus. Moving left to right across a period:
1. The number of protons (nuclear charge) increases
2. Electrons fill into the same shell, so the shielding effect does not increase significantly
3. Therefore, **$Z_{\text{eff}}$ increases markedly**
4. The nucleus attracts outer electrons more strongly
5. **Atomic radius decreases**

This triggers a cascade of effects:
- **Ionization Energy ($IE$)** increases: losing electrons becomes more difficult → metallic character weakens
- **Electronegativity** increases: the tendency to gain electrons strengthens → non-metallic character strengthens
- The absolute value of **Electron Affinity** increases (in the non-metal region)

The **boundary** between metals and non-metals roughly follows the diagonal line B–Si–As–Te–At (boron, silicon, arsenic, tellurium, astatine). Elements near this boundary often exhibit **amphoteric** behavior.

### 8.1.4 Group Trends: The Core Reason for Top-to-Bottom Changes

When moving downward within the same group, although the number of protons increases, the addition of a **complete new electron shell** causes a dramatic increase in atomic radius. This leads to:

- **Atomic radius increases substantially** (this is the dominant factor)
- Outer electrons are increasingly distant from the nucleus
- The shielding effect becomes stronger
- The attraction of the effective nuclear charge on outer electrons **weakens**

Consequently:
- **Metallic elements (e.g., Group I)**: The tendency to lose electrons increases → reactivity increases
- **Non-metallic elements (e.g., Group VII)**: The tendency to gain electrons decreases → reactivity decreases

This principle serves as the **unified explanatory framework** for all vertical trends across the periodic table.

### 8.1.5 Using an Element's Position to Predict Its Properties

This is the core function of the periodic table as a chemical tool: **knowing an element's position in the table (period number + group number) allows for accurate prediction of numerous properties.**

- **Metallic/Non-metallic Nature**: Lower left → strongly metallic; upper right → strongly non-metallic
- **Valence**: For main group elements I → VII, the highest positive oxidation state = group number; the highest negative oxidation state = group number − 8
- **Ion Charge**: See Section 8.1.2
- **Reaction Tendency**: Metals tend to lose electrons (reducing agents); non-metals tend to gain electrons (oxidizing agents)
- **Physical Property Trends**: Melting point, boiling point, hardness, etc., can be reasonably extrapolated from known elements in the same group

---

## 8.2 Group I — The Alkali Metals

### 8.2.1 Atomic Structure Characteristics of Alkali Metals

The common structural feature of alkali metals (lithium Li, sodium Na, potassium K, rubidium Rb, caesium Cs, francium Fr) is: **only 1 electron in the outermost shell** (electron configuration $ns^1$), located in the s-block. This single electron is relatively far from the nucleus and experiences relatively weak Coulomb attraction, making it **extremely easy to lose**.

From the perspective of electron configuration:
- After losing this outermost electron, the alkali metal ion attains the **stable electron configuration of the preceding noble gas**
- For example, $\text{Na}$ (2,8,1) → $\text{Na}^+$ (2,8), isoelectronic with Ne
- This great stability of the noble gas configuration provides a strong thermodynamic driving force for electron loss

### 8.2.2 In-Depth Explanation of Downward Trends

**(1) Melting Point Gradually Decreases**

Melting points of alkali metals decrease from top to bottom:
- Li: 180.5°C
- Na: 97.8°C
- K: 63.4°C
- Rb: 39.3°C
- Cs: 28.5°C

Microscopic explanation: The strength of metallic bonding in alkali metals depends on the **electrostatic attraction between delocalized electrons and positive ions**. Because:
1. Atomic radius increases downward → positive ions get larger
2. The single outer electron gets increasingly distant from the nucleus → delocalized electron density decreases
3. The charge density (charge/volume ratio) of positive ions decreases

Thus, the metallic bond weakens progressively, requiring less thermal energy to disrupt the lattice → melting point drops.

**(2) Density Gradually Increases**

Although both atomic mass and atomic volume increase downward, the **rate of mass increase exceeds the rate of volume increase**, so density generally rises. Notable exceptions: Li has a very low density (0.53 g/cm³), floating on water; Na (0.97 g/cm³) also floats; K (0.86 g/cm³) is anomalously slightly less dense than Na, but thereafter density increases continuously with atomic number.

**(3) Reactivity Gradually Increases — The Most Important Trend**

Alkali metal reactivity increases **sharply** downward. This trend is driven by the continuous decrease in **Ionization Energy ($IE$)** .

The first ionization energy $IE_1$ is the minimum energy required to remove the outermost electron from a gaseous atom:

$$\text{M}(g) \rightarrow \text{M}^+(g) + e^- \quad IE_1$$

Ionization energy has an inverse relationship with atomic radius:

$$IE_1 \propto \frac{Z_{\text{eff}}}{r}$$

where $Z_{\text{eff}}$ is effective nuclear charge and $r$ is atomic radius. Moving down a group, the increase in $Z_{\text{eff}}$ is far less significant than the increase in $r$, so $IE_1$ continuously decreases.

This means: **The further down, the more easily the outer electron can be removed, and the stronger the reducing power.**

### 8.2.3 Quantitative Analysis of Alkali Metals with Water

The general reaction of alkali metals with water is:

$$2\text{M}(s) + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightarrow 2\text{MOH}(aq) + \text{H}_2(g)$$

From a mechanistic perspective, this is a **redox reaction**:
- The alkali metal M is oxidized to $\text{M}^+$ (loses electrons): $$\text{M} \rightarrow \text{M}^+ + e^-$$
- Water's $\text{H}_2\text{O}$ is reduced to $\text{H}_2$ and $\text{OH}^-$ (gains electrons): $$2\text{H}_2\text{O} + 2e^- \rightarrow \text{H}_2 + 2\text{OH}^-$$

The violence of the reaction increases with atomic number:
- Li: Slow reaction, does not melt (high melting point)
- Na: Fast reaction, melts (reaction heat sufficient to melt Na), moves on water surface
- K: Very vigorous reaction, produces a lilac flame (characteristic flame color of K), enough heat to ignite the hydrogen produced
- Rb, Cs: Explosive reaction

Li's relatively mild behavior is not only due to its higher $IE_1$ but also because its product $\text{LiOH}$ has relatively low solubility, partially covering the lithium surface.

### 8.2.4 Alkali Metals with Oxygen

Alkali metals burning in oxygen produce different types of oxides, reflecting the influence of cation size on product stability:

- Li: Mainly forms **lithium oxide**: $$4\text{Li} + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow 2\text{Li}_2\text{O}$$
- Na: Mainly forms **sodium peroxide**: $$2\text{Na} + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{Na}_2\text{O}_2$$
- K, Rb, Cs: Form **superoxides**: $$\text{M} + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{MO}_2$$

The principle behind the changing product types: Large cations (K⁺, Rb⁺, Cs⁺) can better stabilize large anions ($\text{O}_2^-$ superoxide ions), because the lattice energy from large ions is sufficient to compensate for the extra energy required to form $\text{O}_2^-$. In contrast, the small Li⁺ can only stabilize the smallest $\text{O}^{2-}$ ion.

---

## 8.3 Group VII — The Halogens

### 8.3.1 Atomic Structure Characteristics of Halogens

The common structural feature of halogens (fluorine F, chlorine Cl, bromine Br, iodine I, astatine At) is: **7 electrons in the outermost shell** (electron configuration $ns^2np^5$), located in the p-block. Each halogen atom needs only **1 more electron** to reach the stable noble gas electron configuration (octet).

Halogens therefore exhibit an extremely strong **tendency to gain electrons**, making them typical **non-metals** and **oxidizing agents**.

Halogens exist as **diatomic molecules ($\text{X}_2$)** , because two halogen atoms share one pair of electrons to form a **single covalent bond**:

$$\text{X} \cdot + \cdot \text{X} \rightarrow \text{X} : \text{X}$$

The intermolecular forces are **London Dispersion Forces**, which strengthen as the number of electrons in the molecule increases.

### 8.3.2 In-Depth Explanation of Physical Property Trends Downward

**(1) State at Room Temperature**

- F₂: Pale yellow gas
- Cl₂: Yellowish-green gas
- Br₂: Red-brown liquid (the only liquid non-metallic element at r.t.p.)
- I₂: Grey-black solid (sublimes on heating—turns directly from solid to violet vapor without melting)

The shift from gas → liquid → solid originates from the strengthening of **intermolecular forces (London Dispersion Forces)** :

$$F_{\text{London}} \propto \frac{\alpha^2}{r^6}$$

where $\alpha$ is the **polarizability** of the molecule, proportional to the square of the total number of electrons.

Numbers of electrons in halogen molecules $\text{X}_2$: F₂ (18) → Cl₂ (34) → Br₂ (70) → I₂ (106). More electrons make the formation of instantaneous dipoles easier, strengthening London forces and requiring more heat to melt or boil the substance.

**(2) Melting and Boiling Points Gradually Rise**

- F₂: mp −220°C, bp −188°C
- Cl₂: mp −101°C, bp −34°C
- Br₂: mp −7°C, bp 59°C
- I₂: mp 114°C, bp 184°C

The trend is entirely consistent with the strengthening of intermolecular forces.

**(3) Density Gradually Increases**

Since halogens exist as molecules, molecular mass ($M_r$) grows faster than molecular volume, so density increases.

### 8.3.3 The Fundamental Reason for Decreasing Reactivity Downward

Halogen reactivity (oxidizing power) decreases continuously from F → I, opposite to the trend for alkali metals. The root cause lies in the combined effects of **Electron Affinity** and **atomic radius**.

General reaction (with metals to form halides):

$$2\text{M} + \text{X}_2 \rightarrow 2\text{MX}$$

Electron acceptance process:

$$\text{X}_2 + 2e^- \rightarrow 2\text{X}^-$$

The key factor determining halogen oxidizing ability is the **total energy change** when a halogen atom gains one electron and forms a hydrated halide ion:

$$\text{X}(g) + e^- \rightarrow \text{X}^-(g) \quad \Delta H = \text{Electron Affinity}$$

From F to I:

1. **Atomic radius increases**: F has a very small atomic radius (~71 pm), while I is much larger (~133 pm)
2. **Electron cloud density decreases**: In small atoms, the newly added electron is closer to the nucleus and experiences stronger positive attraction
3. **Electron Affinity**: Cl actually has a greater (more negative) electron affinity than F, which seems counterintuitive. F's electron affinity is not as large as Cl's because F's atomic radius is so small that the incoming electron experiences strong repulsion from the 9 electrons already present. However, F₂ has an **anomalously low bond energy** (the F–F bond is much weaker than expected due to lone pair repulsion), and F⁻ has a very large hydration enthalpy. Considering all factors together, F₂ remains the **strongest oxidizing agent**.

**Summary**: Reactivity $\text{F}_2 > \text{Cl}_2 > \text{Br}_2 > \text{I}_2$, because as atomic radius increases, the nucleus's attraction for an additional electron weakens progressively.

### 8.3.4 Halogen Color Intensity and Electronic Transitions

All halogens are colored, and the color **deepens** with increasing atomic number. The origin of color lies in **electronic transitions** between molecular orbitals, absorbing specific wavelengths of visible light.

$$\Delta E = h\nu = \frac{hc}{\lambda}$$

where $\Delta E$ is the energy gap between ground and excited states, $h$ is Planck's constant, $\nu$ is frequency, $\lambda$ is wavelength, and $c$ is the speed of light.

In halogen molecules, as atomic number increases:
- Molecular orbital energy gaps narrow
- The energy of absorbed photons decreases
- Absorbed light shifts from high energy (violet) toward lower energy (red)
- The observed complementary color shifts from pale yellow (F₂) → yellow-green (Cl₂) → red-brown (Br₂) → violet-black (I₂)

### 8.3.5 Deep Principles of Halogen Displacement Reactions

Displacement reactions directly demonstrate the differences in halogen reactivity:

$$\text{X}_2 + 2\text{Y}^- \rightarrow 2\text{X}^- + \text{Y}_2$$

where X is more reactive than Y (higher up in the group).

From a thermodynamic perspective, this reflects differences in **Redox Potential**:
- More reactive halogens (stronger oxidizing agents) have higher **Standard Electrode Potentials ($E^\ominus$)** , making them more readily reduced
- A more reactive halogen can displace a less reactive halogen from its salt solution

Standard electrode potential half-reactions:

$$\text{X}_2 + 2e^- \rightarrow 2\text{X}^- \quad E^\ominus$$

$E^\ominus$ values: $\text{F}_2/\text{F}^- (+2.87\text{V}) > \text{Cl}_2/\text{Cl}^- (+1.36\text{V}) > \text{Br}_2/\text{Br}^- (+1.07\text{V}) > \text{I}_2/\text{I}^- (+0.54\text{V})$

Higher $E^\ominus$ values mean stronger oxidizing ability.

---

## 8.4 Transition Elements

### 8.4.1 Electron Configuration Characteristics of Transition Elements

Definition of transition elements: **Elements that have partially filled d-electron subshells** (or whose common ions have partially filled d subshells).

In electron configurations, the outermost s orbitals and the penultimate d orbitals of transition elements both participate in chemical behavior. Taking the first transition series (Sc → Zn) as an example:

The general electron configuration is: $$[\text{Ar}]\,3d^{1-10}\,4s^{1-2}$$

An important energy crossover phenomenon occurs here: in neutral transition atoms, **the 4s orbital is at a lower energy than the 3d orbital**, so 4s electrons fill before 3d electrons. However, when forming ions, **the 4s electrons are lost first**, because once ionization begins, the 4s orbital's energy rises above that of 3d.

### 8.4.2 The Origin of Colored Compounds — Crystal Field Theory

The reason transition element compounds display a rich variety of colors lies fundamentally in **d-d electronic transitions**.

In a free ion, all five d-orbitals have exactly the same energy (they are degenerate). However, when a transition metal ion is in a compound, the electric field from surrounding ligands (anions or polar molecules) causes these five d-orbitals to **split in energy (Crystal Field Splitting)** .

Taking the octahedral coordination field as an example, the five d-orbitals split into two sets:
- High-energy set: $d_{x^2-y^2}$ and $d_{z^2}$ (denoted as $e_g$)
- Low-energy set: $d_{xy}, d_{yz}, d_{zx}$ (denoted as $t_{2g}$)

The energy difference is denoted $\Delta_{\text{oct}}$ (octahedral field splitting energy):

When visible light strikes a transition metal compound, d-electrons absorb photons of specific wavelengths, jumping from lower to higher energy levels:

$$\Delta_{\text{oct}} = h\nu = \frac{hc}{\lambda}$$

The wavelength (color) of absorbed light depends on the magnitude of $\Delta_{\text{oct}}$, which in turn is influenced by:
- The type and oxidation state of the metal ion (higher oxidation states → larger $\Delta_{\text{oct}}$)
- The type of ligand
- Coordination number and geometry

The unabsorbed light is reflected (the complementary color), which is the color we see. For example:
- $\text{Cu}^{2+}$ compounds absorb red/orange light → appear blue
- $\text{Fe}^{3+}$ compounds absorb blue-green light → appear red-brown
- $\text{Cr}^{3+}$ compounds absorb yellow-green light → appear purple/green
- $\text{Ni}^{2+}$ compounds absorb blue-violet light → appear green

**Why are $\text{Sc}^{3+}$ and $\text{Zn}^{2+}$ compounds colorless?** Because $\text{Sc}^{3+}$ has an empty d subshell ($3d^0$), and $\text{Zn}^{2+}$ has a completely filled d subshell ($3d^{10}$). In both cases, d-electrons cannot undergo transitions between split d-orbitals, so no visible light is absorbed.

### 8.4.3 The Principle of Variable Oxidation States

The reason transition elements can exhibit multiple oxidation states is fundamentally that **d-electrons and s-electrons are close in energy**, and both can partially or fully participate in bonding.

Taking iron (Fe) as an example:
- Electron configuration: $[\text{Ar}]\,3d^6\,4s^2$
- $\text{Fe}^{2+}$ (loses both 4s electrons): $[\text{Ar}]\,3d^6$
- $\text{Fe}^{3+}$ (loses both 4s electrons plus one 3d electron): $[\text{Ar}]\,3d^5$

The half-filled d-orbital configuration of $\text{Fe}^{3+}$ ($3d^5$) has additional **exchange energy stability**, making $\text{Fe}^{3+}$ thermodynamically the more stable oxidation state of iron.

Common oxidation states of typical transition elements:
- Mn: +2, +3, +4, +6, +7 (extremely wide range)
- Cr: +2, +3, +6
- Cu: +1, +2
- Fe: +2, +3
- Co: +2, +3

### 8.4.4 The Deep Mechanism of Catalytic Activity

Transition elements and their compounds are excellent catalysts, which can be understood at multiple levels:

**(1) Variable Oxidation State Mechanism**: Transition metals can alternate between oxidation states during a reaction, providing an electron-transfer "bridge" for reactants. For example, in the Contact process for sulfuric acid production, $\text{V}_2\text{O}_5$ acts as a catalyst:

$$\text{V}_2\text{O}_5 + \text{SO}_2 \rightarrow \text{V}_2\text{O}_4 + \text{SO}_3$$
$$\text{V}_2\text{O}_4 + \frac{1}{2}\text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{V}_2\text{O}_5$$

Vanadium cycles between +5 and +4 oxidation states, lowering the activation energy of the reaction.

**(2) Surface Adsorption Mechanism**: The surfaces of transition metals have unsaturated coordination sites that can adsorb reactant molecules, fixing them in the most favorable geometric configuration for reaction while simultaneously weakening the internal bonds of adsorbed molecules, making reactions easier. This is the fundamental principle behind iron's catalysis of the Haber process and platinum's catalysis of ammonia oxidation.

**(3) d-Orbital Participation**: The d-orbitals of transition metals can form **coordinate bonds** with reactant molecules, stabilizing the transition state and thereby lowering activation energy.

### 8.4.5 The Essential Reasons for High Density and High Melting Points

The high density of transition elements stems from **closely packed metallic crystal lattices** and **large atomic masses**. Their atomic radii in the same period first decrease then slightly increase from left to right, remaining relatively small overall, while atomic mass continues to increase—resulting in small atomic volumes with large masses → high density.

The root cause of high melting points lies in the **extremely strong metallic bonding**. Transition metal bonding involves not only delocalized s-electrons but also d-electrons. d-Electrons can form additional covalent-like bonding components—**d-orbital overlap** enhances the binding force between atoms. Therefore, disrupting the transition metal lattice requires substantial thermal energy. Tungsten (W) has the highest melting point of all metals at 3422°C.

---

## 8.5 Noble Gases

### 8.5.1 The Essence of Stability

Noble gases (He, Ne, Ar, Kr, Xe, Rn) are positioned at the far right of the periodic table, with electron configurations of **completely filled s and p orbitals**:
- He: $1s^2$ (first shell full, only 2 electrons)
- Ne: $[\text{He}]\,2s^2\,2p^6$ (octet)
- Ar: $[\text{Ne}]\,3s^2\,3p^6$ (octet)
- Kr, Xe, Rn: and so forth

This completely filled electron configuration has extremely high **stability** (very low energy), giving noble gases virtually no tendency to lose or gain electrons.

From the perspective of **Ionization Energy**, noble gases have the highest first ionization energy in each period. Removing an electron from a completely filled, stable shell requires enormous energy.

From the perspective of **Electron Affinity**, when a noble gas gains an electron, the new electron must enter the next higher shell, requiring a significant energy cost, so electron affinities are very small or even positive.

### 8.5.2 The Relativity of "Inertness"

Noble gases were once called "inert gases," but this designation is not absolute. In 1962, Neil Bartlett successfully synthesized the first noble gas compound—**xenon hexafluoroplatinate ($\text{Xe}[\text{PtF}_6]$)** —shattering the notion that noble gases could not form compounds at all.

Larger noble gas atoms (Kr, Xe), because of:
1. Larger atomic radii
2. Outer electrons farther from the nucleus and less tightly bound
3. Relatively lower electronegativity (especially Xe)

can, under specific conditions (with strongly electronegative elements like F, O), form compounds such as $\text{XeF}_2$, $\text{XeF}_4$, $\text{XeO}_3$, etc. However, no stable compounds of He or Ne have been discovered to date.

### 8.5.3 Explanation of Monatomic Existence

Unlike most gaseous non-metallic elements (e.g., $\text{H}_2$, $\text{O}_2$, $\text{N}_2$, $\text{Cl}_2$) which exist as diatomic molecules, noble gases exist as **monatomic gases**.

The reason: Noble gases already have a stable electron configuration, so atoms do not form covalent bonds with each other (since covalent bond formation requires electron sharing to achieve an octet for both atoms—they already have one). Only extremely weak **London Dispersion Forces** exist between noble gas atoms, so they exist as single atoms and can be liquefied only at very low temperatures.

---

# TOPIC 9: Metals — A Deep Dive

---

## 9.1 Physical Properties of Metals vs. Non-Metals

### 9.1.1 In-Depth Analysis of Metallic Bonding Theory

The unique physical properties of metals originate from **Metallic Bonding**, a distinctive mode of chemical bonding.

The modern description of metallic bonding is the **"positive ion lattice immersed in a sea of delocalized electrons"** model. Specifically:

In a metal crystal, each metal atom **contributes** its **valence electrons** (usually the outermost s electrons, and in some cases d electrons as well) to become "common property." These electrons no longer belong to any specific atom but move **freely** throughout the metallic lattice—they are called **delocalized electrons** or "free electrons."

The strength of metallic bonding depends on two factors:
1. **The density of delocalized electrons**—the more valence electrons each atom contributes, the stronger the metallic bond
2. **The size of the metal positive ions**—smaller ions create more concentrated electron clouds, leading to stronger electrostatic attraction

This explains why different metals have vastly different melting points and hardness:
- Na (1 valence electron, relatively large ionic radius): mp 97.8°C, very soft
- Mg (2 valence electrons, smaller ionic radius): mp 650°C, harder
- Al (3 valence electrons, even smaller ionic radius): mp 660°C
- Fe (involves d-electrons, additional covalent-like bonding): mp 1538°C

### 9.1.2 A Unified Explanation of Thermal and Electrical Conductivity

**Electrical Conductivity**: Under an applied electric field, **delocalized electrons** in the metal can flow directionally, forming an electric current. Delocalized electrons are therefore called an "electron gas" or "free electron gas."

**Thermal Conductivity**: When one end of a metal is heated, the metal ions at that end vibrate more intensely (their kinetic energy increases). This vibrational energy is transmitted to the other end via two pathways:
1. Delocalized electrons collide with vibrating ions → electrons gain extra kinetic energy → electrons move rapidly to other regions of the metal → collisions transfer energy to other ions
2. Direct propagation through lattice vibrations (phonon conduction mechanism)

Metals are the **best thermal and electrical conductors** among all solids—a direct piece of evidence for the "electron sea" model.

### 9.1.3 The Atomic-Level Explanation of Ductility and Malleability

**Ductility**—the ability to be drawn into wires—and **Malleability**—the ability to be hammered into thin sheets—are properties that distinguish metals from ionic and covalent crystals.

In a metal crystal, layers of atoms can **slide** under external force without breaking the metallic bond. The reasons are:
1. Metallic bonds are **non-directional**—the delocalized electron cloud is uniformly distributed around the positive ions, with no specific directional preference
2. When atomic layers slide, the positive ions in their new positions are still surrounded by the delocalized electron cloud, maintaining electrostatic attraction
3. Therefore, metal layers can slide to new positions without breaking metallic bonds—the metal simply deforms rather than shatters

In sharp contrast:
- **Ionic crystals** (e.g., NaCl): If layers slide, like charges come close together, producing strong repulsion, and the crystal shatters
- **Covalent crystals** (e.g., diamond): Covalent bonds have strict directional preferences. Sliding layers changes bond angles and causes bond breakage

### 9.1.4 Comparing Melting Points: Metals vs. Non-Metals

**Metals**: Due to metallic bonding, most metals have moderate to high melting points. However, alkali metals (weak metallic bonding) have low melting points, while tungsten (extremely strong metallic bonding) has an exceptionally high melting point.

**Non-Metals**: Bonding patterns among non-metals vary:
- Molecular non-metals (e.g., $\text{I}_2$, $\text{S}_8$, $\text{P}_4$): Strong intramolecular covalent bonds but weak intermolecular van der Waals forces → low melting points
- Macromolecular/network non-metals (e.g., diamond C, graphite C): Covalent bonds extend throughout the entire crystal (giant covalent structures) → extremely high melting points

---

## 9.2 Chemical Properties of Metals

### 9.2.1 Reactions with Dilute Acids — Manifestation of Ionization Tendency

The reaction of metals with dilute acids (e.g., dilute HCl, dilute $\text{H}_2\text{SO}_4$) is fundamentally a **redox reaction**:

$$\text{M}(s) + 2\text{H}^+(aq) \rightarrow \text{M}^{2+}(aq) + \text{H}_2(g)$$

(For +1 metals: $2\text{M} + 2\text{H}^+ \rightarrow 2\text{M}^+ + \text{H}_2$)

Whether this reaction can occur and how vigorously it proceeds depends entirely on the metal's **tendency to lose electrons and form cations** (i.e., its standard electrode potential).

From a thermodynamic perspective, the driving force comes from the combination of two half-reactions:

**Oxidation half-reaction** (metal is oxidized): $$\text{M}(s) \rightarrow \text{M}^{n+}(aq) + ne^-$$

**Reduction half-reaction** ($\text{H}^+$ is reduced): $$2\text{H}^+(aq) + 2e^- \rightarrow \text{H}_2(g)$$

The reaction can only proceed if the metal is a stronger reducing agent than hydrogen (i.e., the metal's standard electrode potential is lower than that of hydrogen). This is the fundamental reason why **metals above hydrogen in the reactivity series react with acids to release hydrogen gas, while metals below hydrogen (Cu, Ag, Au) do not**.

### 9.2.2 Reactions with Water and Steam

Metal-water reactions are also redox processes, where the metal reduces $\text{H}^+$ from water to $\text{H}_2$ gas.

**With Cold Water** (very reactive metals):
- K, Na, Ca react vigorously with cold water:

$$2\text{Na}(s) + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightarrow 2\text{NaOH}(aq) + \text{H}_2(g)$$

$$\text{Ca}(s) + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightarrow \text{Ca}(\text{OH})_2(aq) + \text{H}_2(g)$$

- Mg reacts very slowly with cold water (protective $\text{MgO}$ layer on surface), but hot water accelerates the reaction

The vigor of reaction depends on the metal's **reducing strength** and the **solubility of the hydroxide formed**. $\text{Ca}(\text{OH})_2$ is slightly soluble in water and partially covers the calcium surface, making the reaction less violent than with sodium.

**With Steam** (moderately reactive metals):

$$ \text{Mg}(s) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(g) \rightarrow \text{MgO}(s) + \text{H}_2(g) $$

$$\text{Zn}(s) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(g) \rightarrow \text{ZnO}(s) + \text{H}_2(g)$$

At high temperatures, the energy is sufficient to break through the metal's surface oxide layer, allowing the reaction to continue.

### 9.2.3 Reactions with Oxygen

The vigor and product type of metal combustion in oxygen directly reflect reactivity:

- **Very reactive metals** (K, Na): Burn vigorously, form peroxides or superoxides (see Section 8.2.4)
- **Reactive metals** (Mg, Ca): Burn vigorously, form oxides:

$$2\text{Mg} + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow 2\text{MgO}$$

Emits brilliant white light, producing a white solid

- **Moderately reactive metals** (Zn, Fe): React when heated in oxygen:

$$3\text{Fe} + 2\text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{Fe}_3\text{O}_4 \text{ (iron(II,III) oxide, when iron burns in oxygen)}$$

$$2\text{Zn} + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow 2\text{ZnO}$$

- **Unreactive metals** (Cu, Ag): Only react slowly at high temperatures:

$$2\text{Cu} + \text{O}_2 \xrightarrow{\Delta} 2\text{CuO}$$

- **Extremely unreactive metals** (Au, Pt): Do not react with oxygen even at high temperatures

---

## 9.3 Alloys — Structure and Properties

### 9.3.1 Definition and Classification of Alloys

An alloy is a **mixture of a metal with other elements (which can be metallic or non-metallic)** , typically produced by melting the components together and cooling.

An alloy is not a compound—the components do not form a fixed stoichiometric ratio, nor do they form ionic or covalent bonds. An alloy is a **solid solution** or a mixture of **intermetallic compounds**.

### 9.3.2 The Atomic Mechanism of Alloy Hardening — Dislocation Pinning

Pure metals are relatively soft because atomic layers in the metal crystal lattice can slide relatively easily (dislocation motion). When external force is applied to a pure metal, atomic layers move sequentially along **slip planes**, causing plastic deformation without fracture.

However, in an alloy, **differently sized atoms** disrupt the perfect periodicity of the lattice:

- If the added atoms are **smaller** than the host metal atoms, they occupy interstitial positions (interstitial solid solution)
- If the added atoms are **larger** than the host metal atoms, they replace some host atoms in the lattice (substitutional solid solution)

In either case, the presence of foreign atoms creates **local stress fields** in the lattice, which **impede the motion of dislocations**. Dislocations are "defects" in the crystal's atomic planes, and their movement is the primary mechanism of plastic deformation in metals. When a dislocation encounters the stress field produced by foreign atoms, it requires greater force to continue moving. Therefore, alloys are **harder and stronger** than pure metals.

This principle can be summarized in one sentence: **"Imperfection in the crystal lattice is the source of alloy strength."**

### 9.3.3 Microstructural Analysis of Typical Alloys

**Brass** — Alloy of Cu and Zn:
- Pure copper (Cu) has a face-centered cubic structure and is relatively soft
- Adding Zn forms a substitutional solid solution, with Zn atoms replacing some Cu atoms
- The atomic size difference (Cu 128 pm, Zn 134 pm) produces lattice distortion
- Result: Brass is **harder** than pure copper while retaining good ductility and corrosion resistance

**Stainless Steel** — Alloy of Fe + Cr + Ni + C:
- Pure iron rusts when exposed to air (forms loose, flaky $\text{Fe}_2\text{O}_3\cdot x\text{H}_2\text{O}$ that does not protect the interior)
- Adding Cr (at least 10.5%) causes Cr to preferentially form a dense, self-repairing $\text{Cr}_2\text{O}_3$ film on the surface
- This oxide film is extremely thin (only a few nanometers), transparent, firmly bonded to the substrate, and effectively **blocks O₂ and H₂O** from diffusing inward
- Ni further enhances corrosion resistance and workability
- C increases hardness (by forming carbides)
- Consequently, stainless steel is both **hard and corrosion-resistant**

---

## 9.4 The Reactivity Series — Principles and Deep Explanations

### 9.4.1 The Complete Reactivity Series

$$\text{K} > \text{Na} > \text{Ca} > \text{Mg} > \text{Al} > \text{C} > \text{Zn} > \text{Fe} > \text{H} > \text{Cu} > \text{Ag} > \text{Au}$$

It is notable that **carbon (C)** and **hydrogen (H)** are also included. Carbon is not a metal but serves as an important **reducing agent** in metal extraction; hydrogen provides the reference line for determining whether a metal can react with acids.

### 9.4.2 The Quantitative Foundation of the Reactivity Series — Standard Electrode Potentials

The essence of the reactivity series is the ranking of metals by their **Standard Electrode Potentials ($E^\ominus$)** from negative to positive:

$$E^\ominus(\text{M}^{n+}/\text{M})$$

The standard electrode potential measures a metal's tendency to lose electrons and become cations. This quantity is determined by measuring the electromotive force of a galvanic cell formed between the metal and a standard hydrogen electrode ($E^\ominus = 0.00$ V).

For the metal M half-reaction:

$$\text{M}^{n+}(aq) + ne^- \rightarrow \text{M}(s) \quad E^\ominus$$

- Large negative $E^\ominus$ (e.g., $\text{K}^+/\text{K}$ at −2.93 V): The metal loses electrons very readily and is a strong reducing agent
- Large positive $E^\ominus$ (e.g., $\text{Au}^{3+}/\text{Au}$ at +1.50 V): The metal loses electrons with great difficulty and is inert

**The reactivity series is essentially the ranking of $E^\ominus$ values.** The most reactive metal (K) has the most negative $E^\ominus$, and the least reactive metal (Au) has the most positive $E^\ominus$.

### 9.4.3 Microscopic Factors Influencing Metal Reactivity

The process of a metal M losing electrons to form $\text{M}^{n+}(aq)$ can be decomposed thermodynamically into several steps, forming a **Born-Haber cycle**-style analytical framework:

1. **Atomization Enthalpy ($\Delta H_{\text{atom}}$)** : Energy required to convert solid metal to gaseous atoms
2. **Ionization Energy ($IE$)** : Energy required to remove $n$ electrons from gaseous atoms ($IE_1 + IE_2 + ... + IE_n$)
3. **Hydration Enthalpy ($\Delta H_{\text{hyd}}$)** : Energy released when gaseous cations combine with water molecules to form hydrated ions

The sum of these three energy contributions determines the overall tendency for electron loss:

$$\text{Overall tendency} = f(\text{low } \Delta H_{\text{atom}}, \text{low } IE, \text{high } |\Delta H_{\text{hyd}}|)$$

- **Alkali metals (especially below K)**: Low $\Delta H_{\text{atom}}$ (weak metallic bonding), low $IE$ (only one valence electron, far from nucleus), large $\Delta H_{\text{hyd}}$ (small ions) → **extremely high reactivity**
- **Transition metals (e.g., Fe, Zn)**: High $\Delta H_{\text{atom}}$ (d-electrons participate in bonding), multiple electrons must be removed (high total ionization energy) → **low to moderate reactivity**
- **Noble metals (Cu, Ag, Au)**: High $\Delta H_{\text{atom}}$, high $IE$, small $\Delta H_{\text{hyd}}$ (large ions) → **total energy change unfavorable for cation formation** → extremely low reactivity

### 9.4.4 Aluminum's "Apparent Unreactivity" and Its Essence
