Aluminum sits between magnesium and zinc in the reactivity series, making it a fairly reactive metal. Yet in everyday experience, we observe that aluminum does not appear to react noticeably with water or dilute acids—we can even cook with aluminum pots without them corroding.

This **apparent unreactivity** originates from the **dense protective aluminum oxide ($\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$) layer** on the aluminum surface.

The mechanism of formation:

$$4\text{Al}(s) + 3\text{O}_2(g) \rightarrow 2\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3(s)$$

When fresh aluminum is exposed to air, a layer of $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$ approximately 4 nm thick forms **instantly**. This layer has the following critical characteristics:
1. **Extremely thin**—visible light can pass through, so aluminum still appears to have a metallic luster
2. **Dense and non-porous**—O₂ and H₂O molecules cannot diffuse through
3. **Strongly adherent to the substrate**—does not flake off
4. **Self-repairing**—if the surface is scratched, the exposed fresh aluminum immediately forms a new oxide layer
5. **Chemically inert**—does not react with water, most acids (except strong acids like HCl that can dissolve it), or common atmospheric substances

This oxide layer gives aluminum excellent **practical corrosion resistance**, despite being thermodynamically quite reactive. Only when this oxide layer is compromised (e.g., by treating with HgCl₂ to form an aluminum amalgam, or by reaction with strong alkali) does aluminum reveal its true reactive nature.

---

## 9.5 Corrosion of Metals and Its Prevention

### 9.5.1 The Electrochemical Mechanism of Rusting

**Rusting** specifically refers to the corrosion of iron and steel, which is fundamentally different from corrosion of other metals (such as copper turning green or aluminum forming an oxide layer). Rust has a loose, porous, water-absorbing structure that **cannot protect the underlying metal**; instead, it continuously absorbs moisture and oxygen, allowing corrosion to spread progressively inward.

Rusting is an **electrochemical process** that requires the establishment of a **galvanic cell** on the iron surface:

When **oxygen** and **water** are simultaneously present on an iron surface, the following reactions occur:

**Anodic region (iron loses electrons, oxidation occurs)** :

$$\text{Fe}(s) \rightarrow \text{Fe}^{2+}(aq) + 2e^-$$

**Cathodic region (oxygen gains electrons, reduction occurs)** :

$$\text{O}_2(g) + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) + 4e^- \rightarrow 4\text{OH}^-(aq)$$

**Subsequent reactions**:

$$\text{Fe}^{2+}(aq) + 2\text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{Fe}(\text{OH})_2(s)$$

$$\text{Fe}(\text{OH})_2(s) + \text{O}_2(g) \rightarrow \text{Fe}_2\text{O}_3 \cdot x\text{H}_2\text{O}(s) \text{ (rust)}$$

The final chemical formula of rust can be written as $\text{Fe}_2\text{O}_3 \cdot x\text{H}_2\text{O}$, where $x$ is variable (dependent on environmental humidity), typically between 1 and 3.

Rusting requires **three conditions simultaneously**:
1. **Iron** (or iron-containing steel)
2. **Oxygen** (from air)
3. **Water** (liquid water or water vapor in high-humidity environments)

None can be omitted. This explains why iron objects can remain rust-free for millennia in completely dry desert environments, and why iron fully immersed in deoxygenated water does not rust either.

### 9.5.2 The Principle and Limitations of Barrier Methods

**Barrier Methods** involve covering the iron surface with a protective layer that **physically excludes oxygen and water**. These are the most common and economical anti-rust methods.

Common barrier coatings:
- **Paint/Coating**: The most widely used, but once scratched or peeled, the exposed iron corrodes more rapidly (because the scratch becomes a small anodic region, while the surrounding intact painted area becomes a large cathodic region—corrosion concentrates at the scratch)
- **Grease/Oil**: Commonly used for moving mechanical parts, but attracts dust
- **Plastic Coating**: e.g., PVC coating on handles—good durability
- **Plating with other metals** (e.g., tin-plating/"tinplate"): Very effective as long as the coating is intact; but once damaged, the exposed iron will corrode

The fundamental limitation of barrier methods: **Once the barrier layer is damaged, protection is immediately lost, and the damaged area may experience accelerated corrosion due to electrochemical effects.**

### 9.5.3 The Electrochemical Principle of Galvanizing and Sacrificial Protection

**Galvanizing** is a method of coating iron with a layer of zinc. It combines **barrier protection** and **electrochemical protection**—making it one of the most effective rust-prevention methods available.

**Barrier Effect**: The zinc layer itself physically excludes oxygen and water.

**Sacrificial Protection**: Even if the zinc layer is scratched, exposing the underlying iron, the zinc still protects the iron. The reason is that zinc is **above iron in the reactivity series** (Zn is more reactive than Fe), so:

$$\text{Zn}(s) \rightarrow \text{Zn}^{2+}(aq) + 2e^-$$

Zinc preferentially loses electrons, becoming the target of corrosion instead of iron. The $\text{Zn}^{2+}$ produced dissolves in water, while the electrons flow to the iron surface, making the iron the **cathode** and thus protected. The products of zinc corrosion ($\text{Zn}(\text{OH})_2$ and $\text{ZnCO}_3$) also form a protective layer.

This is the essence of **Sacrificial Protection**: using a more reactive metal (called a "sacrificial anode") to protect a less reactive metal. Zinc is the ideal sacrificial metal because:
1. It is more reactive than iron and can donate electrons to protect iron
2. Its own corrosion rate is moderate—it does not deplete too quickly
3. Zinc is relatively inexpensive
4. Zinc forms a protective basic zinc carbonate layer in air, keeping its corrosion rate manageable

### 9.5.4 The Central Role of the Reactivity Series in Corrosion Prevention

All electrochemical corrosion prevention methods are theoretically based on the **reactivity series**:

- **Zinc protects iron**: Zn is above Fe in the reactivity series → Zn corrodes preferentially → Fe is protected
- **Magnesium protects iron**: Mg is even more reactive than Zn, providing stronger protection in extreme environments (e.g., ships' hulls in seawater, underground pipelines)
- **Tin cannot protect iron**: Sn is below Fe in the reactivity series (Sn is less reactive than Fe), so when tin-plated iron (tinplate) is damaged, the iron **corrodes faster** (Fe becomes the anode, Sn becomes the cathode)

This reveals the practical value of the reactivity series in materials science: **The choice of which metal to use as a protective layer depends on the protected metal's position in the series.**

---

## 9.6 Extraction of Metals

### 9.6.1 The Correspondence Between Extraction Method and the Reactivity Series

The form in which a metal occurs naturally and the method by which it is extracted depend entirely on its position in the reactivity series:

| Reactivity | Natural Form | Extraction Method | Example |
|------------|-------------|------------------|---------|
| Very low (Au, Ag) | Native (elemental form) | Physical separation | Gold panning |
| Low (Cu, Hg) | Compounds (sulfides, oxides) | Heating/roasting reduction | Copper smelting |
| Moderate (Zn, Fe, Pb) | Compounds (oxides, carbonates) | **Reduction with carbon** | Blast furnace (iron) |
| High (Mg, Al) | Compounds (oxides, chlorides) | **Electrolysis of molten compounds** | Aluminum smelting |
| Very high (Na, K, Ca) | Compounds (chlorides, carbonates) | **Electrolysis of molten compounds** | Sodium from molten NaCl |

The essence of this correspondence is **energy**. The more reactive a metal, the more stable its compounds, and the more energy is required to reduce them to the elemental state. Carbon reduction (chemical reduction) obtains its energy from burning carbon; electrolysis uses electrical energy, which can provide a much greater energy input.

### 9.6.2 Blast Furnace Iron Extraction — Detailed Principles of Carbon Reduction

Blast furnace ironmaking is a complex gas-solid reaction system involving multiple simultaneous thermochemical processes.

**Overview of Blast Furnace Structure**: A blast furnace is a vertical shaft tens of meters high, divided from top to bottom into throat, stack, belly, bosh, and hearth. Raw materials (iron ore, coke, limestone) are added from the top, while hot air is blasted in from the bottom, creating a counter-current reaction system.

**Detailed Analysis of Each Step**:

**(1) Coke Combustion (providing heat and reductant)**

Preheated hot air is blown in from the bottom of the blast furnace, and coke (C) burns:

$$\text{C}(s) + \text{O}_2(g) \rightarrow \text{CO}_2(g) \quad \Delta H < 0 \text{ (strongly exothermic)}$$

This reaction provides all the thermal energy for the entire blast furnace, bringing the temperature at the hearth to approximately 1900°C.

**(2) Generation of Carbon Monoxide**

The $\text{CO}_2$ produced rises and encounters the red-hot coke layer above, undergoing the **Boudouard reaction**:

$$\text{C}(s) + \text{CO}_2(g) \rightarrow 2\text{CO}(g) \quad \Delta H > 0 \text{ (endothermic)}$$

This reaction converts $\text{CO}_2$ to CO, and CO is the actual species that reduces the iron ore.

**(3) Reduction of Iron Oxides**

Iron ore (mainly $\text{Fe}_2\text{O}_3$, hematite) enters from the top and, during its descent, undergoes a series of stepwise reductions:

Step 1 (approx. 500–700°C):

$$3\text{Fe}_2\text{O}_3 + \text{CO} \rightarrow 2\text{Fe}_3\text{O}_4 + \text{CO}_2$$

Step 2 (approx. 700–900°C):

$$\text{Fe}_3\text{O}_4 + \text{CO} \rightarrow 3\text{FeO} + \text{CO}_2$$

Step 3 (approx. 900–1200°C):

$$\text{FeO} + \text{CO} \rightarrow \text{Fe} + \text{CO}_2$$

The overall net reaction:

$$\text{Fe}_2\text{O}_3 + 3\text{CO} \rightarrow 2\text{Fe} + 3\text{CO}_2$$

The reduced iron is initially solid, but at high temperatures it absorbs carbon to form **pig iron** (containing about 4% carbon) with a lower melting point, which melts into liquid iron at the hearth.

**(4) Decomposition of Limestone and Slag Formation**

Iron ore typically contains $\text{SiO}_2$ (silica/sand) as an impurity, which would contaminate the iron if not removed.

Limestone ($\text{CaCO}_3$) decomposes upon heating in the furnace:

$$\text{CaCO}_3(s) \xrightarrow{\Delta} \text{CaO}(s) + \text{CO}_2(g) \quad \Delta H > 0 \text{ (endothermic)}$$

The $\text{CaO}$ (quicklime) produced then reacts with $\text{SiO}_2$:

$$\text{CaO}(s) + \text{SiO}_2(s) \rightarrow \text{CaSiO}_3(l) \text{ (calcium silicate slag)}$$

$\text{CaSiO}_3$ has a melting point below the operating temperature of the blast furnace, so it exists as a liquid. Its density is lower than molten iron, so it floats on top of the iron and is tapped off separately. This is **slag**.

Uses of slag: Although it is a "waste product" of ironmaking, slag can be used in cement production, road fill, and other applications.

### 9.6.3 Electrolytic Extraction of Aluminum — Energy and Material Challenges

Aluminum is the most abundant metallic element in the Earth's crust (about 8% of soil), yet before the Hall-Héroult process was developed in 1886, aluminum was more expensive than gold. The core difficulty is: **aluminum is too reactive to be reduced from its oxide by carbon.**

**Ore**: The main ore of aluminum is **bauxite**, with the main component being $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3 \cdot x\text{H}_2\text{O}$ (hydrated aluminum oxide). Bauxite typically contains $\text{Fe}_2\text{O}_3$ and $\text{SiO}_2$ as impurities.

**Purification**: Bauxite is first purified via the **Bayer Process** to obtain pure $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$ (alumina).

**The Role of Cryolite**: Pure $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$ has an extremely high melting point (2072°C), making direct molten electrolysis energetically unfeasible. Therefore, $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$ is dissolved in molten **cryolite ($\text{Na}_3\text{AlF}_6$)** to form a molten mixture. This mixture:
1. Has a greatly reduced melting point (operational at about 950°C)
2. Has good electrical conductivity
3. Has good dissolving power for $\text{Al}_2\text{O}_3$
4. Does not undergo side reactions with aluminum or the electrolysis products

**Electrode Reactions**:

Cathode (negative, steel shell lined with carbon blocks):

$$\text{Al}^{3+}(l) + 3e^- \rightarrow \text{Al}(l)$$

Molten aluminum, being denser than the electrolyte, collects at the bottom of the electrolysis cell and is removed periodically.

Anode (positive, carbon rods):

$$2\text{O}^{2-}(l) \rightarrow \text{O}_2(g) + 4e^-$$

**Consumption of Anode Carbon Rods**: The $\text{O}_2$ produced reacts with the carbon anodes at high temperature:

$$\text{C}(s) + \text{O}_2(g) \rightarrow \text{CO}_2(g)$$

Therefore, the carbon anodes are **continuously consumed** and must be replaced regularly. This is a major cost factor in aluminum smelting.

**Energy Consumption**: Electrolyzing 1 kg of aluminum requires approximately 13–15 kWh of electrical energy. Consequently, aluminum smelters are typically located near sources of abundant, cheap electricity (such as hydroelectric power stations).

### 9.6.4 The Thermodynamic Link Between Metal Extraction and the Reactivity Series

Carbon ($\text{C}$) and hydrogen ($\text{H}_2$) can serve as reducing agents, but their ability to reduce metals is limited. This limitation can be understood using the **Ellingham Diagram**, which plots the Gibbs free energy change ($\Delta G$) of various metal oxidation reactions as a function of temperature.

According to thermodynamics:
- Only reduction reactions with $\Delta G < 0$ can occur spontaneously
- The $\Delta G$ for carbon's combination with oxygen becomes increasingly negative at high temperatures, allowing carbon to reduce metals below it in the reactivity series
- Carbon cannot reduce metals above it (such as Al, Mg, Na) because those metals bind oxygen more tightly than carbon does

**General Principle**: In the reactivity series, **any substance can reduce the metals below it from their compounds**. Carbon's position in the series allows it to reduce Zn, Fe, Cu, and other moderately to weakly reactive metals, but not the highly reactive metals above Al.

---

> **Conclusion**: The central thread running through Topic 8 and Topic 9 is that **electrons (especially the behavior of valence electrons) govern everything**. An element's position in the periodic table is determined by its electron configuration; its chemical properties are determined by the ease with which it loses or gains electrons; the essence of metallic bonding is electron delocalization; the reactivity series reflects a metal's tendency to lose electrons; corrosion is the flow of electrons; and extraction is about applying external energy to reclaim electrons. Mastering this thread transforms the periodic table and metal chemistry from scattered pieces of knowledge into a logically self-consistent framework.
> ---
> ---
> # TOPIC 10: Chemistry of the Environment (In-Depth Expansion)

---

## 10.1 Water

### 10.1.1 The Chemical Nature and Molecular Structure of Water

Water is the most common substance on Earth, yet its chemical properties are exceptionally unique. A water molecule consists of two hydrogen atoms and one oxygen atom joined by **polar covalent bonds**, with a bond angle of approximately $104.5^\circ$, forming a **V-shaped (bent) molecular geometry**.

$$\text{H}—\text{O}—\text{H} \quad \text{Bond angle} \approx 104.5^\circ$$

The origin of water's polarity lies in **electronegativity differences**: the electronegativity of oxygen (approximately 3.44) is much greater than that of hydrogen (approximately 2.20). Therefore, in the O—H covalent bond, the shared electron pair is **pulled** towards the oxygen atom, giving the oxygen atom a partial negative charge ($\delta^-$) and each hydrogen atom a partial positive charge ($\delta^+$). This uneven charge distribution makes water a **polar molecule**.

More importantly, **hydrogen bonding** exists between water molecules — a **special type of intermolecular force** much stronger than ordinary van der Waals forces. The $\delta^+$ hydrogen atom of one water molecule experiences electrostatic attraction to the $\delta^-$ oxygen atom of a neighbouring water molecule. It is the presence of hydrogen bonds that gives water its many anomalous physical properties:
- Water's melting point (0°C) and boiling point (100°C) are far higher than those of its Group VI hydrides (e.g., $\text{H}_2\text{S}$ boils at only $-60^\circ\text{C}$)
- Water has an **exceptionally high specific heat capacity** (approximately $4.18 \text{ J/g}·\text{K}$), making it an excellent thermal buffer
- Ice is **less dense** than liquid water (ice floats), because the hydrogen-bonded crystal lattice of ice leaves relatively large gaps between molecules

### 10.1.2 Testing for Water — The Principles of Chemical Methods

The syllabus requires two chemical tests for water, both based on **hydration reactions** that produce **colour changes**.

**Method 1: Anhydrous Copper(II) Sulfate**

Anhydrous copper(II) sulfate is a white powder with the chemical formula $\text{CuSO}_4$ (anhydrous form). When it comes into contact with water, it absorbs water molecules to form **blue hydrated copper(II) sulfate crystals**:

$$\text{CuSO}_4(s) + 5\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightarrow \text{CuSO}_4 \cdot 5\text{H}_2\text{O}(s)$$
$$\text{White} \rightarrow \text{Blue}$$

The microscopic essence of the colour change: In anhydrous $\text{CuSO}_4$, the electron configuration of the $\text{Cu}^{2+}$ ion determines its light absorption properties, appearing white. When water molecules coordinate with the $\text{Cu}^{2+}$ ion to form the $[\text{Cu}(\text{H}_2\text{O})_4]^{2+}$ complex ion, the ligand field alters the d-orbital energy-level splitting of $\text{Cu}^{2+}$, causing it to selectively absorb orange-red light and reflect blue light — hence the blue appearance.

**Method 2: Anhydrous Cobalt(II) Chloride**

Anhydrous cobalt(II) chloride is a blue solid (or blue test paper). Upon contact with water, a hydration reaction occurs:

$$\text{CoCl}_2(s) + 6\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightarrow \text{CoCl}_2 \cdot 6\text{H}_2\text{O}(s)$$
$$\text{Blue} \rightarrow \text{Pink}$$

Again, this is a colour change caused by a change in the coordination environment of the $\text{Co}^{2+}$ ion. In the anhydrous state, the $\text{Co}^{2+}$ ion's coordination environment appears blue. When six water molecules coordinate with $\text{Co}^{2+}$ to form $[\text{Co}(\text{H}_2\text{O})_6]^{2+}$, the complex appears pink. This reaction is **reversible** — heating the hydrated cobalt chloride drives off the water molecules, and the colour changes back from pink to blue:

$$\text{CoCl}_2 \cdot 6\text{H}_2\text{O}(s) \xrightarrow{\Delta} \text{CoCl}_2(s) + 6\text{H}_2\text{O}(g)$$

Thus, both substances serve as indicators for water and can also be used to detect hydrated/anhydrous states.

### 10.1.3 Testing the Purity of Water

**Melting point and boiling point** are physical criteria for judging the purity of a substance. A pure substance has a **definite and sharp** melting point and boiling point. For pure water:
- **Melting Point**: $0^\circ\text{C}$ (the temperature of an ice-water mixture stays constant at 0°C until all ice has melted)
- **Boiling Point**: $100^\circ\text{C}$ (at standard atmospheric pressure)

When water contains impurities, **Freezing Point Depression** and **Boiling Point Elevation** occur. Impurities disrupt the ordered crystal lattice formation, requiring a lower temperature for the water to freeze. At the same time, impurities lower the vapour pressure of the solvent (water), requiring a higher temperature for the solution's vapour pressure to equal the external pressure (i.e., its boiling point).

The thermodynamic principle behind this can be understood via **Raoult's Law**:

$$P_{\text{solution}} = X_{\text{solvent}} \times P^\circ_{\text{solvent}}$$

where $P_{\text{solution}}$ is the vapour pressure of the solution, $X_{\text{solvent}}$ is the mole fraction of the solvent, and $P^\circ_{\text{solvent}}$ is the vapour pressure of the pure solvent. When a solute is present, $X_{\text{solvent}} < 1$, so $P_{\text{solution}} < P^\circ_{\text{solvent}}$, and the solution requires a higher temperature to reach its boiling point.

Therefore, by measuring the melting point and boiling point of water — if the melting point is below 0°C or the boiling point is above 100°C — the water can be judged to contain impurities and is not pure.

### 10.1.4 Distilled Water vs Tap Water

Distilled water is water purified through the process of **distillation**. The principle of distillation is based on differences in boiling points: water is heated to become water vapour (evaporation), and the vapour is then cooled to recondense into liquid water. During this process, non-volatile impurities in the water (such as dissolved minerals, metal ions, microorganisms, etc.) remain in the distillation flask, while pure water vapour is collected as condensate. Therefore, distilled water contains **virtually no dissolved chemical impurities**.

The reasons distilled water is preferable to tap water in chemical experiments include:
1. **Avoiding interference with reactions**: Ions in tap water such as $\text{Ca}^{2+}$, $\text{Mg}^{2+}$, $\text{Cl}^-$, and $\text{HCO}_3^-$ may cause unintended side reactions with reagents
2. **Ensuring reproducibility of results**: The composition of tap water can vary by location and time of collection; using distilled water eliminates this variable
3. **Titration accuracy**: Tap water may contain impurities that react with the titrant

### 10.1.5 Substances in Natural Water — Sources and Impacts

Natural water is not pure water but a complex mixture of many substances. Each substance has its source and exerts **beneficial** or **harmful** dual impacts on ecosystems.

**(1) Dissolved Oxygen (DO)**

Oxygen molecules dissolved in water mainly come from:
- **Atmospheric oxygen** dissolving into water across the water surface
- **Photosynthesis by aquatic plants** releasing oxygen

Dissolved oxygen is **essential** for aquatic ecosystems: aquatic animals (fish, crustaceans, etc.) use dissolved oxygen for respiration through their gills. When dissolved oxygen levels fall below $2 \text{ mg/L}$, most fish cannot survive, leading to **fish kills**.

**(2) Metal Compounds**

Metal compounds originate from rock weathering, mineral dissolution, and industrial discharges. Certain metal ions (such as $\text{Fe}^{2+}, \text{Zn}^{2+}, \text{Mg}^{2+}$) are **essential trace elements** for enzymes in aquatic organisms and are **beneficial** for sustaining life. However, **heavy metal ions** (such as $\text{Pb}^{2+}, \text{Hg}^{2+}, \text{Cd}^{2+}$) are **highly toxic** even at very low concentrations, because these heavy metal ions can bind to the thiol groups ($-\text{SH}$) in proteins and enzymes within organisms, causing enzyme deactivation. This poses serious health risks to aquatic life and to humans through drinking water or the food chain.

**(3) Plastics**

Plastics are synthetic high-molecular-weight polymers that enter water bodies and break down into **microplastics** — particles smaller than 5 mm in diameter. Microplastics can be ingested by aquatic organisms, accumulating in their digestive tracts and causing physical damage. At the same time, the surface of microplastics can adsorb toxic pollutants from the water, which then undergo **biomagnification** along the **food chain**, ultimately endangering the health of humans at the top of the food chain.

**(4) Sewage**

Domestic sewage contains organic matter, microorganisms, and other substances. When untreated sewage is discharged into water bodies, **harmful microbes** (such as bacteria, viruses, and parasites) can cause **waterborne diseases** (such as cholera, typhoid, and dysentery). Furthermore, organic matter in sewage is decomposed by microorganisms, a process that consumes large amounts of dissolved oxygen, leading to **oxygen depletion**.

**(5) Nitrates and Phosphates**

Nitrates ($\text{NO}_3^-$) mainly come from agricultural **fertilisers**, while phosphates ($\text{PO}_4^{3-}$) come from fertilisers and **detergents**. Both enter rivers and lakes via surface runoff during rainfall. Although they are essential nutrients for plant growth, excessive input into water bodies triggers a serious ecological problem — **eutrophication**. However, the syllabus explicitly states that **details of the eutrophication process are not required**.

Among these substances, nitrates and phosphates are **beneficial** for plant growth but **harmful** in excess; dissolved oxygen and beneficial metal compounds are **beneficial** for maintaining ecology; while plastics, microbial contaminants in sewage, and toxic heavy metals are **harmful**.

### 10.1.6 Domestic Water Treatment — A Multi-Step Purification Process

Water from natural sources must undergo a series of physical and chemical treatment steps to become safe drinking water. Each step serves a specific purpose.

**Step 1: Sedimentation and Filtration**

**Purpose**: **Removal of suspended solids**

Water is directed into settling tanks where the flow velocity is reduced, allowing larger suspended particles (sediment, debris, etc.) to settle to the bottom by gravity. The water then passes through **sand filters** (layers of sand and gravel with different particle sizes) which trap finer suspended particles. This is a **physical separation** process.

**Step 2: Carbon Treatment**

**Purpose**: **Removal of tastes and odours**

Water passes through a bed of **Activated Carbon**. Activated carbon has an extremely large **specific surface area** (up to 500–1500 m² per gram), and its extensive network of micropores adsorbs organic molecules, residual chlorine, and odour-causing compounds through **physical adsorption** — a surface phenomenon, not a chemical reaction.

**Step 3: Chlorination**

**Purpose**: **Killing harmful microorganisms**

Chlorine gas ($\text{Cl}_2$) or sodium hypochlorite ($\text{NaClO}$) is added to the water. Chlorine reacts with water to form **hypochlorous acid**:

$$\text{Cl}_2 + \text{H}_2\text{O} \rightarrow \text{HCl} + \text{HOCl}$$

Hypochlorous acid ($\text{HOCl}$) is a powerful oxidising agent that can penetrate microbial cell walls, destroy their enzyme systems and genetic material, thereby killing bacteria, viruses, and other pathogens. This is a **chemical disinfection** process that ensures the microbiological safety of drinking water.

---

## 10.2 Fertilisers

### 10.2.1 Essential Elements for Plant Growth

Plants require various chemical elements for growth, with the three most important **macronutrients** being:
- **Nitrogen (N)**: A core component of **proteins, chlorophyll, and nucleic acids (DNA/RNA)**. Nitrogen deficiency causes yellowing of leaves and stunted growth.
- **Phosphorus (P)**: A key element in **ATP (the energy currency), nucleic acids, and phospholipids** of cell membranes. It promotes root development, and flower and fruit formation.
- **Potassium (K)**: Activates many enzymes, regulates water balance and stomatal opening/closing, and enhances disease resistance.

### 10.2.2 Types of Chemical Fertilisers

**Ammonium salts and nitrates** are the most commonly used nitrogen fertilisers because they provide **soluble nitrogen sources** that plants can absorb directly.

Ammonium salts (e.g., $\text{NH}_4\text{Cl}, (\text{NH}_4)_2\text{SO}_4$) supply $\text{NH}_4^+$ ions, which plant roots can absorb via active transport.

Nitrates (e.g., $\text{NaNO}_3, \text{NH}_4\text{NO}_3$) supply $\text{NO}_3^-$ ions, another form of nitrogen absorbable by plants.

**Ammonium nitrate ($\text{NH}_4\text{NO}_3$)** is one of the most ideal nitrogen fertilisers because it simultaneously provides two forms of nitrogen — ammonium nitrogen ($\text{NH}_4^+$) and nitrate nitrogen ($\text{NO}_3^-$) — with a nitrogen content as high as 35%.

### 10.2.3 NPK Fertilisers

**NPK fertilisers** are **compound fertilisers** containing all three elements: **N** (nitrogen), **P** (phosphorus), and **K** (potassium). Since these three elements are often simultaneously deficient in soil, compound fertilisers can supplement all essential plant nutrients in a single application.

- **N** sources: ammonium salts, nitrates, urea, etc.
- **P** sources: phosphates (e.g., calcium phosphate, ammonium phosphate)
- **K** sources: potassium salts (e.g., potassium chloride $\text{KCl}$, potassium sulfate $\text{K}_2\text{SO}_4$)

NPK fertiliser packaging is typically labelled with three numbers, such as **15-15-15**, representing the percentage content of N, P₂O₅, and K₂O respectively.

### 10.2.4 Environmental Issues with Fertilisers

Although fertilisers are crucial for increasing crop yields, overuse leads to environmental problems. Excess nitrates and phosphates enter water bodies via rainfall runoff, triggering **eutrophication**, which causes algal blooms, oxygen depletion, aquatic organism death, and other cascading effects. Additionally, nitrates can leach into groundwater, contaminating drinking water sources and posing health risks to humans (such as blue baby syndrome in infants).

---

## 10.3 Air Quality and Climate

### 10.3.1 Composition of Clean, Dry Air

The atmosphere is a mixture of many gases. The composition of **clean, dry air** (standard atmosphere without water vapour and pollutants) is fixed:

| Gas | Percentage by Volume | Formula |
|-----|:-------------------:|:-------:|
| Nitrogen | $\approx 78\%$ | $\text{N}_2$ |
| Oxygen | $\approx 21\%$ | $\text{O}_2$ |
| Argon | $\approx 0.9\%$ | Ar |
| Carbon dioxide | $\approx 0.04\%$ | $\text{CO}_2$ |
| Other noble gases | Trace | Ne, He, Kr, Xe |

Among these, oxygen ($\text{O}_2$) is the most crucial reactive component, supporting combustion and aerobic respiration.

### 10.3.2 Air Pollutants — Sources and Chemical Mechanisms

The syllabus requires knowledge of six major air pollutants. The **formation mechanisms** and **environmental effects** of each are described in detail below:

---

#### (1) Carbon Dioxide ($\text{CO}_2$)

**Formation Mechanism — Complete Combustion**

When hydrocarbons (the main components of fossil fuels) are burned with a sufficient supply of oxygen, **complete combustion** occurs:

$$\text{C}_x\text{H}_y + \left(x + \frac{y}{4}\right)\text{O}_2 \rightarrow x\text{CO}_2 + \frac{y}{2}\text{H}_2\text{O}$$

For example, the complete combustion of methane:

$$\text{CH}_4 + 2\text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{CO}_2 + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}$$

**Environmental Effect — Global Warming**

$\text{CO}_2$ is a major **greenhouse gas**. It allows short-wave radiation from the Sun (visible light) to penetrate the atmosphere and reach the Earth's surface, but it **absorbs** the **long-wave infrared radiation (heat)** emitted by the Earth's surface, re-radiating some of this heat back towards the ground, thereby preventing heat from escaping into space. This is the **Greenhouse Effect**. A moderate greenhouse effect keeps the Earth at a habitable temperature (approximately 15°C average), but human activities have caused a sharp rise in $\text{CO}_2$ concentrations, intensifying the greenhouse effect and leading to global average temperature increases, which in turn trigger **Climate Change** — including more frequent extreme weather events, glacial melting, and sea-level rise.

---

#### (2) Carbon Monoxide ($\text{CO}$) and Particulates

**Formation Mechanism — Incomplete Combustion**

When the oxygen supply is insufficient, hydrocarbons cannot be fully oxidised. There are not enough oxygen atoms to convert all carbon into $\text{CO}_2$, so **carbon monoxide ($\text{CO}$)** and **carbon particles / soot (particulates)** are produced:

$$\text{CH}_4 + \frac{3}{2}\text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{CO} + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}$$

More generally, for carbon-rich fuels such as coal:

$$\text{C} + \frac{1}{2}\text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{CO}$$

$\text{CO}$ is a **colourless, odourless, toxic** gas. Its toxicity mechanism is: $\text{CO}$ binds to **haemoglobin** in the blood approximately **250 times more strongly** than oxygen does. Once $\text{CO}$ binds to haemoglobin to form **carboxyhaemoglobin**, it occupies the sites that should carry $\text{O}_2$, drastically **reducing the blood's oxygen-carrying capacity**, leading to oxygen deprivation in tissues and organs. Mild poisoning causes headaches and dizziness; severe poisoning can be fatal within minutes.

**Particulates** are unburned carbon particles (soot), extremely small in size (PM10 and PM2.5 refer to particles with diameters $\leq 10 \mu\text{m}$ and $\leq 2.5 \mu\text{m}$, respectively). These fine particles can penetrate deep into the lungs, **increasing the risk of respiratory diseases (such as asthma, bronchitis) and lung cancer**.

---

#### (3) Methane ($\text{CH}_4$)

**Formation Mechanism — Biological Decomposition**

Methane is the **main component of natural gas**. Atmospheric methane mainly comes from the decomposition of organic matter in **anaerobic (oxygen-free)** environments:
- **Wetlands and swamps**: Plant remains are decomposed by methanogenic bacteria under oxygen-depleted water
- **Livestock farming**: Microbial fermentation in the digestive systems of ruminant animals (cows, sheep, etc.) produces methane (released via belching and flatulence)
- **Landfill sites**: Organic waste decomposes under oxygen-deficient conditions

**Environmental Effect — Potent Greenhouse Gas**

The greenhouse effect intensity (Global Warming Potential, GWP) of methane is approximately **25 times that of $\text{CO}_2$**. Although the atmospheric concentration of methane is far lower than that of $\text{CO}_2$, its extremely strong infrared absorption capacity makes its contribution to the greenhouse effect non-negligible.

---

#### (4) Oxides of Nitrogen ($\text{NO}_x$)

**Formation Mechanism — High-Temperature Fixation**

Inside car engines, temperatures can reach **over 2500°C**. At such high temperatures, the stable triple bond in atmospheric **nitrogen ($\text{N}_2$)** is broken, and it reacts with **oxygen ($\text{O}_2$)**:

$$\text{N}_2(g) + \text{O}_2(g) \rightarrow 2\text{NO}(g)$$

The nitrogen monoxide ($\text{NO}$) produced is rapidly further oxidised to nitrogen dioxide ($\text{NO}_2$) in the atmosphere:

$$2\text{NO}(g) + \text{O}_2(g) \rightarrow 2\text{NO}_2(g)$$

This is why car exhaust contains $\text{NO}_x$ (the collective term for $\text{NO}$ and $\text{NO}_2$). This is a **non-combustion process** — it is the direct combination of nitrogen and oxygen at high temperatures. The same reaction occurs naturally during lightning strikes.

**Environmental Effects — Multiple and Severe**:

**Effect 1: Acid Rain**

$\text{NO}_2$ reacts with water vapour in the atmosphere to form **nitric acid**:

$$4\text{NO}_2 + 2\text{H}_2\text{O} + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow 4\text{HNO}_3$$

Nitric acid falls with rain as a component of **acid rain**. Acid rain can have a pH as low as 3–4, causing severe damage to buildings (erosion of marble and limestone), soil (acidification, leaching of nutrients), forests (leaf damage), and water bodies (fish death).

**Effect 2: Photochemical Smog**

Under intense **ultraviolet** light, $\text{NO}_x$ reacts with volatile organic compounds to produce **ozone ($\text{O}_3$)** and other harmful photochemical oxidants, forming a pale blue haze known as **photochemical smog**, which irritates the eyes and respiratory tract.

**Effect 3: Respiratory Problems**

$\text{NO}_2$ itself is an irritant gas that can trigger or worsen asthma, bronchitis, and other respiratory diseases.

---

#### (5) Sulfur Dioxide ($\text{SO}_2$)

**Formation Mechanism — Combustion of Sulfur-Containing Fuels**

Coal and oil naturally contain sulfur (in the form of organic sulfur or inorganic sulfide compounds). These sulfur compounds are oxidised during combustion:

$$\text{S} + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{SO}_2$$

For example, the combustion of $\text{FeS}_2$ (pyrite / iron disulfide, a common impurity in coal):

$$4\text{FeS}_2 + 11\text{O}_2 \rightarrow 2\text{Fe}_2\text{O}_3 + 8\text{SO}_2$$

**Environmental Effect — Acid Rain**

$\text{SO}_2$ is the primary source of acid rain. $\text{SO}_2$ is converted to **sulfuric acid** in the atmosphere through reaction with oxygen and water:

$$2\text{SO}_2 + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow 2\text{SO}_3$$
$$\text{SO}_3 + \text{H}_2\text{O} \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{SO}_4$$

Sulfuric acid is a strong acid, making acid rain extremely corrosive.

---

### 10.3.3 Detailed Mechanism of the Greenhouse Effect

The Supplement section of the syllabus requires understanding the **specific mechanism** by which greenhouse gases cause global warming:

The greenhouse effect is not a recent phenomenon — in fact, Earth's atmosphere has always had a greenhouse effect. It raises the Earth's average temperature from approximately $-18^\circ\text{C}$ (the theoretical value without an atmosphere) to approximately $+15^\circ\text{C}$, creating a habitable climate for life. The problem is that **human activities have greatly intensified this effect**.

The **energy transfer chain** consists of the following three steps:

**Step 1: Solar Radiation (Shortwave) Reaches Earth**

The Sun's surface temperature is approximately 5500°C, so its radiation is primarily **visible light** and **ultraviolet** — i.e., **shortwave radiation**. This shortwave radiation easily passes through greenhouse gases in the atmosphere (greenhouse gases are largely transparent to shortwave radiation), reaches the Earth's surface, and is absorbed, warming the surface.

**Step 2: Terrestrial Heat Radiation (Longwave) Radiates Towards Space**

After being heated, the Earth's surface, like any hot object, radiates energy outward. However, because the Earth's surface temperature is far lower than the Sun's (averaging about 15°C), its radiation peaks in the **infrared (IR)** range — i.e., **longwave radiation**.

**Step 3: Greenhouse Gases Absorb and Re-Radiate Heat**

The molecular structures of greenhouse gases ($\text{CO}_2, \text{CH}_4$, etc.) allow them to selectively **absorb** specific wavelengths of **infrared radiation**. After absorbing heat, these molecules are excited to higher vibrational energy levels. They then **re-emit energy in the form of infrared radiation**. Some of this radiation goes out into space, but **some is re-radiated back towards the Earth's surface**, thereby reducing the net loss of heat from Earth to space. This is the essence of the greenhouse effect — **reducing heat loss to space**.

Expressed in terms of energy balance:

$$\text{Net surface temperature} \propto \text{Absorbed solar radiation} - \text{Net heat radiation to space}$$

Increasing greenhouse gas concentrations reduces the second term on the right (net heat radiation to space), so the net effect is a rise in surface temperature.

The **vibrational modes** of a molecule determine its ability to absorb infrared radiation. $\text{CO}_2$ is a linear triatomic molecule with several vibrational modes (symmetric stretching, asymmetric stretching, and bending), whose energies correspond precisely to the wavelengths of infrared radiation. $\text{CH}_4$ has even more vibrational modes, so its single-molecule capacity to absorb infrared radiation is stronger (approximately 25 times that of $\text{CO}_2$), although its atmospheric concentration is much lower than that of $\text{CO}_2$.

### 10.3.4 Strategies for Addressing Environmental Issues

The syllabus requires an understanding of strategies for addressing different environmental issues and their underlying chemical principles.

#### Strategies for Addressing Climate Change (Greenhouse Effect)

**(1) Planting Trees**

Trees absorb $\text{CO}_2$ from the atmosphere through **photosynthesis**:

$$6\text{CO}_2 + 6\text{H}_2\text{O} \xrightarrow{\text{light, chlorophyll}} \text{C}_6\text{H}_{12}\text{O}_6 + 6\text{O}_2$$

Each mole of glucose produced fixes 6 moles of $\text{CO}_2$ (approximately 264 g). Forests are therefore known as **carbon sinks** — natural systems that absorb and store carbon from the atmosphere.

**(2) Reduction in Livestock Farming**

Ruminant animals produce large amounts of methane during digestion (through enteric fermentation and manure decomposition). Reducing livestock farming can lower methane emissions. Since methane's greenhouse effect intensity is about 25 times that of $\text{CO}_2$, reducing methane is highly effective for slowing short-term climate warming.

**(3) Decreasing Use of Fossil Fuels**

The combustion of fossil fuels (coal, oil, natural gas) is the main human source of $\text{CO}_2$. Reducing their use directly reduces carbon emissions.

**(4) Increasing Use of Hydrogen and Renewable Energy**

- **Hydrogen energy**: The only product of hydrogen combustion is water — **completely carbon-free**:
  $$2\text{H}_2 + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow 2\text{H}_2\text{O}$$
  However, it should be noted that the current industrial method for producing hydrogen (steam reforming) still relies on fossil fuels and emits $\text{CO}_2$. Truly "green hydrogen" requires using renewable energy for water electrolysis.

- **Renewable energy**: Wind, solar, hydroelectric, and geothermal energy involve no combustion and therefore produce no $\text{CO}_2$. They convert naturally occurring energy flows into usable electrical or thermal energy.

#### Strategies for Addressing Acid Rain

**(1) Catalytic Converters in Vehicles**

Catalytic converters installed in vehicle exhaust systems use precious metals such as **platinum (Pt)** , **palladium (Pd)** , and **rhodium (Rh)** as catalysts. At approximately 300–500°C, they simultaneously convert three harmful gases:

- **Reducing $\text{NO}_x$**: Converting $\text{NO}$ to harmless $\text{N}_2$
  $$2\text{CO} + 2\text{NO} \xrightarrow{\text{catalyst}} 2\text{CO}_2 + \text{N}_2$$
- **Oxidising $\text{CO}$**: Converting carbon monoxide to $\text{CO}_2$
  $$2\text{CO} + \text{O}_2 \xrightarrow{\text{catalyst}} 2\text{CO}_2$$
- **Oxidising unburnt hydrocarbons**: Converting them to $\text{CO}_2$ and $\text{H}_2\text{O}$

By **simultaneously carrying out oxidation and reduction reactions**, the catalytic converter converts three major pollutants into harmless substances, making it a key technology for controlling vehicle exhaust emissions.

**(2) Using Low-Sulfur Fuels**

Through processes such as **hydrodesulfurisation**, sulfur compounds are removed from fuels during oil refining, thereby reducing $\text{SO}_2$ formation at the source. For example, the sulfur content standard for road diesel and gasoline has been reduced from several hundred ppm to below 10 ppm in modern fuels.

**(3) Flue Gas Desulfurisation (FGD)**

In the flues of coal-fired power plants, **calcium oxide ($\text{CaO}$)** is used to absorb $\text{SO}_2$:

$$\text{CaO}(s) + \text{SO}_2(g) \rightarrow \text{CaSO}_3(s)$$

The resulting **calcium sulfite ($\text{CaSO}_3$)** is further oxidised to **calcium sulfate ($\text{CaSO}_4$)** , i.e., **gypsum** — which can be used to produce plasterboard for construction, achieving resource utilisation of waste material.

### 10.3.5 Detailed Mechanism of Nitrogen Oxide Formation and Catalytic Converter Reactions

At the Supplement level, a deeper understanding of the formation of $\text{NO}_x$ inside engines and the chemistry of catalytic conversion is required.

Inside a car engine's combustion chamber, temperatures can reach as high as 2500°C. At such high temperatures, nitrogen and oxygen in the air undergo **thermal NO formation** (the Zeldovich mechanism in simplified form):

$$\text{N}_2 + \text{O} \rightarrow \text{NO} + \text{N}$$
$$\text{N} + \text{O}_2 \rightarrow \text{NO} + \text{O}$$

The $\text{N} \equiv \text{N}$ triple bond in the nitrogen molecule is extremely strong (bond energy approximately 941 kJ/mol) and highly stable at room temperature. However, at high temperatures, atomic vibrations intensify, the bond is broken, and N atoms recombine with O atoms to form NO.

The specific reactions occurring inside a catalytic converter:

$$2\text{CO} + 2\text{NO} \xrightarrow{\text{Pt, Rh}} 2\text{CO}_2 + \text{N}_2$$

In this reaction:
- **$\text{CO}$ is oxidised** to $\text{CO}_2$ (oxidation)
- **$\text{NO}$ is reduced** to $\text{N}_2$ (reduction)
- This is a **redox reaction**, with the catalyst surface providing active sites for reactant adsorption and reaction

### 10.3.6 Photosynthesis — The Key Link in the Carbon Cycle

Photosynthesis is one of the most important chemical reactions on Earth. It converts **inorganic carbon** ($\text{CO}_2$) into **organic carbon** (glucose) while releasing oxygen. This process forms the core of the **Carbon Cycle**.

Symbol equation:

$$6\text{CO}_2 + 6\text{H}_2\text{O} \xrightarrow{\text{chlorophyll, light energy}} \text{C}_6\text{H}_{12}\text{O}_6 + 6\text{O}_2$$

The essence of this reaction is the **conversion of light energy into chemical energy**. Chlorophyll molecules absorb light at specific wavelengths (mainly red and blue-violet light), elevating electrons to higher energy states. Through a series of complex enzyme-catalysed reactions (the light-dependent and light-independent stages of photosynthesis), $\text{CO}_2$ is reduced to carbohydrates.

From an energy perspective:

$$6\text{CO}_2 + 6\text{H}_2\text{O} \rightarrow \text{C}_6\text{H}_{12}\text{O}_6 + 6\text{O}_2 \quad \Delta H = +2800 \text{ kJ/mol}$$

This is a **highly endothermic** reaction, with energy supplied by sunlight.

---

# TOPIC 11: Organic Chemistry (In-Depth Expansion)

---

## 11.1 Organic Compounds: The Chemistry of Carbon

Organic chemistry is the study of **carbon compounds**. Carbon's uniqueness lies in its **bonding capacity**: the carbon atom has four electrons in its outermost shell and can form **4 covalent bonds** to achieve a stable octet configuration. More importantly, carbon atoms can form **stable C—C single bonds, C=C double bonds, and even C≡C triple bonds** with each other, and can connect into **straight chains, branched chains, and ring structures**. This **catenation** ability is the fundamental reason for the vast diversity of organic compounds.

## 11.2 Homologous Series — An In-Depth Understanding

A **homologous series** is one of the most fundamental concepts in organic chemistry. A homologous series is a family of compounds that share the **same functional group** and **same general formula**.

### 11.2.1 Characteristics of a Homologous Series

A complete homologous series exhibits all of the following characteristics, which form a logical progression from structure to properties:

**(1) Same Functional Group**

A functional group is an atom or group of atoms **that determines the chemical characteristics of the series**. For example, all alcohols contain the $-\text{OH}$ hydroxyl group, and all carboxylic acids contain the $-\text{COOH}$ carboxyl group. It is the functional group that causes all members of a series to exhibit **similar chemical properties**.

**(2) Same General Formula**

All members of a homologous series conform to a mathematical formula: $\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n+2}$ (alkanes), $\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n}$ (alkenes), etc. The general formula expresses a **quantitative relationship** between the numbers of carbon and hydrogen atoms in the molecule.

**(3) Differ by $-\text{CH}_2-$ Unit**

Adjacent members differ in composition by one $\text{CH}_2$ unit. For example, methane ($\text{CH}_4$) → ethane ($\text{C}_2\text{H}_6$) → propane ($\text{C}_3\text{H}_8$), each time adding a $-\text{CH}_2-$ unit and increasing the molecular mass by 14.

**(4) Gradual Trend in Physical Properties**

As molecular mass (i.e., carbon chain length) increases, intermolecular forces (especially **London dispersion forces**) strengthen, leading to **regular, gradual changes** in physical properties:
- Boiling points gradually increase
- Melting points gradually increase (though may show alternating patterns)
- Density gradually increases (but remains lower than water)
- State changes from gas → liquid → solid
- Water solubility gradually decreases (longer carbon chains increase hydrophobicity)

**(5) Similar Chemical Properties**

Because the **functional group is the same**, the **types and patterns of chemical reactions** are consistent. For example, all alkanes undergo combustion and substitution reactions; all alkenes undergo addition reactions. However, reaction rates may differ depending on chain length and molecular structure.

### 11.2.2 The Four Key Homologous Series — General Formulae and Functional Groups

**Alkanes**: $\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n+2}$

Alkanes are **saturated hydrocarbons**; all C—C bonds in the molecule are **single bonds**. The carbon atoms are $sp^3$ hybridised, with bond angles of approximately $109.5^\circ$ and tetrahedral molecular geometry. Since there is no functional group in the traditional sense (or one could say the C—H and C—C single bonds themselves are the functional groups), alkanes have relatively low chemical reactivity.

**Alkenes**: $\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n}$

Alkenes contain a **carbon-carbon double bond ($\text{C}=\text{C}$)** , which is their functional group. The double bond consists of one σ bond and one π bond: the σ bond is formed by head-on overlap of $sp^2$ hybrid orbitals, while the π bond is formed by sideways overlap of unhybridised p orbitals. The π bond is higher in energy and weaker in bond strength, making it easier to break — this makes alkenes **much more reactive** than alkanes, primarily undergoing **addition reactions**.

**Alcohols**: $\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n+1}\text{OH}$

The functional group of alcohols is the **hydroxyl group ($-\text{OH}$)** . The O—H bond in the hydroxyl group is relatively polar, giving alcohols some polarity and hydrogen-bonding capacity. Alcohols can undergo **combustion, oxidation, esterification**, and other reactions.

**Carboxylic Acids**: $\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n+1}\text{COOH}$

The functional group of carboxylic acids is the **carboxyl group ($-\text{COOH}$)** , which consists of a carbonyl group ($\text{C}=\text{O}$) and a hydroxyl group ($-\text{OH}$). Due to the electron-withdrawing effect of the carbonyl group, the O—H bond polarity is enhanced, making H more easily dissociable as $\text{H}^+$. Therefore, carboxylic acids exhibit **weak acidity**.

### 11.2.3 Saturated and Unsaturated

**Saturated Compound**: All carbon atoms in the molecule are connected by **single bonds** only, and each carbon atom is bonded to the maximum number of hydrogen atoms. Saturated compounds can only undergo **substitution reactions** (one atom or group replacing another).

**Unsaturated Compound**: The molecule contains at least one **carbon-carbon double or triple bond**, so the carbon atoms do not have the maximum number of hydrogen atoms. Unsaturated compounds can undergo **addition reactions** (adding atoms across the double bond, converting unsaturation to saturation).

## 11.3 Structural Formulae and Structural Isomerism

### 11.3.1 Different Types of Chemical Formulae

In organic chemistry, the same molecule can be described using different levels of chemical formulae, ranging from simple to detailed:

**Molecular Formula**: Indicates only the **total number of atoms of each element** in the molecule, e.g., $\text{C}_4\text{H}_{10}$. The molecular formula cannot distinguish between isomers.

**Structural Formula**: Describes the **unambiguous** way atoms are connected in the molecule. For example, $\text{CH}_3\text{CH}_2\text{CH}_2\text{CH}_3$ and $\text{CH}_3\text{CH}(\text{CH}_3)\text{CH}_3$. Structural formulae can distinguish between isomers.

**Displayed Formula**: Shows all atoms and all bonds, displaying **every atom and every covalent bond** in the molecule as a complete diagram.

### 11.3.2 Structural Isomerism — In-Depth Principles

**Structural Isomers**: Compounds that have the **same molecular formula but different structural formulae**. Isomerism exists because carbon atoms can be connected in different ways.

Isomerism stems from a core feature of organic chemistry — carbon atoms can form chains in multiple patterns.

**Alkane Isomerism**: For $\text{C}_4\text{H}_{10}$, the carbon skeleton can be arranged in two ways:

Type 1 — **Straight Chain (Unbranched)** : Four carbon atoms linked in a straight line:

$$\text{CH}_3-\text{CH}_2-\text{CH}_2-\text{CH}_3 \quad \text{Butane}$$

Type 2 — **Branched Chain**: Three carbon atoms form the main chain, with one carbon atom as a branch attached to the middle carbon:

$$\text{CH}_3-\text{CH}(\text{CH}_3)-\text{CH}_3 \quad \text{2-Methylpropane}$$

Both have the molecular formula $\text{C}_4\text{H}_{10}$, but they differ in physical properties such as boiling point (the straight-chain isomer has a higher boiling point because the molecules have a larger contact area and stronger dispersion forces).

**Alkene Isomerism**: For $\text{C}_4\text{H}_8$, the situation is more complex because **different positions of the double bond** also create isomers:

$$\text{CH}_3-\text{CH}_2-\text{CH}=\text{CH}_2 \quad \text{But-1-ene}$$
$$\text{CH}_3-\text{CH}=\text{CH}-\text{CH}_3 \quad \text{But-2-ene}$$

Different positions of the double bond lead to different molecular structures and differences in physical properties and some chemical properties.

**(Supplementary knowledge, not required by the syllabus but worth understanding)**: For but-2-ene, **cis-trans isomerism (geometric isomerism)** also exists — because the double bond cannot freely rotate, the groups attached to the two carbon atoms of the double bond can be on the **same side (cis)** or **opposite sides (trans)** of the double bond. This is a form of **stereoisomerism**.

## 11.4 Functional Groups — The Core Determining Chemical Properties

The concept of functional groups is the foundation for classifying organic chemistry. **A functional group is an atom or group of atoms within a molecule that determines its specific chemical reactivity.**

Core principle: Different parts of a molecule exist in different chemical environments. The **functional group region** has the strongest bond polarity and the most uneven electron density distribution, making it the **active site** for chemical reactions (bond breaking and formation). The hydrocarbon backbone (alkyl part) of the molecule is relatively inert under most conditions.

This "functional group determines properties, carbon chain affects physical properties" division of labour is the cornerstone of the entire theoretical framework of organic chemistry.

## 11.5 Fossil Fuels and Petroleum Fractional Distillation

### 11.5.1 Fossil Fuels

**Fossil fuels** are combustible substances formed from the remains of ancient organisms over millions of years of geological processes. The three main fossil fuels are:

- **Coal**: Formed from ancient plants through carbonisation under oxygen-free conditions; mainly composed of carbon
- **Natural gas**: Main component is **methane ($\text{CH}_4$)** 
- **Petroleum**: A complex **mixture of hydrocarbons** formed from the remains of ancient marine organisms

**Hydrocarbons** are compounds containing **only carbon and hydrogen** atoms. Petroleum is exactly such a complex mixture containing hundreds of different hydrocarbons (mainly alkanes, along with cycloalkanes and aromatic hydrocarbons).

### 11.5.2 Fractional Distillation — Physical Separation Using Boiling Point Differences

**Fractional distillation** is a physical process that separates the components of petroleum (called **fractions**) based on their **differences in boiling points**. This is not a chemical reaction.

**How the Fractionating Column Works**:

Petroleum is first heated to about 400°C, causing most components to vapourise. The gaseous mixture enters a fractionating column (hottest at the bottom, coolest at the top). As the mixture of gases rises through the column, the temperature gradually decreases.

- **Long-chain hydrocarbons with high boiling points**: Condense in the **lower part** of the column where they encounter cooler temperatures and are collected at the bottom
- **Short-chain hydrocarbons with lower boiling points**: Continue rising to the **middle or upper parts** of the column before condensing
- **Hydrocarbons with the lowest boiling points**: Remain gaseous all the way to the **top** of the column

**Trends in Physical Properties from Bottom to Top of the Column**:

| Property | Bottom (Long Chains) | → | Top (Short Chains) |
|----------|:-------------------:|:-:|:------------------:|
| Chain length | Long | → | Short |
| Boiling point | High | → | Low |
| Volatility | Low (hard to vaporise) | → | High (easily vaporised) |
| Viscosity | High (thick) | → | Low (thin) |

The origin of these trends lies in the strength of **London dispersion forces**: longer hydrocarbon chains have more electrons, their electron clouds are more polarisable, and adjacent molecules have larger contact areas, resulting in stronger dispersion forces. More thermal energy (higher temperature) is required to overcome these forces and allow molecules to enter the gas phase. Similarly, stronger intermolecular forces lead to higher viscosity (greater resistance to molecules sliding past each other).

### 11.5.3 Uses of the Fractions

| Fraction | Carbon Range | Main Uses |
|----------|:------------:|-----------|
| Refinery Gas | $\text{C}_1-\text{C}_4$ | Domestic fuel (heating, cooking) |
| Gasoline / Petrol | $\text{C}_5-\text{C}_{10}$ | Car fuel |
| Naphtha | $\text{C}_6-\text{C}_{14}$ | **Chemical feedstock** — used for manufacturing plastics, etc. |
| Kerosene / Paraffin | $\text{C}_{10}-\text{C}_{16}$ | Jet engine fuel |
| Diesel / Gas Oil | $\text{C}_{14}-\text{C}_{20}$ | Diesel engine fuel |
| Fuel Oil | $\text{C}_{20}-\text{C}_{50}$ | Ship fuel, home heating oil |
| Lubricating Oil | $\text{C}_{20}-\text{C}_{50}$ | Lubricants, waxes, polishes |
| Bitumen | $\text{C}_{50+}$ | Road surfacing |

## 11.6 Alkanes

### 11.6.1 Structure and Stability of Alkanes

Alkanes are the simplest organic compounds. All carbon atoms are $sp^3$ hybridised, forming four equivalent σ bonds with bond angles of $109.5^\circ$ in a tetrahedral geometry. All C—C bonds are single bonds (σ bonds) with relatively high bond energy (approximately 348 kJ/mol), making them quite stable. C—H bonds also have relatively high bond energy (approximately 412 kJ/mol).

Since all bonds are **strong and of very low polarity**, alkanes are **generally unreactive** compounds. The name "paraffin" (from Latin *parum affinis*, meaning "little affinity") reflects this low reactivity.

### 11.6.2 Reactions of Alkanes

Although alkanes are generally unreactive, two important reactions can occur under appropriate conditions:

**Reaction 1 — Combustion**

Alkanes are excellent fuels because the oxidation of C—H and C—C bonds releases large amounts of energy. Complete combustion converts all carbon and hydrogen to $\text{CO}_2$ and $\text{H}_2\text{O}$:

$$\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n+2} + \left(\frac{3n+1}{2}\right)\text{O}_2 \rightarrow n\text{CO}_2 + (n+1)\text{H}_2\text{O}$$

### 11.6.3 The Mechanism of Substitution Reactions

**Reaction 2 — Substitution with Chlorine**

Under **ultraviolet (UV) light** irradiation, alkanes react with halogens (such as chlorine) in a **substitution reaction**:

$$\text{CH}_4 + \text{Cl}_2 \xrightarrow{\text{UV}} \text{CH}_3\text{Cl} + \text{HCl}$$

Products: **Chloromethane** and **hydrogen chloride**.

**The concept of substitution**: One **atom or group** in a molecule is **replaced** by another **atom or group**. In the case of methane, one H atom is replaced by one Cl atom.

**Brief explanation of the mechanism (supplementary understanding)**: This is a type of **free radical chain reaction**. The energy provided by UV light is sufficient to break the $\text{Cl}—\text{Cl}$ bond in $\text{Cl}_2$ (bond energy approximately 242 kJ/mol), generating two highly reactive **chlorine free radicals ($\text{Cl}·$)** :

$$\text{Cl}_2 \xrightarrow{UV} 2\text{Cl}·$$

The chlorine radical abstracts an H atom from methane, producing HCl and a **methyl radical ($\text{CH}_3·$)** :

$$\text{Cl}· + \text{CH}_4 \rightarrow \text{HCl} + \text{CH}_3·$$

The methyl radical then reacts with another $\text{Cl}_2$ molecule, forming $\text{CH}_3\text{Cl}$ and regenerating a chlorine radical:

$$\text{CH}_3· + \text{Cl}_2 \rightarrow \text{CH}_3\text{Cl} + \text{Cl}·$$

The regenerated chlorine radical continues to react with new methane molecules, establishing a **chain reaction**. This is why UV light is a **necessary condition** for the reaction — it provides the **activation energy ($E_a$)** to initiate the formation of the initial radicals.

Note: The syllabus only requires knowledge of **monosubstitution** products. In practice, if chlorine is in excess, multiple H atoms can be successively substituted, producing $\text{CH}_2\text{Cl}_2$ (dichloromethane), $\text{CHCl}_3$ (trichloromethane/chloroform), and $\text{CCl}_4$ (tetrachloromethane/carbon tetrachloride).

---

## 11.7 Alkenes

### 11.7.1 Structure and Unsaturation of Alkenes

The characteristic functional group of alkenes is the **carbon-carbon double bond ($\text{C}=\text{C}$)** , which is the key to understanding all the chemical properties of alkenes.

**Electronic structure of the double bond**: The double bond consists of one σ bond and one π bond.
- **σ bond**: Formed by **head-on** overlap of $sp^2$ hybrid orbitals from the two carbon atoms along the bond axis; relatively high bond energy
- **π bond**: Formed by **sideways** overlap of unhybridised p orbitals on the two carbon atoms; located above and below the plane of the σ bond

The π bond's electron cloud is distributed above and below the σ bond plane. This **delocalisation** of π electrons makes them more susceptible to attack by electrophilic reagents. The π bond has a lower bond energy than the σ bond, so in chemical reactions, **the π bond breaks first**, while the σ bond remains intact. This is the microscopic reason why **addition reactions** can occur.

The double-bonded carbon atoms are $sp^2$ hybridised, with three $sp^2$ hybrid orbitals lying in the same plane at angles of 120°. Therefore, all atoms directly attached to the double-bonded carbons lie in the same plane.

Alkenes are called **unsaturated hydrocarbons** because their molecules contain fewer hydrogen atoms than the corresponding alkanes ($\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n}$ vs $\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n+2}$). The term "unsaturated" means that the double bond can accept additional atoms to become "saturated."

### 11.7.2 Manufacture of Alkenes — Cracking

The **cracking** of alkanes (especially long-chain alkanes) is the main industrial method for producing alkenes. Cracking is carried out at high temperatures (approximately 600–700°C) in the presence of a catalyst (typically zeolite/alumina-silica).

The essence of cracking: At high temperatures, C—C single bonds (bond energy approximately 348 kJ/mol) undergo **homolytic fission**, generating two radical fragments. These radicals then undergo rearrangement, β-scission, and other processes, ultimately yielding a **shorter-chain alkane** and an **alkene**.

$$\text{C}_{16}\text{H}_{34} \rightarrow \text{C}_8\text{H}_{18} + \text{C}_8\text{H}_{16}$$

Sometimes hydrogen is also produced:

$$\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n+2} \rightarrow \text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n} + \text{H}_2$$

**Two core purposes of cracking**:

1. **Produce high-value short-chain products**: Long-chain hydrocarbons obtained from fractional distillation have limited demand, while short-chain hydrocarbons (especially $\text{C}_5-\text{C}_{10}$ alkanes for petrol and alkenes as chemical feedstocks) are in high demand.
2. **Produce alkenes**: Natural petroleum contains very few alkenes, yet alkenes are valuable raw materials for manufacturing plastics (polymers), ethanol, and other chemicals. Cracking is the deliberate route for producing alkenes.

### 11.7.3 Distinguishing Saturated from Unsaturated — The Bromine Water Test

This is an important and practical test, and the chemical principle behind it is worth understanding in depth.

**Procedure**: Add **orange-red/reddish-brown** **bromine water ($\text{Br}_2(aq)$)** to the substance being tested.

**Alkane (saturated)**: The colour of the bromine water remains unchanged ($\text{Br}_2$ does not dissolve in alkanes, and no reaction occurs).

**Alkene (unsaturated)**: The **reddish-brown colour of bromine water rapidly disappears**, becoming colourless.

**Principle**: The double bond of the alkene undergoes an **addition reaction** with bromine molecules. Each of the two carbon atoms of the double bond adds one bromine atom, producing a colourless **dibromoalkane**:

$$\text{C}_2\text{H}_4 + \text{Br}_2 \rightarrow \text{C}_2\text{H}_4\text{Br}_2 \quad \text{1,2-dibromoethane (colourless)}$$

The disappearance of colour is because $\text{Br}_2$ molecules (reddish-brown) are consumed and converted into colourless organic bromine compounds. This reaction is rapid and the visual change is distinct, making it the classic method for distinguishing saturated from unsaturated hydrocarbons.

### 11.7.4 Addition Reactions of Alkenes — A Unified Principle

**Addition reactions** are the characteristic reactions of alkenes. All addition reactions follow a consistent pattern:

$$\text{C} = \text{C} + \text{A} - \text{B} \rightarrow \text{C} - \text{C} \text{ (A and B on the two carbons respectively)}$$

That is: **the π bond breaks, the σ bond remains intact**, and atoms or groups are added to each side of the double bond, **forming only one product**. The unsaturated compound becomes saturated.

The syllabus requires three specific addition reactions:

**(1) Addition with Bromine/Bromine Water** (used for identification — see above)

**(2) Addition with Hydrogen — Hydrogenation**

$$\text{C}_2\text{H}_4 + \text{H}_2 \xrightarrow{\text{Ni catalyst}} \text{C}_2\text{H}_6 \quad \text{(ethane)}$$

This reaction requires **nickel** as a catalyst (and appropriate heating). The H—H bond in the hydrogen molecule ($\text{H}_2$) is very strong (bond energy 436 kJ/mol). The catalyst's role is to adsorb $\text{H}_2$ molecules and weaken the H—H bond, making it easier for H atoms to add across the double bond.

Industrial application: **Hydrogenation** of vegetable oils (which contain unsaturated C=C double bonds) to produce semi-solid fats such as margarine.

**(3) Addition with Steam — Hydration**

$$\text{C}_2\text{H}_4 + \text{H}_2\text{O} \xrightarrow{\text{acid catalyst}} \text{C}_2\text{H}_5\text{OH} \quad \text{(ethanol)}$$

This reaction requires **phosphoric or sulfuric acid** as a catalyst under conditions of high temperature (300°C) and high pressure (60 atm / 6000 kPa). This is an important industrial method for **producing ethanol** (alongside fermentation).

From the perspective of **atom economy**, addition reactions are 100% atom efficient — **all atoms** in the reactants are incorporated into the product, with no by-products.

---

## 11.8 Ethanol

Ethanol ($\text{CH}_3\text{CH}_2\text{OH}$ or $\text{C}_2\text{H}_5\text{OH}$) is one of the most important alcohols, with the hydroxyl ($-\text{OH}$) functional group.

### 11.8.1 Methods of Producing Ethanol — Comparing the Two Routes

**Method 1: Fermentation**

Fermentation is the traditional method of converting **renewable biological resources** (such as sugar cane, corn, grains) into ethanol. Its chemical essence is an **enzyme-catalysed reaction**.

Glucose is converted to ethanol and carbon dioxide through a series of steps catalysed by enzymes (zymase, pyruvate decarboxylase, alcohol dehydrogenase, etc.) secreted by **yeast**:

$$\text{C}_6\text{H}_{12}\text{O}_6 \xrightarrow{\text{yeast}, 25-35^\circ\text{C}, \text{anaerobic}} 2\text{C}_2\text{H}_5\text{OH} + 2\text{CO}_2$$

**Key conditions**:
- **Temperature 25–35°C**: Enzymes have an optimal temperature range; too high a temperature denatures (deactivates) the enzyme proteins, while too low a temperature slows the reaction rate too much
- **Anaerobic conditions (absence of oxygen)** : Under oxygen-free conditions, yeast converts sugar into ethanol through **anaerobic respiration** (under aerobic conditions, yeast performs aerobic respiration, producing $\text{CO}_2$ and $\text{H}_2\text{O}$, not ethanol)
- **Yeast**: Provides the biological catalysts — enzymes

The advantages and disadvantages of fermentation can be understood across multiple dimensions:

| Aspect | Advantages | Disadvantages |
|--------|------------|---------------|
| Raw material | Renewable, widely available (sugar crops, grains) | Competes with food supply, may affect food prices |
| Energy | Low energy consumption (room temperature and pressure) | — |
| Product purity | — | Produces a dilute ethanol solution (about 8–15%), requiring distillation for concentration |
| Rate | — | Slow reaction, takes several days |
| Sustainability | Renewable energy, carbon neutral ($\text{CO}_2$ released equals $\text{CO}_2$ absorbed during plant growth) | — |
| Economics | Simple equipment, low investment | Purification is time-consuming and energy-intensive |

**Method 2: Catalytic Addition of Steam to Ethene**

This is an industrial method for converting **petrochemical feedstock** (ethene) into ethanol, utilising the addition reaction principle of alkenes:

$$\text{C}_2\text{H}_4 + \text{H}_2\text{O} \xrightarrow{\text{acid catalyst}, 300^\circ\text{C}, 60 \text{ atm}} \text{C}_2\text{H}_5\text{OH}$$

**Key conditions**:
- **300°C**: Provides sufficient thermal energy to overcome the activation energy barrier, but should not be too high, otherwise the equilibrium shifts in the reverse (endothermic) direction
- **6000 kPa / 60 atm**: High pressure favours the forward reaction (the direction with fewer gas molecules)
- **Acid catalyst**: Typically phosphoric acid ($\text{H}_3\text{PO}_4$), which facilitates proton addition

| Aspect | Advantages | Disadvantages |
|--------|------------|---------------|
| Product purity | High-purity ethanol (easy to separate) | — |
| Rate | Fast reaction | — |
| Sustainability | — | Relies on fossil fuels (petrochemical route) |
| Raw material | — | Ethene comes from petroleum, **non-renewable** |
| Conditions | — | High temperature and pressure, high energy consumption |
| Economics | Continuous large-scale production | High equipment investment, high energy consumption |

The two routes fundamentally contrast **renewable vs fossil resources**, **biological catalysis vs chemical catalysis**, and **batch vs continuous processing**.

### 11.8.2 Combustion of Ethanol (as a Fuel)

Ethanol is a liquid fuel that burns completely and cleanly:

$$\text{C}_2\text{H}_5\text{OH} + 3\text{O}_2 \rightarrow 2\text{CO}_2 + 3\text{H}_2\text{O}$$

Applications of ethanol as a fuel include:
- **Fuel additive**: Blended with petrol (e.g., E10 contains 10% ethanol, E85 contains 85% ethanol) to increase octane rating and reduce exhaust emissions
- **Alternative fuel**: In countries such as Brazil, ethanol is produced on a large scale from sugar cane and widely used as a vehicle fuel

### 11.8.3 Ethanol as a Solvent

Because the ethanol molecule contains both a polar hydroxyl group (enabling miscibility with water) and a non-polar ethyl group (enabling dissolution of many organic substances), ethanol is an **excellent polar organic solvent**, widely used in:
- Cosmetics, perfumes, and personal care products
- Pharmaceutical preparations (tinctures)
- Laboratory solvents
- Disinfectants (70% ethanol solution has optimal bactericidal effect)

---

## 11.9 Carboxylic Acids

### 11.9.1 Structure and Origin of Acidity in Carboxylic Acids

The general formula of carboxylic acids is $\text{C}_n\text{H}_{2n+1}\text{COOH}$, with the functional group being the **carboxyl group ($-\text{COOH}$)** . The carboxyl group consists of a **carbonyl group ($\text{C}=\text{O}$)** and a **hydroxyl group ($-\text{OH}$)** .

**Structural origin of acidity**:

The weak **acidity** of carboxylic acids can be understood in depth through electronic effects:

The oxygen of the carbonyl group (highly electronegative) attracts electrons through the **inductive effect**, reducing the electron density in the O—H bond and increasing its polarity. More importantly, when $\text{H}^+$ dissociates, the negative charge on the resulting **carboxylate ion ($\text{RCOO}^-$)** can be **delocalised** between the two oxygen atoms through **resonance**:

$$\text{R}-\text{C}(=\text{O})-\text{O}^- \longleftrightarrow \text{R}-\text{C}(-\text{O}^-) = \text{O}$$

This resonance stabilisation makes the carboxylate ion far more stable than the alkoxide ion ($\text{RO}^-$) of an alcohol, making carboxylic acids much stronger acids than alcohols (the hydroxyl group in alcohols does not exhibit acidity).

However, carboxylic acids are **weak acids** rather than strong acids, because their dissociation in water is **partially reversible**:

$$\text{CH}_3\text{COOH}(aq) \rightleftharpoons \text{H}^+(aq) + \text{CH}_3\text{COO}^-(aq)$$

### 11.9.2 Typical Reactions of Carboxylic Acids

The syllabus requires three typical reactions, all reflecting the properties of carboxylic acids as **acids**:

**(1) Reaction with Metals**

Carboxylic acids react with reactive metals to form **carboxylate salts** and **hydrogen gas**:

$$2\text{CH}_3\text{COOH} + \text{Mg} \rightarrow (\text{CH}_3\text{COO})_2\text{Mg} + \text{H}_2$$
$$\text{Ethanoic acid} + \text{Magnesium} \rightarrow \text{Magnesium ethanoate} + \text{Hydrogen}$$

**(2) Reaction with Bases**

Carboxylic acids undergo **neutralisation** with bases to form **carboxylate salts** and **water**:

$$\text{CH}_3\text{COOH} + \text{NaOH} \rightarrow \text{CH}_3\text{COONa} + \text{H}_2\text{O}$$
$$\text{Ethanoic acid} + \text{Sodium hydroxide} \rightarrow \text{Sodium ethanoate} + \text{Water}$$

**(3) Reaction with Carbonates**

Carboxylic acids react with carbonates to form **carboxylate salts**, **carbon dioxide**, and **water**:

$$2\text{CH}_3\text{COOH} + \text{Na}_2\text{CO}_3 \rightarrow 2\text{CH}_3\text{COONa} + \text{CO}_2 + \text{H}_2\text{O}$$
$$2\text{CH}_3\text{COOH} + \text{CaCO}_3 \rightarrow (\text{CH}_3\text{COO})_2\text{Ca} + \text{CO}_2 + \text{H}_2\text{O}$$

This demonstrates that carboxylic acids are **stronger acids than carbonic acid** (carbonic acid $pK_{a1} \approx 6.37$, ethanoic acid $pK_a \approx 4.76$).

### 11.9.3 Formation of Ethanoic Acid

The oxidation of ethanol to ethanoic acid is a classic example of the progressive oxidation: alcohol → aldehyde → carboxylic acid:

$$\text{CH}_3\text{CH}_2\text{OH} \xrightarrow{[\text{O}]} \text{CH}_3\text{CHO} \xrightarrow{[\text{O}]} \text{CH}_3\text{COOH}$$
$$\text{Ethanol} \rightarrow \text{Ethanal} \rightarrow \text{Ethanoic acid}$$

**Method 1: Chemical Oxidation**

Using **acidified potassium manganate(VII) ($\text{KMnO}_4/\text{H}^+$)** as the oxidising agent. The $\text{MnO}_4^-$ ion (purple) is reduced to $\text{Mn}^{2+}$ (almost colourless) during the reaction, so the **purple colour fading** can serve as an indicator of reaction progress:

$$\text{CH}_3\text{CH}_2\text{OH} + 2[\text{O}] \xrightarrow{\text{KMnO}_4/\text{H}^+} \text{CH}_3\text{COOH} + \text{H}_2\text{O}$$

**Method 2: Biological Oxidation (Vinegar Production)**

Under the action of bacteria such as **Acetobacter**, ethanol is oxidised to ethanoic acid. This is the core reaction in **vinegar production**:

$$\text{C}_2\text{H}_5\text{OH} + \text{O}_2 \xrightarrow{\text{Acetobacter}} \text{CH}_3\text{COOH} + \text{H}_2\text{O}$$

When ethanol (alcoholic beverages) is exposed to air, bacteria use ethanol as an energy source, carrying out oxidation reactions catalysed by enzymes to produce ethanoic acid — this is the chemical essence of how wine turns sour.

### 11.9.4 Esterification

**Esterification** is a **condensation reaction** between a **carboxylic acid** and an **alcohol**, catalysed by **concentrated sulfuric acid**:

$$\text{CH}_3\text{COOH} + \text{C}_2\text{H}_5\text{OH} \xrightarrow{\text{H}_2\text{SO}_4} \text{CH}_3\text{COOC}_2\text{H}_5 + \text{H}_2\text{O}$$

**Analysis of the reaction**:
- This is a **reversible reaction** (concentrated sulfuric acid additionally acts as a **dehydrating agent**, absorbing the water produced and shifting the equilibrium to the right, thus increasing the yield)
- The carboxylic acid loses its $-\text{OH}$ group, the alcohol loses its $-\text{H}$ atom (from the hydroxyl group), and they combine to form an **ester bond ($-\text{COO}-$)** and **water**
- Esterification is a **condensation reaction** because two molecules join together while eliminating a small molecule (water)

**Esters** have the general formula $\text{RCOOR}'$, where R comes from the carboxylic acid and R' from the alcohol. Esters typically have **pleasant, fruity odours** — the fragrances of natural fruits mostly come from ester compounds.

---

## 11.10 Polymers

### 11.10.1 Basic Concepts of Polymerisation

A **polymer** is a **large molecule** composed of many **repeat units** linked by chemical bonds, with molecular masses typically ranging from thousands to millions. The small starting molecules that form polymers are called **monomers**.

$$\text{Number of monomers} \rightarrow \text{Polymer} + \text{possible small molecule by-products}$$

The extent of polymerisation is expressed by the **degree of polymerisation ($n$)** , i.e., the number of monomer units in the polymer molecule.

### 11.10.2 Addition Polymerisation

**Definition and characteristics**: Monomer molecules contain C=C double bonds. During polymerisation, the **double bond opens**, and monomer molecules link together to form a long chain, **with no small molecule by-products**.

**Electronic-level principle**: Under the action of an initiator or heat/light, the π bond in the C=C double bond breaks, forming radicals or ionic active centres. Through **chain propagation**, monomers are sequentially added to the growing chain.

**Formation of poly(ethene)** — the classic example:

$$n\ \text{CH}_2=\text{CH}_2 \rightarrow [-\text{CH}_2-\text{CH}_2-]_n$$

- Monomer: **Ethene ($\text{C}_2\text{H}_4$)** 
- Repeat unit: $-\text{CH}_2-\text{CH}_2-$
- Polymer: **Poly(ethene) / Polyethylene (PE)**

Polyethylene is one of the most widely used plastics. It comes in different densities: high-density polyethylene (HDPE, for rigid bottles, pipes) and low-density polyethylene (LDPE, for films, plastic bags).

### 11.10.3 Condensation Polymerisation

**Definition and characteristics**: Two different types of monomers (each containing two or more reactive groups) react together, and each bond formation is accompanied by the **elimination of a small molecule** (usually water). Each step is a **substitution reaction** forming new covalent bonds.

The syllabus requires two types of condensation polymers:

**(1) Polyamides — Nylon as an Example**

- **Monomers**: A **dicarboxylic acid** (containing two $-\text{COOH}$ groups) and a **diamine** (containing two $-\text{NH}_2$ groups)
- **Reaction**: Carboxyl groups react with amino groups to form **amide bonds ($-\text{CO}-\text{NH}-$)** , eliminating water molecules
- **Repeat unit structure**: $[-\text{CO}-(\text{CH}_2)_n-\text{CO}-\text{NH}-(\text{CH}_2)_m-\text{NH}-]$

**Nylon** was one of the first synthetic fibres. It is strong and elastic, used for clothing, ropes, and other applications.

**(2) Polyesters — PET as an Example**

- **Monomers**: A **dicarboxylic acid** (containing two $-\text{COOH}$ groups) and a **diol** (containing two $-\text{OH}$ groups)
