- **Reaction**: Carboxyl groups react with hydroxyl groups to form **ester bonds ($-\text{CO}-\text{O}-$)** , eliminating water molecules
- **Repeat unit structure**: $[-\text{CO}-\text{C}_6\text{H}_4-\text{CO}-\text{O}-(\text{CH}_2)_2-\text{O}-]$

**PET (Polyethylene Terephthalate)** is used for plastic bottles (drinks bottles) and polyester fibres (clothing).

**Comparison of Addition vs Condensation Polymerisation**:

| Feature | Addition Polymerisation | Condensation Polymerisation |
|---------|:----------------------:|:---------------------------:|
| Monomer type | Unsaturated monomers with C=C double bonds | Two monomers with different functional groups |
| By-product | **None** | **Small molecule by-product** (e.g., water) |
| Product composition | Same empirical formula as monomer (repeat unit = monomer) | Composition differs from simple sum of monomers |
| Chain growth | Through double bond opening | Through condensation between functional groups |
| Bond type | C—C single bonds | Amide bonds or ester bonds |

### 11.10.4 Environmental Challenges of Plastics

The chemical stability of plastics is both an advantage (durability, corrosion resistance) and a disadvantage (difficulty degrading in the natural environment), leading to serious environmental challenges:

**(1) Landfill**
Plastics can persist in landfill sites for **hundreds of years** without decomposing, occupying valuable landfill space. Even partial degradation may release microplastics into soil and groundwater.

**(2) Accumulation in Oceans**
Large quantities of plastic waste eventually reach the oceans. Ocean currents concentrate them into **ocean garbage patches** (such as the Great Pacific Garbage Patch). Marine animals (sea turtles, seabirds, fish) often mistake plastic for food and ingest it, leading to digestive tract blockages, starvation, and even death. In the ocean, plastics break down under sunlight, weathering, and wave action into **microplastics**, which enter the marine food chain.

**(3) Formation of Toxic Gases from Burning**
Chlorine-containing plastics such as PVC (polyvinyl chloride) produce **hydrogen chloride ($\text{HCl}$)** and **dioxins** when burned. Dioxins are known **carcinogens** and **persistent organic pollutants**. Even plastics without halogens produce **carbon monoxide ($\text{CO}$)** and **polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons (PAHs)** if combusted incompletely.

### 11.10.5 Closed-Loop Recycling of PET

One special advantage of PET is its ability to be **depolymerised** — under specific conditions (such as heating or treatment with chemical reagents), it can be broken back down into its original monomers, which can then be re-polymerised into new PET. This achieves a closed loop of **chemical recycling**:

$$\text{PET polymer} \rightleftharpoons \text{Monomers (terephthalic acid + ethylene glycol)}$$

This **polymerisation-depolymerisation-repolymerisation** cycle provides a chemical pathway for the sustainable use of plastics.

### 11.10.6 Proteins — Natural Polyamides

**Proteins** are among the most important macromolecules in living organisms. Their essence is that of **natural polyamides**.

**Amino acids** are the **monomers** that make up proteins. Their general formula is:

$$\text{H}_2\text{N}-\text{CHR}-\text{COOH}$$

The R group (side chain) differs among different amino acids, determining the chemical characteristics of each amino acid (polarity, acidity/basicity, hydrophilicity/hydrophobicity, etc.).

**Formation of the peptide bond**: The $-\text{NH}_2$ group of one amino acid reacts with the $-\text{COOH}$ group of another amino acid, forming an **amide bond (here called a peptide bond)** and eliminating a water molecule:

$$\text{H}_2\text{N}-\text{CHR}_1-\text{COOH} + \text{H}_2\text{N}-\text{CHR}_2-\text{COOH} \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{N}-\text{CHR}_1-\text{CO}-\text{NH}-\text{CHR}_2-\text{COOH} + \text{H}_2\text{O}$$

Multiple amino acids linked by peptide bonds form a **polypeptide chain**. Proteins consist of one or more polypeptide chains that undergo complex folding and assembly.

The structure of proteins can be represented by the repeat unit:

$$[-\text{NH}-\text{CHR}-\text{CO}-]_n$$

This is identical in structure to the amide bond ($\text{CO}-\text{NH}$) found in synthetic polyamides. Therefore, proteins are truly **natural polyamides**.

---

> **Conclusion**: Topic 10 (Chemistry of the Environment) reveals the intricate web of interactions between chemistry, human activities, and Earth's ecosystems. From the microscopic level (how greenhouse gas molecules absorb infrared radiation) to the macroscopic level (how acid rain damages forests and lakes), chemistry provides the key tools for understanding environmental problems and finding solutions. Topic 11 (Organic Chemistry) showcases the boundless versatility of the carbon element — from the simplest methane to complex protein macromolecules, from fossil fuels to renewable biomass resources. The framework of organic chemistry helps us understand the chemical nature of both biological substances and synthetic materials, while also providing the theoretical foundation for developing sustainable materials and technologies.
---
---
# Topic 12: Experimental Techniques and Chemical Analysis — In-Depth Principles

---

## 12.1 Experimental Design

### 12.1.1 Fundamental Concepts of Experimental Measurements

Chemistry is a **quantitative science**, and every experiment relies on the precise measurement of physical quantities. The essence of measurement is comparing a quantity to be measured against a **standard unit**. In chemical experiments, the most fundamental and core measurable physical quantities include: **time**, **temperature**, **mass**, and **volume**. Each physical quantity corresponds to specifically designed instruments, and the choice of instrument directly determines the **precision** and **accuracy** of the measurement results.

### 12.1.2 Time Measurement Instruments

**Stop-watches** are the most essential time-measuring tools in chemical kinetics experiments. Their operating principle is based on the **piezoelectric effect of quartz crystals** — when an electric field is applied, the quartz crystal vibrates at an extremely stable frequency. By counting these vibrations, precise time measurement is achieved. The smallest scale division of a stop-watch (typically 0.01 s or 0.1 s) determines the **resolution** of the measurement. However, in practical operation, human reaction delay (typically between 0.2–0.3 s) is the main factor limiting measurement precision. Therefore, when reaction rates are very fast, **data loggers** with sensors should be used for automatic timing to eliminate human error.

### 12.1.3 Temperature Measurement Instruments

**Thermometers** operate on the principle of **thermal expansion** of substances. Common **liquid-in-glass thermometers** utilise the expansion of mercury or alcohol — when temperature rises, the liquid volume increases and the column rises along the capillary tube; when temperature falls, the liquid contracts and the column descends. The **range** and **scale division** of a thermometer must match the experimental requirements:

- **Ordinary thermometer**: Range $-10^\circ\text{C}$ to $110^\circ\text{C}$, scale division $1^\circ\text{C}$, suitable for routine experiments
- **Beckmann thermometer**: Scale division up to $0.01^\circ\text{C}$, specifically designed for precise calorimetric experiments

A core principle of temperature measurement is **thermal equilibrium** — the thermometer must be in full contact with the system being measured until the temperatures of both are identical. Only then does the reading represent the true temperature of the system. If the thermometer is read after being removed from the system, the reading will deviate from the true value due to the influence of the ambient temperature.

**Temperature calibration** is a non-negligible issue. Thermometer scales are based on the **fixed-point calibration method** — using the freezing point ($0^\circ\text{C}$) and boiling point ($100^\circ\text{C}$, under standard atmospheric pressure) of water as two reference points. If the thermometer reading deviates at these two points, all measured values require correction.

### 12.1.4 Mass Measurement Instruments

**Balances** have evolved from mechanical to electronic principles. At the IGCSE level, two main types are used:

**Electronic Balances (Digital Balances)** operate on the principle of **electromagnetic force compensation** — when an object is placed on the pan, a sensor detects displacement, and an electromagnetic coil generates a force equal in magnitude but opposite in direction to gravity, restoring the pan to its original position. The magnitude of this compensating current is proportional to the mass. The scale division of an electronic balance is typically $0.01\,\text{g}$ or $0.001\,\text{g}$.

When using a balance, the following principles must be understood:

- **Calibration**: The balance must be calibrated with standard masses before use because gravitational acceleration varies with geographical location
- **Levelling**: The balance must be placed on a level surface; otherwise, gravitational components will cause systematic errors
- **Zero Drift**: Electronic components may experience zero drift due to temperature effects, so the balance must be zeroed before each weighing
- **Error Propagation**: When using the "method of differences" (weighing before and after a reaction, e.g., measuring $\text{CO}_2$ gas release), the errors from two weighings are superimposed

### 12.1.5 Volume Measurement Instruments

Volume measurement is one of the most precise operations in chemical experiments, with different instruments having fundamentally different design principles.

**Burette**:
A burette is an instrument for precisely **dispensing** variable volumes of liquid. Its design is based on the following principles:
- The slender, uniformly-bore glass tube ensures significant changes in liquid column height per unit volume, thereby improving reading precision
- The **stopcock** controls flow rate, with precision down to one drop (approximately $0.05\,\text{cm}^3$)
- Readings must be taken at the **lowest point of the meniscus**; this is because the cohesive forces within water are greater than the adhesive forces between water and glass, forming a concave meniscus (mercury forms a convex meniscus)
- Standard burettes have a capacity of $50.00\,\text{cm}^3$ with a scale division of $0.1\,\text{cm}^3$, and can be estimated to $0.01\,\text{cm}^3$

$$\text{Volume dispensed} = V_{\text{final}} - V_{\text{initial}}$$

**Volumetric Pipette**:
A volumetric pipette is an instrument for precisely **transferring** a fixed volume of liquid. Its principle utilises **siphoning action** — after liquid is drawn above the calibration mark, controlling the sealing of the fingertip allows the liquid level to slowly descend to the mark. At this point, the pipette contains precisely the calibrated volume. When released, the liquid flows out by gravity. The small amount remaining at the tip **must not be blown out**, because the pipette's calibration already accounts for this residual amount. Common sizes include $10.00\,\text{cm}^3$, $20.00\,\text{cm}^3$, and $25.00\,\text{cm}^3$.

**Measuring Cylinder**:
Measuring cylinders are used for approximate volume measurements. Their design is based on the **reverse application of Archimedes' displacement principle** — a given volume of liquid occupies a height proportional to it in a cylindrical container. The scale division of a measuring cylinder is much larger than that of a burette or volumetric pipette, typically $1\,\text{cm}^3$ or $0.5\,\text{cm}^3$, making it suitable for operations not requiring high precision. When in use, it must be placed on a level surface and read vertically, with the line of sight level with the lowest point of the meniscus (**parallax elimination**).

**Gas Syringe**:
A gas syringe is a specialised instrument for measuring **gas volumes**. Its core structure is a sealed glass barrel with a plunger. When gas enters the syringe through tubing, it pushes the plunger outward, and the volume is read from the scale. The greatest advantage of the gas syringe is that it allows **real-time monitoring** of gas production within a **closed system**, making it suitable for reaction rate studies. Typical capacities are $100\,\text{cm}^3$ or $50\,\text{cm}^3$, with a scale division of $1\,\text{cm}^3$.

When using a gas syringe, **temperature effects** must be considered. According to the ideal gas law:

$$PV = nRT$$

If the temperature changes during the experiment, even if the amount of substance $n$ remains constant, the volume $V$ will change due to variations in $T$. Therefore, when comparing volume readings at different times, the temperature must be kept constant, or a temperature correction must be applied.

### 12.1.6 Principles for Evaluating Experimental Methods

Evaluating the merits of an experimental method requires consideration from multiple dimensions:

**Precision**: The **degree of consistency** among repeated measurements, reflecting the magnitude of **random errors**. High precision means small dispersion among measured values, but does not necessarily mean they are close to the true value.

**Accuracy**: The **degree of closeness** between measured values and the **true value**, reflecting the magnitude of **systematic errors**. High accuracy means the measured values are collectively close to the true value.

**Reliability**: The **reproducibility** of experimental results. A reliable experiment should yield consistent results at different times and when performed by different operators.

**Validity and Suitability**: Whether the experimental method truly measures what it claims to measure, and whether it is suitable for the specific experimental conditions (e.g., temperature range, concentration range, reaction rate, etc.).

**Safety**: Whether the chemical substances and operational steps involved in the experiment present any hazards.

**Economic and Time Efficiency**: The cost, time required, and availability of necessary apparatus and equipment.

### 12.1.7 Basic Concepts of Solutions

Understanding the strict definitions of solution-related terminology is a prerequisite for correct experimental operation and data analysis.

**Solvent**: The substance that dissolves other substances, typically the component present in the largest quantity in an experiment. In aqueous solutions, water is the solvent. The choice of solvent is based on the **"like dissolves like" principle** — polar solvents dissolve polar solutes, and non-polar solvents dissolve non-polar solutes.

**Solute**: The substance that is dissolved in the solvent. Solutes can be solids, liquids, or gases. When a solute dissolves, its particles (molecules or ions) become surrounded by solvent molecules — a process called **solvation**.

**Solution**: A **homogeneous mixture** formed by the uniform dispersion of a solute in a solvent. The components of a solution are mixed uniformly at the molecular or ionic level, with no phase separation.

**Saturated Solution**: A solution in which, at a **specified temperature**, the solvent has dissolved the **maximum concentration** of solute possible. At this point, a **dynamic equilibrium** is established between the undissolved solute and the dissolved solute:

$$\text{Solute (undissolved)} \rightleftharpoons \text{Solute (dissolved)}$$

The essence of this equilibrium is that the rate at which solute dissolves from the solid surface equals the rate at which solute returns from the solution to the solid surface (**crystallisation rate**). The saturation concentration varies with temperature — for most solids, solubility increases with temperature. Solubility is expressed quantitatively as:

$$\text{Solubility} = \frac{\text{mass of solute (g)}}{\text{100 g solvent}} \quad \text{at a specified temperature}$$

**Residue**: The substance that **remains** after evaporation, distillation, filtration, or any similar separation process. In filtration, the residue is the insoluble solid retained on the filter paper; in evaporation, the residue is the solid left after the solvent has been boiled off.

**Filtrate**: The clear liquid or solution that has **passed through a filter**. The filtrate contains all particles capable of passing through the pores of the filter paper — i.e., the solvent and all soluble solutes.

---

## 12.2 Acid-Base Titrations

### 12.2.1 Fundamental Principles of Titration

Acid-base titration is a **quantitative analytical technique** whose core principle is based on the **neutralisation reaction**:

$$\text{H}^+(aq) + \text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{O}(l)$$

The essence of titration is: a solution of known concentration (the **titrant**) is gradually added from a burette to a known volume of a solution of unknown concentration (the **analyte**). By observing the colour change of an **indicator**, the moment when the reaction is exactly complete is identified — this is the **stoichiometric point (equivalence point)**.

### 12.2.2 Core Components of the Titration Apparatus

**Burette**:
The design of the burette embodies a combination of geometric precision and fluid dynamics principles. The uniform internal diameter ensures that the volume change per unit height is consistent throughout, effectively converting volume measurements into linear displacement readings. The precision scale allows the operator to control the added liquid with great accuracy, down to approximately $0.05\,\text{cm}^3$ (one drop). When reading, the burette must be **vertically clamped** to a stand, with the eye level with the lowest point of the meniscus to eliminate **parallax error**.

**Volumetric Pipette and Pipette Filler**:
The volumetric pipette ensures precise liquid transfer through accurate volume calibration. When using a filler to draw up liquid, one crucial principle must be followed: **the mouth must never be used for pipetting**. This is a safety consideration to prevent acids, alkalis, or toxic liquids from entering the mouth. The procedure of using a pipette embodies the principle of **rinsing** — before drawing the test liquid, the pipette is rinsed 2–3 times with the liquid to be measured. The purpose is to remove residual water inside the pipette, preventing dilution of the test solution. The physical rationale is that residual water would alter the concentration of the original solution, undermining the basis of quantitative analysis.

**Conical Flask (Erlenmeyer Flask)** :
The conical flask has specific functional design considerations: its narrow neck effectively prevents liquid splashing during swirling; its wide base facilitates thorough mixing of the titrant and analyte, either by magnetic stirrer or manual swirling. During titration, a **white tile** should be placed under the conical flask to make the indicator colour change more clearly visible.

### 12.2.3 Principles of Titration Indicators

Acid-base indicators are themselves **weak organic acids or bases**, whose molecular structures exhibit different colours between their **conjugate acid form (HIn)** and **conjugate base form (In$^-$)** . A **proton transfer equilibrium** exists between these two forms:

$$\text{HIn}(aq) \rightleftharpoons \text{H}^+(aq) + \text{In}^-(aq)$$

$$\text{Colour A} \quad \quad \text{Colour B}$$

According to Le Chatelier's principle:
- In an acidic environment (high $[\text{H}^+]$), the equilibrium shifts left, and **Colour A** dominates
- In an alkaline environment (low $[\text{H}^+]$), the equilibrium shifts right, and **Colour B** dominates

The **colour change interval** of an indicator is determined by its dissociation constant $K_{\text{In}}$:

$$K_{\text{In}} = \frac{[\text{H}^+][\text{In}^-]}{[\text{HIn}]}$$

The human eye can reliably detect colour changes only when $[\text{In}^-]/[\text{HIn}] \approx 10$ or $\approx 0.1$, corresponding to a pH range of approximately:

$$\text{pH} = \text{p}K_{\text{In}} \pm 1$$

The core principle for selecting an indicator is: the **colour change interval** of the indicator must completely or largely fall within the **titration jump range** (the range near the equivalence point where pH changes abruptly).

### 12.2.4 Determining the End-Point

The **end-point** is the moment when the indicator changes colour, while the **equivalence point** is the theoretical point at which the acid and base have exactly completely reacted. The goal of titration analysis is to make these two points **coincide as closely as possible**. The method of determining the end-point varies depending on the type of indicator:

- **Thymolphthalein**: Colourless in acidic solutions, turns blue in alkaline solutions. The end-point is from colourless to the first appearance of a stable pale blue colour.
- **Methyl orange**: Red in acidic solutions, yellow in alkaline solutions. The end-point is from red, through orange, to yellow.

A key operational technique: when approaching the end-point, the titrant should be added **"half-drop by half-drop"** — first open the stopcock to let half a drop hang at the tip of the burette, touch this half-drop to the inner wall of the conical flask, then rinse the wall with deionised water and swirl. This operation significantly improves the precision of locating the end-point.

### 12.2.5 Physicochemical Sources of Titration Errors

Understanding the diverse sources of error in titration is a prerequisite for improving experimental quality:

**Operational Errors**:
- **Reading error**: Line of sight not level with the lowest point of the meniscus, causing parallax
- **Burette leakage**: Stopcock not fully closed, causing continuous leakage of titrant
- **Insufficient swirling**: Local overtitration, leading to premature end-point determination
- **Excessive titration speed**: Insufficient mixing and reaction time, causing end-point "drift"

**Systematic Errors**:
- **Inappropriate indicator selection**: Indicator colour change interval does not match the titration jump range
- **Inaccurate titrant concentration calibration**: Insufficient purity or drying of the primary standard substance
- **Temperature effects**: Temperature changes affect solution volume (thermal expansion/contraction) and reaction equilibrium
- **$\text{CO}_2$ interference**: Carbon dioxide from the air dissolves in water to form carbonic acid, consuming titrant

**End-Point Judgement Errors**:
- Different operators perceive colours differently
- The indicator itself is a weak acid or base and consumes a very small amount of titrant (called the **indicator blank**)

### 12.2.6 Core Quantitative Relationships in Titration Analysis

The quantitative basis of titration analysis is **stoichiometric relationships**. For the reaction:

$$a\text{A} + b\text{B} \rightarrow \text{products}$$

At the equivalence point:

$$n_A : n_B = a : b$$

$$\frac{c_A V_A}{a} = \frac{c_B V_B}{b}$$

Where $c$ is concentration (mol/dm³) and $V$ is volume (dm³). This relationship is the mathematical heart of all volumetric analysis, linking a measurable physical quantity (volume) to a quantity that cannot be directly measured (concentration).

---

## 12.3 Chromatography

### 12.3.1 Physicochemical Principles of Chromatographic Separation

Chromatography is a class of separation techniques based on **differences in the distribution of components between a stationary phase and a mobile phase**. At the IGCSE level, the focus is on **paper chromatography**, whose core principles involve several layers:

**Partition Equilibrium**:
When a substance dissolves in a solvent, and the solvent acts as the **mobile phase** moving along the **stationary phase** (water molecules adsorbed onto cellulose fibres of the filter paper), the solute molecules establish a **dynamic partition equilibrium** between the mobile and stationary phases:

$$\text{Solute}_{\text{(mobile phase)}} \rightleftharpoons \text{Solute}_{\text{(stationary phase)}}$$

This equilibrium obeys **Nernst's Distribution Law**:

$$K = \frac{[\text{Solute}]_{\text{stationary phase}}}{[\text{Solute}]_{\text{mobile phase}}}$$

Where $K$ is the **partition coefficient**. Substances with larger $K$ values tend to remain more in the stationary phase and therefore move more slowly; substances with smaller $K$ values tend to move more with the mobile phase and therefore move more quickly.

**Capillary Action**:
The driving force for the upward movement of the solvent along the filter paper is **capillary action**. When the filter paper is immersed in the solvent, the **adhesive forces** between the solvent molecules and the paper fibres are greater than the **cohesive forces** between the solvent molecules themselves, causing the solvent to be "sucked" into the microscopic gaps between fibres. This process is described by the **Young-Laplace equation**:

$$\Delta P = \frac{2\gamma \cos\theta}{r}$$

Where $\Delta P$ is the capillary pressure difference, $\gamma$ is the surface tension, $\theta$ is the contact angle, and $r$ is the capillary radius. The smaller the fibre gaps in the filter paper, the stronger the capillary action, and the faster the solvent rises.

### 12.3.2 Operational Principles of Paper Chromatography

While the operation of paper chromatography may appear simple, each step is based on specific chemical principles:

**Spotting**:
The sample solution is spotted onto a **baseline** approximately 1–2 cm from the bottom of the filter paper. The reason a pencil (not ink) must be used for marking the baseline is that ink pigments themselves would dissolve in the solvent and migrate, interfering with the chromatographic results. After each spotting, the solvent must be allowed to fully evaporate before repeating the spotting, in order to **concentrate** the sample and improve subsequent detection sensitivity.

**Development**:
The filter paper is placed in a sealed chromatography tank, with the solvent level below the baseline. This condition is extremely critical — if the solvent level is above the baseline, the sample would directly dissolve into the solvent reservoir rather than being carried along the filter paper, rendering separation meaningless. The purpose of the sealed environment is to **saturate** the air inside the tank with solvent vapour, preventing excessive evaporation of solvent from the filter paper and ensuring the mobile phase continues to move upward steadily.

**The Essence of Separation**:
The fundamental reason why different components in a mixture travel different distances along the chromatography direction is that each component has a **different partition coefficient $K$** between the stationary and mobile phases. This difference can originate from:

1. **Polarity differences**: The more polar a substance, the stronger its interaction with the stationary phase (polar water molecules), resulting in shorter travel distance (smaller $R_f$)
2. **Molecular size**: Smaller molecules dissolve more readily in the mobile phase and migrate with it
3. **Hydrogen bonding ability**: Functional groups capable of hydrogen bonding with the stationary phase (e.g., $-\text{OH}$) slow down movement

### 12.3.3 Physical Meaning of the $R_f$ Value

The **$R_f$ value (Retention Factor / Retardation Factor)** is a characteristic constant describing the migration behaviour of a substance in a specific chromatographic system:

$$R_f = \frac{\text{distance travelled by substance}}{\text{distance travelled by solvent front}}$$

The $R_f$ value essentially reflects the **proportion of the substance partitioned between two immiscible phases**. $R_f$ always lies between 0 and 1:

- $R_f \approx 0$: The substance is strongly adsorbed to the stationary phase and hardly moves with the mobile phase
- $R_f \approx 1$: The substance is almost completely dissolved in the mobile phase and moves with the solvent front
- $R_f \approx 0.5$: The substance is approximately equally distributed between the stationary and mobile phases

The $R_f$ value is a **system-dependent parameter** — the same substance will have different $R_f$ values under different solvent systems, temperatures, and types of filter paper. Therefore, identification of a substance requires parallel experiments using **known reference standards** under **identical conditions**, comparing $R_f$ values to confirm identity.

### 12.3.4 Chromatographic Identification of Pure Substances and Mixtures

On a chromatogram:
- **Pure substance**: Produces only **one spot** under specific conditions, corresponding to a single unique $R_f$ value
- **Mixture**: Produces **multiple spots**, each spot corresponding to one component
- **Unknown identification**: If an unknown substance and a known standard have the same $R_f$ value under identical conditions, they can be preliminarily identified as the same substance

### 12.3.5 Principles for Detecting Colourless Substances

When the substance to be separated is itself colourless, a **locating agent** must be used to make the spots visible. The principle of a locating agent is typically based on **chemical reaction colour development** — by spraying or dipping, the locating agent reacts with the substance to produce a coloured compound. For example, amino acids can be detected by spraying with ninhydrin and heating; the principle is that ninhydrin reacts with the amino group ($-\text{NH}_2$) of the amino acid to produce a purple compound. Another key characteristic of locating agents is **non-selectivity** — they should react with all members of the same series, not just with individual substances.

### 12.3.6 Broader Principles of Chromatography

Paper chromatography is merely an introductory form of chromatography. The broader principles can be extended to:

**Adsorption Chromatography**: Separates substances based on their differential adsorption capacity onto a solid adsorbent (such as silica gel or alumina). The adsorption process is essentially a **surface interaction** — polar interactions between active sites on the adsorbent surface and the solute molecules. The adsorption isotherm can be described by the **Langmuir adsorption model** or the **Freundlich adsorption model**.

**Column Chromatography**: The adsorbent is packed into a glass column, and the sample and eluent (mobile phase) are added from the top. Different components elute from the bottom of the column sequentially based on their adsorption strengths, forming an **elution curve (chromatogram)**. Each pure component collected is called a **fraction**.

**Thin Layer Chromatography (TLC)** : The adsorbent is coated onto a glass or aluminium plate instead of filter paper. It offers advantages of faster separation, higher resolution, and easier visualisation, with the $R_f$ value defined identically to paper chromatography.

---

## 12.4 Separation and Purification

### 12.4.1 Physicochemical Basis of Separation Methods

The ultimate goal of separating a mixture is to **purify** the target component from other substances. The essence of all separation methods is to exploit **differences in physical properties** among the components of a mixture by applying some driving force to achieve spatial separation. These physical properties include: **solubility**, **boiling point**, **particle size**, **density**, **polarity**, **volatility**, and **electrical charge**, among others.

### 12.4.2 Dissolution and Extraction

**Using a suitable solvent**:
This is the most fundamental and commonly used separation method. Its principle is based on the **"like dissolves like"** rule — polar solutes dissolve in polar solvents, and non-polar solutes dissolve in non-polar solvents. In a mixture, if only one component is soluble in a particular solvent, it can be selectively extracted from the mixture.

The thermodynamic driving force for extraction is the **Gibbs free energy of mixing**:

$$\Delta G_{\text{mix}} = \Delta H_{\text{mix}} - T\Delta S_{\text{mix}}$$

When $\Delta G_{\text{mix}} < 0$, the dissolution process can occur spontaneously. $\Delta H_{\text{mix}}$ includes the interaction energies between solute and solvent, while $\Delta S_{\text{mix}}$ reflects the increase in entropy as the solute disperses within the solvent.

### 12.4.3 Filtration

Filtration is a technique for solid-liquid separation based on **differences in particle size**. Its core principle relies on the **sieving effect of a porous medium** — filter paper (or filter membrane) contains numerous micron-sized pores through which liquid (and solutes dissolved within it) can pass freely, while solid particles are retained because their size exceeds the pore diameter.

**Gravity Filtration**:
The liquid passes through the filter paper under the influence of gravity alone. The filter paper is folded into a **cone** to increase the filtration area and maintain a consistent three-layer thickness to prevent tearing. During filtration, a glass rod is used to guide the liquid toward the centre of the filter paper, preventing splashing; the filtrate should flow along the inner wall of the container to prevent splashing. The liquid level in the filter paper should be **below** the paper's edge by approximately 0.5 cm; otherwise, solid particles may creep over the edge of the filter paper due to capillary action (the "wicking effect").

**Vacuum Filtration (Suction Filtration)** :
A vacuum pump connected to the filter flask reduces the pressure inside the flask, using atmospheric pressure to force the liquid through the filter paper. According to:

$$\Delta P = P_{\text{atm}} - P_{\text{vacuum}}$$

The greater the pressure difference, the faster the filtration rate. Vacuum filtration is suitable when rapid separation is required or when the solid needs to be dried during the filtration process. However, caution is needed: for very fine precipitates, excessive pressure difference may cause particles to block the pores of the filter paper, actually reducing filtration efficiency.

### 12.4.4 Crystallisation

Crystallisation is a method for purifying solid substances from solution based on **differences in solubility**. Its core principle is that different substances exhibit different degrees of change in solubility with temperature in the same solvent.

**The Driving Force for Crystallisation — Supersaturation**:
Crystallisation can only occur from a **supersaturated solution**. Supersaturation is a **metastable state** — the concentration of solute exceeds the equilibrium solubility at that temperature, but crystals have not yet spontaneously precipitated. As the solution is cooled or the solvent is evaporated, the driving force for the system to transition from the metastable state to thermodynamic equilibrium increases, eventually reaching a critical point where crystal nuclei begin to form.

**Nucleation**:
The first step of crystallisation is the formation of a **nucleus** — solute molecules randomly collide and aggregate in solution to form extremely small ordered assemblies. Nucleation must overcome an **energy barrier**, because the newly formed solid-liquid interface requires additional surface energy. The magnitude of this energy barrier is described by the **Gibbs-Thomson relation**:

$$\Delta G_{\text{nucleation}} = \frac{16\pi\gamma^3 V_m^2}{3(kT\ln S)^2}$$

Where $\gamma$ is the interfacial tension, $V_m$ is the molar volume, and $S$ is the supersaturation ratio. The greater the supersaturation, the lower the energy barrier, and the faster the nucleation rate.

**Crystal Growth**:
Once nuclei are formed, solute molecules diffuse from the solution to the crystal surface and embed themselves into the crystal lattice, gradually enlarging the crystal. The rate of crystal growth is controlled by two factors:
1. **Mass transport**: The rate of diffusion of solute from the bulk solution to the crystal surface
2. **Surface integration**: The rate at which solute molecules find appropriate positions on the crystal surface and embed into the lattice

Slow cooling yields large, pure crystals because solute molecules have sufficient time to arrange themselves in the most thermodynamically stable manner; rapid cooling results in small crystals, which may even trap impurities.

### 12.4.5 Simple Distillation

Simple distillation is a technique for separating **liquids from non-volatile solids** or **two liquids with very different boiling points** ($\geq 40^\circ\text{C}$ difference) based on **boiling point differences**. Its physical principles are as follows:

- **Heating phase**: The liquid absorbs thermal energy, molecular kinetic energy increases, and when the vapour pressure reaches the external atmospheric pressure, the liquid boils
- **Evaporation phase**: Liquid molecules gain sufficient kinetic energy to overcome intermolecular attractive forces, escaping from the liquid surface into the vapour phase
- **Condensation phase**: The vapour molecules encounter the cold inner wall of the condenser, lose kinetic energy, and intermolecular forces pull them back into the liquid state
- **Collection phase**: The pure condensate (distillate) flows into the receiver

**Principles of Key Distillation Apparatus Components**:

- **Distillation flask**: Filled to no more than 2/3 of its capacity, with **boiling chips** added. The porous structure of boiling chips provides **nucleation sites for vaporisation**, enabling uniform bubble formation and preventing **bumping** — a phenomenon where local superheating causes sudden, violent liquid ejection
- **Thermometer**: The bulb should be positioned **slightly below** the sidearm of the distillation flask, so that it measures the **temperature of the vapour** rather than the temperature of the liquid. During distillation, the thermometer reading indicates the boiling point of the distillate
- **Liebig Condenser**: The **bottom-in, top-out** design of the cooling water ensures the condenser jacket is always filled with cold water, maximising heat exchange efficiency. The cooling water enters from the lower end and exits from the upper end, creating a **counter-current flow** opposite to the direction of vapour flow, maximising heat transfer efficiency

### 12.4.6 Fractional Distillation

Fractional distillation is a technique for separating **mixtures of two or more liquids with similar boiling points**. The key component that elevates it above simple distillation is the **fractionating column**.

**Physical Principle of the Fractionating Column — Theoretical Plates**:
The fractionating column is packed with glass beads, glass rings, or stainless steel packing, providing a large surface area. As the mixed vapour rises, it encounters the cooler packing surface and undergoes **partial condensation** — the higher-boiling component condenses preferentially into liquid; as the condensate flows downward along the packing, it is **partially re-vaporised** by the rising hot vapour — the lower-boiling component vaporises preferentially. This **condensation-vaporisation cycle** repeats at each layer of packing, effectively performing multiple simple distillations in succession.

Each condensation-vaporisation cycle is called a **theoretical plate**. The efficiency of a fractionating column is expressed by the **number of theoretical plates ($N$)** :

$$N = \frac{L}{\text{HETP}}$$

Where $L$ is the column length and $\text{HETP}$ is the **Height Equivalent to a Theoretical Plate**. The greater the number of theoretical plates, the higher the separation efficiency.

**Temperature Gradient**:
A **temperature gradient** exists from bottom to top of the fractionating column — the bottom is hottest (close to the boiling point of the liquid in the distillation flask), and the top is coolest (close to the condensation temperature of the distillate). This gradient is particularly crucial when the boiling points of the components in the mixture are close together. During fractional distillation, the thermometer reading gradually rises as the lower-boiling component is progressively distilled off.

### 12.4.7 Determination of Purity

**Melting Point** and **Boiling Point** are the most fundamental physical constants of a substance and are core indicators for judging purity.

**Principle of Melting Point Determination**:
Pure substances have a very narrow melting range (typically $\leq 1^\circ\text{C}$), meaning the temperature difference between the onset and completion of melting is extremely small. If impurities are present, the melting point **decreases** (Freezing Point Depression), and the **melting range broadens** (a larger temperature difference between the start and end of melting).

The thermodynamic explanation for this phenomenon is: impurities disrupt the regularity of the pure substance's crystal lattice, lowering the **escape energy barrier** for molecules within the crystal, so less thermal energy is required to break down the crystal lattice.

**Principle of Boiling Point Determination**:
A pure substance has a fixed boiling point at constant pressure. When impurities are present, the boiling point typically **increases** (Boiling Point Elevation), because the presence of impurities lowers the vapour pressure of the solvent.

According to **Raoult's Law**:

$$P_{\text{solution}} = \chi_{\text{solvent}} \cdot P_{\text{pure solvent}}$$

Where $\chi_{\text{solvent}}$ is the mole fraction of the solvent, and $P_{\text{pure solvent}}$ is the vapour pressure of the pure solvent. When a solute is present, $\chi_{\text{solvent}} < 1$, so $P_{\text{solution}} < P_{\text{pure solvent}}$. The solution requires a higher temperature for its vapour pressure to reach the external atmospheric pressure and thus boil.

At standard atmospheric pressure, pure water boils at $100^\circ\text{C}$. If the measured value deviates from this, it indicates the presence of impurities. Notably, if the impurity is volatile, the boiling point of the mixture may be lower than that of the pure substance; if the impurity is non-volatile, the boiling point will be higher.

---

## 12.5 Identification of Ions and Gases

### 12.5.1 The Chemical Logic of Qualitative Analysis

The purpose of qualitative analysis is to determine **which** ions or molecules are present in a sample, not their quantities. Its methodological foundation is **characteristic chemical reactions** — each ion (or gas), when reacted with a specific reagent, produces a product with **unique observable features**. These features can be: **the colour of a precipitate**, **the release of a gas**, **a colour change**, or **a flame colour**.

A well-designed qualitative analysis scheme typically employs **logical elimination** — first using group reagents to classify ions into broad categories, then within each category using specific reagents to confirm each ion individually. This systematic analytical strategy originates from the classical qualitative analysis scheme developed by Fresenius.

### 12.5.2 Chemical Principles of Anion Identification

#### Carbonate ($\text{CO}_3^{2-}$)

**Principle**: Carbonates react with strong acids (such as $\text{HCl}$) to produce carbon dioxide gas. The essence of this reaction is **protonation** — $\text{H}^+$ from the acid combines with oxygen atoms in the carbonate ion, disrupting the stable structure of $\text{CO}_3^{2-}$:

$$\text{CO}_3^{2-}(aq) + 2\text{H}^+(aq) \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{CO}_3(aq) \rightarrow \text{H}_2\text{O}(l) + \text{CO}_2(g)$$

Carbonic acid ($\text{H}_2\text{CO}_3$) is extremely unstable and rapidly decomposes into water and carbon dioxide at room temperature. The $\text{CO}_2$ gas produced is passed through **limewater** (a saturated aqueous solution of $\text{Ca(OH)}_2$) :

$$\text{CO}_2(g) + \text{Ca(OH)}_2(aq) \rightarrow \text{CaCO}_3(s) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(l)$$

The $\text{CaCO}_3$ formed is a white insoluble precipitate, causing the limewater to **turn cloudy**. If excess $\text{CO}_2$ is passed through, the cloudiness clears again:

$$\text{CaCO}_3(s) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(l) + \text{CO}_2(g) \rightarrow \text{Ca}^{2+}(aq) + 2\text{HCO}_3^-(aq)$$

This is because calcium carbonate is converted into soluble **calcium hydrogencarbonate**. This reversible process demonstrates the principle that **"acid salts are generally more soluble than normal salts"** .

#### Halide Ions ($\text{Cl}^-$, $\text{Br}^-$, $\text{I}^-$)

**Principle**: Silver ions ($\text{Ag}^+$) combine with halide ions to form **insoluble silver halide precipitates**:

$$\text{Ag}^+(aq) + \text{X}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{AgX}(s)$$

Where $\text{X} = \text{Cl}, \text{Br}, \text{I}$.

The three silver halides differ in **colour and solubility**, enabling differentiation:

- $\text{AgCl}$: **White** precipitate, soluble in dilute ammonia (forming $[\text{Ag(NH}_3)_2]^+$ complex ions)
- $\text{AgBr}$: **Cream** precipitate, partially soluble in concentrated ammonia
- $\text{AgI}$: **Yellow** precipitate, insoluble in ammonia

**Why acidify with dilute $\text{HNO}_3$?**
The purpose of adding dilute nitric acid is to **eliminate interfering ions**. Many non-halide anions (such as $\text{CO}_3^{2-}$, $\text{SO}_3^{2-}$, $\text{PO}_4^{3-}$) also react with $\text{Ag}^+$ to form precipitates (e.g., $\text{Ag}_2\text{CO}_3$ is a white precipitate), but these precipitates decompose under acidic conditions ($\text{Ag}_2\text{CO}_3 + 2\text{H}^+ \rightarrow 2\text{Ag}^+ + \text{H}_2\text{O} + \text{CO}_2$), thereby eliminating interference. $\text{AgCl}$, $\text{AgBr}$, and $\text{AgI}$ do not dissolve in dilute nitric acid.

#### Nitrate ($\text{NO}_3^-$)

**Principle**: In strongly alkaline conditions, nitrate is **reduced** to ammonia gas by an active metal (aluminium):

$$\text{3NO}_3^-(aq) + 8\text{Al}(s) + 5\text{OH}^-(aq) + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightarrow 3\text{NH}_3(g) + 8\text{AlO}_2^-(aq)$$

This reaction utilises the **strong reducing power of aluminium** — aluminium dissolves in alkaline solution to form $[\text{Al(OH)}_4]^-$, simultaneously releasing electrons. These electrons reduce $\text{N(V)}$ in $\text{NO}_3^-$ to $\text{N(-III)}$ (i.e., $\text{NH}_3$). The ammonia gas produced is tested with damp red litmus paper, which turns blue (alkaline).

#### Sulfate ($\text{SO}_4^{2-}$)

**Principle**: Barium ions ($\text{Ba}^{2+}$) combine with sulfate ions to form an **insoluble barium sulfate precipitate**:

$$\text{Ba}^{2+}(aq) + \text{SO}_4^{2-}(aq) \rightarrow \text{BaSO}_4(s)$$

$\text{BaSO}_4$ is an extremely insoluble white precipitate ($K_{sp} = 1.1 \times 10^{-10}$), completely insoluble even under acidic conditions. This is its key difference from $\text{BaCO}_3$ or $\text{BaSO}_3$ — while the latter two are also white precipitates, they dissolve in dilute nitric acid.

The two purposes of acidifying with dilute $\text{HNO}_3$:
1. To eliminate interference from $\text{CO}_3^{2-}$, $\text{SO}_3^{2-}$, $\text{PO}_4^{3-}$ and other ions (their precipitates with $\text{Ba}^{2+}$ are acid-soluble)
2. To provide an acidic environment that prevents the formation of $\text{BaCO}_3$ precipitate

#### Sulfite ($\text{SO}_3^{2-}$)

**Principle**: Sulfite ions have **reducing properties** and can reduce manganate(VII) ions ($\text{MnO}_4^-$) to $\text{Mn}^{2+}$:

$$\text{5SO}_3^{2-}(aq) + 2\text{MnO}_4^-(aq) + 6\text{H}^+(aq) \rightarrow 5\text{SO}_4^{2-}(aq) + 2\text{Mn}^{2+}(aq) + 3\text{H}_2\text{O}(l)$$

$\text{MnO}_4^-$ is deep purple in colour, while $\text{Mn}^{2+}$ is nearly colourless. Therefore, the observed phenomenon is **purple potassium manganate(VII) solution turning colourless**. The essence of this reaction is that **$\text{S(IV)}$ is oxidised to $\text{S(VI)}$** while **$\text{Mn(VII)}$ is reduced to $\text{Mn(II)}$** — a classic **redox reaction**.

### 12.5.3 Chemical Principles of Cation Identification

#### Sodium Hydroxide and Ammonia Precipitation Methods

The core principle of using $\text{NaOH}$ or $\text{NH}_3$ solutions to identify cations is: most transition metal cations and certain main group cations react with $\text{OH}^-$ to form **insoluble hydroxide precipitates**, which have **characteristic colours**. Some amphoteric hydroxides can re-dissolve in excess $\text{OH}^-$.

**Characteristic Reactions of Each Cation**:

**$\text{Al}^{3+}$ (Aluminium ions)** :
Reacts with a small amount of $\text{NaOH}$ to form a **white gelatinous** $\text{Al(OH)}_3$ precipitate:

$$\text{Al}^{3+}(aq) + 3\text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{Al(OH)}_3(s)$$

$\text{Al(OH)}_3$ is a classic **amphoteric hydroxide**. It dissolves in excess $\text{NaOH}$ to form tetrahydroxoaluminate ions:

$$\text{Al(OH)}_3(s) + \text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow [\text{Al(OH)}_4]^-(aq) \quad (\text{colourless solution})$$

This property arises from the high charge-to-radius ratio of $\text{Al}^{3+}$, enabling it to act either as an acid (donating $\text{H}^+$) or as a base (accepting $\text{H}^+$).

Reaction with ammonia: $\text{Al(OH)}_3$ does **not** dissolve in excess ammonia — this property can be used to distinguish it from $\text{Zn}^{2+}$.

**$\text{NH}_4^+$ (Ammonium ions)** :
Reacts with $\text{OH}^-$ to release ammonia gas:

$$\text{NH}_4^+(aq) + \text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{NH}_3(g) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(l)$$

This is a reaction where a **weak base is displaced by a strong base**. Ammonia gas has a characteristic pungent odour and turns damp red litmus paper blue. Heating accelerates the reaction because the solubility of ammonia in water decreases with increasing temperature.

**$\text{Ca}^{2+}$ (Calcium ions)** :
Reacts with $\text{OH}^-$ to form a white $\text{Ca(OH)}_2$ precipitate. $\text{Ca(OH)}_2$ is classified as "slightly soluble" ($0.16\,\text{g}/100\,\text{g}\,\text{H}_2\text{O}$ at $20^\circ\text{C}$). Therefore, the precipitate is typically less dense than $\text{Mg(OH)}_2$ and may require higher concentrations to be clearly observable.

**$\text{Cr}^{3+}$ (Chromium(III) ions)** :
Reacts with $\text{OH}^-$ to form a **grey-green** $\text{Cr(OH)}_3$ precipitate. $\text{Cr(OH)}_3$ is also amphoteric, dissolving in excess $\text{NaOH}$ to form a green solution of $[\text{Cr(OH)}_4]^-$ (or $\text{CrO}_2^-$):

$$\text{Cr(OH)}_3(s) + \text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow [\text{Cr(OH)}_4]^-(aq)$$

In excess ammonia, $\text{Cr(OH)}_3$ dissolves slightly to form a purple solution (complex formation), though this is not a focus of examinations.

**$\text{Cu}^{2+}$ (Copper(II) ions)** :
Reacts with $\text{OH}^-$ to form a **blue** $\text{Cu(OH)}_2$ precipitate:

$$\text{Cu}^{2+}(aq) + 2\text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{Cu(OH)}_2(s)$$

$\text{Cu(OH)}_2$ is a blue gelatinous precipitate. Upon heating, it decomposes to black $\text{CuO}$:

$$\text{Cu(OH)}_2(s) \xrightarrow{\Delta} \text{CuO}(s) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(g)$$

Reaction with excess ammonia: $\text{Cu(OH)}_2$ dissolves to form the deep blue $[\text{Cu(NH}_3)_4]^{2+}$ complex:

$$\text{Cu(OH)}_2(s) + 4\text{NH}_3(aq) \rightarrow [\text{Cu(NH}_3)_4]^{2+}(aq) + 2\text{OH}^-(aq)$$

The deep blue colour of this complex is a characteristic identifier, originating from **d-d electronic transitions** caused by **ligand field splitting**.

**$\text{Fe}^{2+}$ (Iron(II) ions)** :
Reacts with $\text{OH}^-$ to form a **dull green** $\text{Fe(OH)}_2$ precipitate:

$$\text{Fe}^{2+}(aq) + 2\text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{Fe(OH)}_2(s)$$

$\text{Fe(OH)}_2$ is unstable in air and is rapidly oxidised by oxygen to reddish-brown $\text{Fe(OH)}_3$:

$$4\text{Fe(OH)}_2(s) + \text{O}_2(g) + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightarrow 4\text{Fe(OH)}_3(s)$$

This oxidation process demonstrates the **reducing nature of $\text{Fe}^{2+}$** — $\text{Fe(II)}$ spontaneously oxidises to $\text{Fe(III)}$ in the presence of air.

**$\text{Fe}^{3+}$ (Iron(III) ions)** :
Reacts with $\text{OH}^-$ to form a **reddish-brown** $\text{Fe(OH)}_3$ precipitate:

$$\text{Fe}^{3+}(aq) + 3\text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow \text{Fe(OH)}_3(s)$$

$\text{Fe(OH)}_3$ does **not** react with excess $\text{NaOH}$ or $\text{NH}_3$ (it is not amphoteric), distinguishing it from other trivalent cations. Reddish-brown is a strongly characteristic colour of $\text{Fe(III)}$.

**$\text{Zn}^{2+}$ (Zinc ions)** :
Reacts with $\text{OH}^-$ to form a **white** $\text{Zn(OH)}_2$ precipitate. $\text{Zn(OH)}_2$ is an amphoteric hydroxide, dissolving in excess $\text{NaOH}$:

$$\text{Zn(OH)}_2(s) + 2\text{OH}^-(aq) \rightarrow [\text{Zn(OH)}_4]^{2-}(aq)$$

It also dissolves in excess ammonia, forming $[\text{Zn(NH}_3)_4]^{2+}$:

$$\text{Zn(OH)}_2(s) + 4\text{NH}_3(aq) \rightarrow [\text{Zn(NH}_3)_4]^{2+}(aq) + 2\text{OH}^-(aq)$$

Both $\text{Zn}^{2+}$ and $\text{Al}^{3+}$ dissolve in excess $\text{NaOH}$, but $\text{Zn}^{2+}$ dissolves in excess ammonia while $\text{Al}^{3+}$ does not — this is the key distinction between them.

### 12.5.4 Atomic Physics Principles of Flame Tests

The principle of the **Flame Test** involves the most fundamental form of **atomic emission spectroscopy**.

When a metal ion or its compound is introduced into a flame, energy is transferred from the flame to the metal atoms, causing**outer-shell electrons** to **transition from the ground state to an excited state**:

$$\text{M}(g) + \text{Energy} \rightarrow \text{M}^*(g)$$

Excited state atoms have an extremely short lifetime (approximately $10^{-8}\,\text{s}$). The electrons return to the ground state via **spontaneous emission**, releasing the excess energy:

$$\text{M}^*(g) \rightarrow \text{M}(g) + h\nu$$

According to **Planck's relation**, the relationship between the energy of the emitted photon and its frequency is:

$$E = h\nu = \frac{hc}{\lambda}$$

Where:
- $E$ = photon energy (J)
- $h$ = Planck's constant ($6.626 \times 10^{-34}\,\text{J}\cdot\text{s}$)
- $\nu$ = light frequency (Hz)
- $c$ = speed of light ($3.00 \times 10^8\,\text{m/s}$)
- $\lambda$ = light wavelength (m)

Each metallic element has a **unique electronic energy level structure** (determined by its nuclear charge and electron configuration), so the photons emitted during transitions have **characteristic wavelengths**, manifested as **characteristic flame colours**:

| Metal Ion | Flame Colour | Wavelength $\lambda$ (nm) | Corresponding Transition |
|-----------|-------------|--------------------------|------------------------|
| $\text{Li}^+$ | **Crimson** | ~670 | $2p \rightarrow 2s$ |
| $\text{Na}^+$ | **Yellow** | ~589 (doublet) | $3p \rightarrow 3s$ |
| $\text{K}^+$ | **Lilac** | ~768, 404 | $4p \rightarrow 4s$ |
| $\text{Ca}^{2+}$ | **Brick red** | ~622, 553 | $4p \rightarrow 4s$ |
| $\text{Ba}^{2+}$ | **Apple green** | ~524, 514 | $6p \rightarrow 5d$ |
| $\text{Cu}^{2+}$ | **Blue-green** | ~515, 450 | Multiple electron transitions |

**Why is the yellow colour of sodium the brightest?**
The two spectral lines (589.0 nm and 589.6 nm, known as the **sodium D-lines**) corresponding to the $3p \rightarrow 3s$ transition in sodium happen to fall within the region of highest human visual sensitivity. Moreover, sodium atoms have a relatively low ionisation energy and are easily excited. Therefore, even trace amounts of sodium impurity in a sample can **overwhelm the colours of other elements**. This is why **cobalt glass** must be used when observing potassium's lilac colour — cobalt glass absorbs yellow light while allowing purple light to pass through.

### 12.5.5 Chemical Principles of Gas Identification

#### Ammonia ($\text{NH}_3$)

Ammonia is an **alkaline gas**. When exposed to **damp red litmus paper**, the ammonia dissolves in the moisture on the paper to form $\text{OH}^-$ ions:

$$\text{NH}_3(g) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightleftharpoons \text{NH}_4^+(aq) + \text{OH}^-(aq)$$

The $\text{OH}^-$ ions change the litmus paper from red to blue. The equilibrium constant for this reaction is $K_b = 1.8 \times 10^{-5}$ ($25^\circ\text{C}$), indicating that ammonia solution is a **weak base**, but still sufficient to change the indicator's colour. The paper must be kept **damp** because dry $\text{NH}_3$ molecules cannot produce $\text{OH}^-$ ions nor effectively interact with the litmus molecules.

#### Carbon Dioxide ($\text{CO}_2$)

The reaction when carbon dioxide is passed through limewater ($\text{Ca(OH)}_2$ saturated solution) has been discussed in detail in the anion identification section. The core principle here is the **reversibility of precipitate formation and dissolution** — this is unique to $\text{CO}_2$; the test for oxygen produces no precipitate, and the test for hydrogen does not cause limewater to turn cloudy and then clear again.

#### Chlorine ($\text{Cl}_2$)

Chlorine has **oxidising** and **acidic** properties. When damp litmus paper is exposed to chlorine gas:
1. Chlorine dissolves in water to form hydrochloric acid and hypochlorous acid:

$$\text{Cl}_2(g) + \text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightleftharpoons \text{HCl}(aq) + \text{HOCl}(aq)$$

2. The acidity of $\text{HCl}$ causes the litmus paper to **turn red** first
3. $\text{HOCl}$ (hypochlorous acid) is a strong oxidising agent that **bleaches** the litmus pigment, turning the paper white

This "red first, then white" sequential phenomenon is the **characteristic reaction of chlorine**. It is also the key distinction between chlorine and other acidic gases (such as $\text{SO}_2$ — $\text{SO}_2$ can also turn litmus red but does not fully bleach it).

#### Hydrogen ($\text{H}_2$)

Hydrogen is **combustible**. When a **lighted splint** is inserted into hydrogen (or brought near a source of hydrogen), the hydrogen reacts with oxygen in the air:

$$2\text{H}_2(g) + \text{O}_2(g) \rightarrow 2\text{H}_2\text{O}(g)$$

The reaction rate is extremely fast, releasing a large amount of heat. The gas volume expands sharply, producing a **sharp squeaky pop sound**. The essence of this sound is a **chemical reaction wave** — the flame front propagates rapidly through the mixed gases, causing a sudden local pressure increase that generates sound waves.

#### Oxygen ($\text{O}_2$)

Oxygen supports **combustion**. When a **glowing splint** is inserted into oxygen, the oxygen acts as a combustion supporter, accelerating the burning of the splint:

$$\text{C}(s) + \text{O}_2(g) \rightarrow \text{CO}_2(g)$$

The oxygen concentration in pure oxygen (100%) is far higher than that in air (approximately 21%). Therefore, the rate of combustion increases dramatically, manifested as the splint **relighting** (re-igniting with a flame). Using a glowing rather than a flaming splint ensures that the splint does not burn out prematurely, making the phenomenon more distinct.

This reaction illustrates the **effect of concentration on reaction rate** — according to collision theory, when the oxygen concentration increases five-fold, the effective collision frequency between $\text{C}$ and $\text{O}_2$ also increases correspondingly, significantly enhancing the reaction rate.

#### Sulfur Dioxide ($\text{SO}_2$)

Sulfur dioxide possesses **reducing properties** (similar to sulfite ions). When passed through **acidified potassium manganate(VII) solution**:

$$\text{5SO}_2(g) + 2\text{MnO}_4^-(aq) + 2\text{H}_2\text{O}(l) \rightarrow 5\text{SO}_4^{2-}(aq) + 2\text{Mn}^{2+}(aq) + 4\text{H}^+(aq)$$

$\text{MnO}_4^-$ (purple) is reduced by $\text{SO}_2$ to nearly colourless $\text{Mn}^{2+}$, so the solution **changes from purple to colourless**. This reaction is identical to the identification of sulfite ions, reflecting the reducing nature of $\text{SO}_2$.

---

### 12.5.6 The Systematic Logic of Qualitative Analysis

Qualitative analysis is not a collection of isolated tests, but an entire system of **logical judgement**. When designing an identification scheme, the following principles must be followed:

**The Principle of Interference Elimination**: A single reagent may react with multiple ions to produce similar phenomena. Therefore, the range of possibilities must be gradually narrowed through **sequential elimination**. For example, in anion identification, first test for $\text{CO}_3^{2-}$ with dilute acid (produces gas), then test for $\text{SO}_4^{2-}$ with $\text{Ba}^{2+}$ under acidic conditions (produces acid-insoluble precipitate), and finally test for halide ions with $\text{Ag}^+$.

**The Principle of Control Experiments**: Any observed phenomenon should be verified through **blank controls** and **known reference standards** to eliminate the possibility of false positives or false negatives caused by reagent contamination or operational errors.

**The Principle of Concentration Sensitivity**: Certain reactions require sufficiently high ion concentrations to produce observable phenomena. For example, the precipitation of $\text{Ca}^{2+}$ with $\text{OH}^-$ may not be observable in dilute solutions due to the slight solubility of $\text{Ca(OH)}_2$. In such cases, confirmation should be sought by **concentrating** the sample.

**The Trade-off between Specificity and Sensitivity**: An ideal identification reaction should possess both **high specificity** (only the target ion produces the phenomenon) and **high sensitivity** (detectable even in trace amounts). In practice, multiple mutually corroborating reactions are often required.

---

> **Concluding Remarks**: Topic 12 (Experimental Techniques and Chemical Analysis) is the most practically oriented section of the entire IGCSE Chemistry syllabus and the one most closely connected to everyday laboratory chemistry. From the most fundamental principles of measurement, through precise titration analysis, to systematic ion identification schemes, every technique is underpinned by profound physicochemical principles — **partition equilibrium** (the essence of $R_f$ values), **precipitation-dissolution equilibrium** (the basis of ion identification), **acid-base proton transfer** (the soul of titration and indicators), and **atomic energy level transitions** (the physical origin of flame tests). Understanding the internal logic of these principles is far more important than rote memorisation of operational procedures — it equips you, when faced with any unknown sample, to design a reliable identification scheme starting from first principles.
